THE RED PAGE
THE NOVELS OF SNAITH
\T HAT of the future of the English
j y novel ? Meredith and Hardy, the
masters, are silent ; Conrad and
Henry Jan 3s and George Moore
have lost themselves in the arid deserts of
futile subtlety ; but what of the younger
generations at their heels ? To whom may
we with confidence look- for the handing on
of the torch ? '
Of the eight or nine names that occur
to the mind, there are four whose work
enables us to look somewhat hopefully at
the future—Maurice Hewlett, Wm. J. Locke,
author of The florals of Marcus Ordojne and
The Beloved Vagabond, May Sinclair, who
produced that remarkable novel of pro-
mise, The Divine Fire, and J. C. Snaitli,
author of Broke of Covenden and Henry
Northcote (Constable, per Sydney Book Club).
Hewlett has secured for himself a unique
niche in English literature ; the claims of
Sydney Royce Lysaght, author of Her
Majesty's Rebels, of M. P. Willcocks, author
of The Wingless Victory, and of John Gals-
worthy, author of The Man of Property and
The Country House, will be investigated
later; and of the others the works of
Snaith require from us the greatest atten-
tion. For Snaith may be regarded as the
legitimate heir of George Meredith. This
is not to say that he is a mere filcher of the
master’s style, nor yet that he is Meredith’s
equal. But there are in Broke of Covenden
as genuine comedy, as careful a painting of
character, as artistic a reticence, and as in-
timate an acquaintance with what may be
regarded as the Meredithian archetype—the
unadulterated insular English landed proprie-
tor—described by Snaith in the formula :
sense of humor, nil: pride, the algebraic
figure x —as in the novels of our living classic.
Snaith lacks Meredith’s psychological insight
into woman, his uncanny knowledge of the
secret springs of our actions, and his ability
to track egotism to its most hidden lair—he
lacks, too, Meredith’s craggy style; but
in his appreciation of the true tragi-comedy,
Snaith reaches close to his master. Broke
of Covenden is a study of the English
gentleman with a feudal soul, strangely sur-
viving in a hostile, modern world. Beaten
upon by all the agencies of this swiftly
changing world, the man, unmalleable, un-
teachable, becomes a mere dull log, in his
stubborn isolation almost impervious to mis-
fortune.
J*
Broke had six daughters and one son,
and all were decorated with the aristo-
cratic Broke nose. To the girls this was
really their life tragedy—“ their looks had
all foregathered in their ridiculous noses.”
Upon the face of Delia, the youngest, it
looked “a little unexpected.”
Hers was a pathetically plain little face,
which . . . had an air of candor, of unfearing
innocence that was appealing. But there was a
memorable nose stuck in the middle. It fasci-
nated the young man ; never had he seen an organ
so remarkable on the face of a woman. It might
have made him laugh if somehow, out of propor-
tion as it was, it did not seem, after all, in an
absurd, whimsical manner, to be appropriate to
her quaintness, and to hold, quite rightly and
properly, its niche in nature. . . . Insensibly it
lured his picturesque fancy to revert to the
crusaders and the days of chivalry, so that when
he looked dt it again, the glamor of a poetic age
was shining on it. i
Ihe future of the Broke family depends
upon a moneyed marriage which Mrs. Broke,
the suave, managing mother, is arranging
for her son Billy. But Billy, having
his share of the Broke masterfulness, breaks
his chains. The scene where Billy explains
to his mother what he has done is delightful
comedy.
• r < *° y?U mean, my darling, by that
1 luiculous assertion that you were married yes-
terday morning ?”
Ridiculous ! ’ said Billy. “ Why ridiculous,
mummy?
“ Then it is true, my dear one ? ”
tl Of course. I wouldn’t rag you, you know.”
And who, pray, is the favored person ? ”
1 here-was ii naif-smile still lurking in her cool
blue eyes. There was the same suggestion of a
shgntly amused suavity with which she had begun
tne conversation.
‘‘She’s a peach,” said Billy.
“ Who, my love?”
“ A. peach ! ”
I don t think I.know the family. I believe
there are some people called Mowbray Peach in
Warwickshire. ...”
“I don’t mean people at all, you dear old silly.
A peach—a regular picture, y’ know. Something
sweet and tempting and good to look at.”
And who is this person, my love ? Who is
sweet and tempting and good to look at ? ” -
ilywife, said Billy, taking his hat off one
knee and placing it on the other.
Of course, my love. But who was she before
she w<is your wife?”
Our young gentleman paused. He carefully
‘n C - C IIS upon his other knee. .. .
P t>, • ’ lf y°u must know, she is a little girl out
of Perkin and Warheek’s shop in Bond-street, the
right-hand side; you know, going out of Picca-
“ Thank you, my darling, that is all I wished
to know .. I suppose, my angel,” she said in a
iow, matter-of-fact voice, “you know that you
have mined us all ? ”
J*
In Broke, Snaith has painted a great
gallery of portraits, some of them a little
fivntastic—rather caricatures than likenesses,
lhe novelist’s worst fault, just as evident in
his second novel, is his tendency to make his
characters talk in too literary and artificial
a style, while occasionally he allows his
sense of humor to lead him unwittingly into
the grotesquely ridiculous. Occasionally he
demands too much from his reader’s o-ood-
nature. Properly belonging to the regions
,°P era *‘ s le scene where the Duke
of Wimbledon, given carte blanche to select
Ta l6 n tlio Broko § ir^s ( w dh nose) as his
-lineboss, pathetically anxious “to do the
ught thing,’’conceives it his duty to select the
winning name from a hat. The last scenes,
too, as the remorseless tragedy is logically
-worked out, are keyed toohigh. Snaith aspires
to levels where, as yet, his ability cannot
sustain him. But the book is strikingly
original, ably constructed, and carried tofts
-y'-th a sublime disdain of the suscepti-
bilities of the average novel-reader.
Jt
M e turnto Henry Northcote, expecting to
find the Meredithian manner more empha-
sised—and there is scarcely a trace of the
hand that penned Broke. Like W. J. Locke,
Snaith has here been caught by the glamor of
the fantastic. There is however no indication
ot a failing of the stream of his originality ;
he has conceived and carried successfully
out one of the most remarkable scenes in
literature. The story may be briefly indi-
cated. To Henry Northcote, advocate,
starving in his chambers in Shepherd’s Inn
comes a prosperous solicitor with a brief for
the defence of a woman in a murder trial. But
the advent of the solicitor has been heralded
by a more mysterious visitor—the identity
or even existence of whom will set the reader
away on the wings of speculation. The only
possible defence of the woman—a common
woman of the streets who has poisoned a
man with whom she was living—is insanity •
but “Cad” Northcote, as he was called at
school has such a preposterous belief in
himself that he proposes to sweep aside by
his undoubted gift of eloquence the damning
circumstantial evidence that must bring her
neck to the noose. Snaith’s craftsmanship is
herein cunningiy displayed by his reticence
about the character of the woman whose life
is to hang—or not to hang—upon the slender
thread of this egotistic and loquacious advo-
cate. We -have not a hint of her real
character till past the middle of the
book Then follows the great scene of
tiie trial. Northcote contents himself with
cross-examining one witness; but the effect
of her admissions, which are put into her
mouth by Northcote, are electrical. Then
he proceeds to address the jury Most
novelists cautiously content themselves by
telling us that their heroes are brilliant
talkers, or brilliant writers ; but, if they are
wise, they do not attempt to give us actual
transcripts of the speeches or the writings of
their puppets. Else the illusion would belost.
Even Meiedith fails when, instead of merely
insisting that Diana was a brilliant wit, he
retails to us some of the specimens of her
mt—and. the reader, prepared to take
Meiedith s assertion on trust, confronted
with the evidence gratuitously submitted
confesses that their brilliance is not what
Meredith had cunningly led him to suspect.
But Snaith Ims the daring not only to assert
that JNprthcote was a brilliant pleader, but
to place before us almost the whole of North-
cote’s speech, and allow us to judge for our-
selves.
And the speech, reported for us almost
verbatim, is as remarkable as Snaith
had insisted it was, though whether it would
have oeen possible in a court of law is a
matter for legal opinion. He begins by re-
counting a vision lie had, in which he saw the
phice of the jurors taken by another jury,
wlfose faces he recognises.
“ The first face I had recognised was that of an
old man, urbane and ironical, a citizen of the
world ; it was the face of Plato. . . . Thinkers,
warriors, saints and innovators began to teem
before my gaze. ... I thought I discerned the
faces of at least two women among the assembly ;
one was that of Joan of Arc, the other that of
Mary the Magdalene.”
And the foreman ?
“ It was with an indescribable curiosity that I
observed a slight, strangely-garbed figure emerge
from their midst. And when he came to assume
his place at the head of his immortal companions,
which you, sir, are occupying now, I was devoured
by an overpowering eagerness to look upon his
face.... And when at last he lifted his head and
the emanation of his pale and luminous features
shone out of the gloom and overspread this
assembly, a kind of half-stifled sob of surprise, a
sort of shudder of recognition passed over the'
crowded court. The. face was that of the mart
called Jesus of Nazareth. ...”
So, having elected his own remarkable j ury,
the advocate proceeds to bring it to his point
of view—the supreme danger of keeping
“ a tree standing in England upon which we
nail women. ’ But his climax is still mox-e
cruel. After detailing a case of a miscarriage
of justice involving the hanging of a man
only twelve months before, Nortlicote
points out that “ the present counsel for the
Crown is the same counsel who prosecuted
that man, and the judge who sent that man
to his doom is seated above you in all the
panoply of - his office. I leave him now (he
concludes) to deal if he is able in a like
manner with this poor Magdalene, who may
or may not have fallen by the way.”
J*
It need not be said what the verdict is.
Every reader will concur iu it. But this
is not the climax ; it is the real beginning of
the book ; for suddenly the woman, kept so
long in the background, emerges .upon the
scene, and the book culminates in a sternly
logical series of unexpected incidents that
are as remorselessly depicted, and with as
little regal’d/for the gentle reader’s feelings,
as the slow tragedy of Broke of Govenden .
With two such books, each in the class FI,
already to his credit, Snaith should go far.
Professor Gregory, entrusted With the prepara-
tion of the second edition of Dr, A. Russell
Wallace’s geography of Australasia (E. Stanford,
London, 155.), found it necessary, owing to the
rapid growth of geographical knowledge of the
subject, to rewrite the volume ; and a work of over
(100 pages, interspersed with illustrations and
excellent maps, is the result. Gregory has, for
the general reader, an appreciative introduction
dealing with such subjects as the isolation of
Australia, defence, the White Australia policy,
the ideals of the Labor Party, and the hopefulness
of the Australian. He points out that the
“remarkable unity of life, of interests, and of
manners is a great source of- strength to Aus-
tralia, and a security for its future welfare ; for
the stronger, abler section of society are not
handicapped by such a burden of the weak, the
ignorant, and the discontented as in the caste-
ridden countries of Europe. The character of the
Australasian people is the most gratifying feature
in the political development of this quarter of the
globe.” He rightly emphasises “the drift of
failures and ‘ wasters,’ shipped out to the Anti-
podes by shamed British relatives,” as a more
serious danger to the. Australian population than
the earlier leaven of convicts. “But even these
unpromising immigrants are absorbed without
much permanent injury to the race.” Gregory
puts down to the homogenity of the population
“ the exceptional high standard of honesty,”
which, in its turn, “is one of the factors in the
high efficiency of Australian work.” As to de-
fence, he points out that a hostile battleship
could do but comparatively- little damage to a
scattered town like Melbourne, which is spread
over 254 square miles, while the number of shots
a battleship could afford to fire at that city would
be comparatively small. “So if Melbourne de-
clined to be bluffed, and remembered that
the battleship could do less damage than a
hurricane, and that its visit would be much less
costly than a drought, the raider could, do nothing
to justify his risks.” Upon the White Australia
policy he remarks that the Anglo-Indian is as
determined as the Australian that his son shall
inherit the traditions of his race, and removes him
from contact with an Oriental race, for much the
same reasons that the Australian refuses to domi-
cile an Oriental race around his home. Though
in several details the geographical portion of the
work is not accurate, the book is a useful and
timely contribution to the knowledge of ourselves.
Compilations such as, Phrases and Navies:
their Origins and Meanings, by Trench H. John-
son (T. W. Laurie, 65.), are frequently useful,
but necessarily never complete. The present
volume devotes much attention to the explanation
of the origin of London place-names, and of
American phrases. The compiler has apparently
never heard of alone hand, nor has he anything to
say about a bosker.
AT DAWN.
The night-lone/ clamor of winds grew still,
The forest rested—its foes withdrawn:
On sounding ocean and silent hill
There crept a sense of the coming dawn.
A bird awoke on a- leaning limb
And fluttered its plumes a moment’s space ;
Dark purple lay on the sea’s far rim ;
The sky grew pale as a dying face.
Then all the trees and the rocks and heights
With wondering faces watched the East:
It seemed an altar hung with lights
And waiting for a vestured priest.
And in that intimate first hour
When land and sea rejoiced as one,
And Nature , like an opening flower ,
Gave incense, came the burning sun.
Yet, while the hour of gold went by,
I saw through all its pageantry
The vast indifference- of the sky,
The heartless beauty of the sea.
For wet and wan, and cold and sped
Beyond the breakers’ reach of pearl,
There lay a strong man drowned and dead,
And in his arms a drowned white girl.
N.S.WY
RODERIC QUINN.
IS LITER.I TUUK DEAD?
Herbert Paul is much concerned, in the Con-
temporary, over the death of literature. It is
being glam by science. “The scientific future isfull
of exciting possibilities, for which mere literature
can offer no equivalent.” Paul proceeds to hurl
at moribund literature heavy and inconvenient
bricks like these : —-“Literature maybe an elegant
amusement, but, after all, it is only permutations
and combinations of words. Have we not had
enough of it ? What is the need of it, except to
make the conclusions of science intelligible to the
masses ? ' Is it possible to carry 'tliefiart of expres-
sion further than Plato carried it more than two
thousand years ago ? There is no progress in
literature. There is nothing else in science, for
there is no limit to discovery.” Having-thus
battered the poor thing into a comatose condition,
Paul proceeds to argue with her. His contention
is that though there will always be books to be
written, the highest intellects will not continue
to be attracted to literature as in the past.
“ Will the highest intellects concern themselves
with insoluble problems, with windows that ex-
clude the light, and passages that lead to nothing ?
Or will they be drawn, are they being drawn
even now, into the more fruitful methods of
experiment add exactitude? ... Is science ac-
quiring an absolute dominion over the minds of
men?... That is the supreme attraction of
science. Its possibilities are infinite. In litera-
ture, in metaphysics, the best that can be has
been done. . . . The highest minds have the
loftiest aspirations,, which poetry and other forms
of literature have satisfied hitherto. If science
can be proved to hold the key of the universe,
complete ’ satisfaction cannot be sought else-
where.” And literature did not say a word. She
was busy writing a little poem that would live a
thousand years after Herbert Paul had proved to
her that she was extremely dead.
The Problem Solved, the author, J. Watson,
Carlton, te'ls usis “the outcome of years of study,
hours of sacrifice, and the burning of the midnight
oil, whilst some of my fellow citizens were enjoy-
ing themselves, sleeping the sleep of peace, or
torn asunder upon the rack of poverty.” Frankly
we admit we haven’t read it. Still it contains
odes to the Creator, and one to a certain politi-
cian, beginning breezily like this :—
“ Hail, Joseph, Premier of the premier State,
Thine hour-glass empties, leave it not too late ;
Hell’s mouth still opens for its daily prey,
In error, sir, you may just stray its way.”
There is also a stimulating poem entitled, “The
Christian’s Farewell to Unrestricted Individual-
ism.” Most of the-book, however, is just prose.
Poetry to order. Australia To-day (190 Flin-
ders-street, Melb.) is offering a £25 prize for the
best poem, and another £25 for the best prose
essay, upon the subject of “Australia’s Destiny
in the Empire.” The poem must not exceed one
thousand words —not lines —and the essay seven
thousand words. Poets and essayists should
write for details to the address above given ; but
as the competition closes on August 1st, they
should set- a match to their inspiration without
loss of time.
A somewhat conventional novel is Constance
Smedley’s Conflict (Constable) noteworthy only
for the careful studies of modern women. The
heroine is a girl clerk who is left with the
management of a big “tube ” works, and the story
now she controls the immense business is interest-
ing ; but the love interest is built upon the model
that enables the expert novel-reader to pick the
winner as soon as the weights are declared. Con-
stance Smedley, however, has succeeded in de-
picting with insight certain types of the un-
satisfied advanced woman of ’ the day. Her
solution of their troubles is one which has worked
very well in the past—marriage and motherhood.
—F3.
Out of the West comes tw periodical called
Australia Junior, a healthy, patriotic “ bar-
raeker” for a healthy and patriotic Australian
rabe. It is published at Perth, price nothing, and
edited by J. J. Simons, whose youth does not yet
entitle him to a vote. The magazine is addressed
to Australian schoolboys, its motto is “ a white,
self-contained, self-reliant nation,” and the editor
is ah enthusiastic believer in the Australian game
of football. The publication deserves to be in the
hands of every Australian boy.
“Ambitious” writes asking for “a reliable
literary guide—one that tells you what publica-
tions. English, American and Australasian, pay
for copy.” Such a guide would sell, but there
seems no chance of its ever being written. For, in
print, editors betray a discreet reticence as to their
rate of pay. The Literary Year Book (Routledge,
London) affords particulars of English periodical
publications; but the information is usually con-
fined to addresses and generalities. Herewith some
samples : — Harper's : Authoritative articles deal-
ing with travel, science, art, and literature ; also,
high-class short stories. Remuneration is given.
Helping Words : Articles on religious and family
subjects, 1500 words. Serials, 20,000 words.
Domestic tales, 3000 words. Payment by arrange-
ment. Cash on acceptance. Home Circle:
Domestic stories with happy tone (5000 words).
Remuneration about one guinea a page. Economic
Review: Occasional contributors required ; econo-
mic and social ‘subjects; length, ten to fifteen
pages ; no remuneration. Methodist Recorder :
Articles relating to Methodism, usually illus-
trated, of from 2000 to 2500 words. Short stories
with a Methodist flavor, 1500 to 2000 words, or a
Methodist serial. Remuneration by agreement.
Smart Set: Bright, short articles are considered,
100 to 1000 words. Also, modern short tales, no
mud or blood (500 to 5000, or 30,000 to 4Q.OOO
words). Poems also. Payment is made at from
£2 to £10 a thousand words.
Echoes from Arernus, by Willoughby-Andrew,
Sydney, are rhymes interesting chiefly from the
fact that they were composed during the author’s
fourteen years’ confinement in N.S. Wales pri-
sons, serving a death-commuted sentence. The
book is dedicated to the chaplains who visited
him in prison. There is more yerbal ingenuity

VAIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BLLETIN.]
Ballade of Life.
With clang of bells and calling horn,
Along the world our wheels incline,
The caravans that crimson morn
Drove faring forth in deep design.
The Orient Spring sun. stirs like wine ;
The lark above the mountain wall
God-speeds us with a voice divine —
Where shall we rest at evenfall ?
Across far leagues there is a bourne
Whose banks are rest for thee and thine ;
Where, sorrow-spent and travel-worn.
Our hands the flowers of peace may twine.
Yet o’er us now the midday shine,
As to the distances we crawl;
The white dust lifts along the line—
Where shall we rest at evenfall ?
Birds sing no more in hedge and thorn,
Their morning raptures rich and fine ;
No more the hail of bending corn
’Neath the dawn skies incarnadine.
Behind, the hot air-circles whine,
The vultures droop above us all.
Where is the halting-place benign ?
Where shall we rest at evenfall ?
l’envoi.
Life, as fain pilgrims to a shrine,
And so beset by many a thrall,
Since the deep distance lends no sign,
Where shall we rest at evenfall ?
MAOKILAND.
C. ALLAN MARRIS.
Unforgotten.
What, do you say I forget ?
Nay, you are wrong,
Dear girl, whom I courted and met
A sweet summer long.
The years you have been out of sight
Many memories fill,
But you know from the verses I write
I’m in love with you still.
Child, I remember you well—
Short, were you, or tali ?
Be not angry that I cannot tell
Such a trifle withal.
Were you only a maiden petite,
Or queenly, divine ?
Yet I know how your heart used to beat
When it pressed upon mine.
Still I remember your eyes—
They were blue, or else gray ?
I know not, nor will I surmise,
But I see them, to-day,
Upturned to my own (while your lips
Were all ripe to be kissed),
Ashine through a rainy eclipse,
Like stars in a mist.
Sweet, I remember your hair—
It was gold—perchance brown ?
But I know that my lips wandered there
When your head was bent down.
Though I know not your voice should you speak,
Would you hold me to blame?
I remember your breath on my cheek
As you murmured my name.
See, I remember you quite.
Forgotten ?—ah, no !
For I’m sitting and dreaming to-night
Of that year long ago. s
Face—voice—in oblivion’s grave
Long perished they lie ;
But the glory of love that they gave
Shall never go by.
VIC.
PRIAM.
The Law.
Last night I had a fearful dream, and this is what
I saw : v
A fiendish monster grey, uncouth, that called itself
the Law.
Through rays of truth and innocence I saw it
fiercely grope,
The while it grasped a prison-key, a gallows and
a rope.
It shambled past my office door, with ghostly gait
and grim ;
It searched through Hunger’s lone abode, and
peered in corners dim.
(Adown the street I heard a cry that told of driven
fear,
And yet the Monster heeded not —it scarcely
seemed to hear !)
I saw it sneaking silently where faint the gas-
light shone
On mortals hungry, homeless, and it roughly
moved them on.
It gnashed its teeth and tore its hair, and then,
with hate intense,
It plundered thro’ the realms of Love, and Hope,
and Innocence.
It saw thepeople’s heritage filched by the hand of
Fat,
And heeded not—for it had more important work
than that!
It saw the Pharisee despoil the widow and her
child
(The Monster dined with Fat that night and
hideously smiled).
Last night I had a frightful dream—and this is
what 1 saw :
An uncouth , hate-inspiring Thing that catted itself
the Law.
The while it gazed into my face, cold shudders shook
me thro'.
I woke and looked around the world — and found
MY DRKAM WAS TRUK !

The Bulletin
Registered at the General Post Office, Sydney, for Transmission by Post as a Newspaper.
Vol. 28.—N0. 1430.
THURSDAY, JULY 11, 1907.
Pkice 6d.
COLLAPSED.
The Anti-Federal party’s latest grievance has burst. The Deakin Government has arranged things so that there will be no interruption to
the ocean mail service, as there was when the blundering Reid was in office. Consequently, the people who have been saying that N.S.W.
should secede from the Commonwealth because an English letter might be delayed a little next year are very angry.

How to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin ivould oblige the paper
(and, to an extent , themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager, l'o send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
alivays is.

THURSDAY, JULY 11, 1907.
REDUCING THE N.S.W. DEBT.
A proud man these days—and to some extent a
deservedly proud man—is Joseph Carruthers,
State Premier of New South Wales. The financial
year closed on 30th June, 1907, and during that year
—provided the audit bears out the representations
made —the fixed debt of the State decreased by
£33,902. There is also a considerable addition to
the cash on hand, and if Carruthers applied
that to the reduction of the public debt, the
decrease would be no less than £722,868-
providing the figures can bear auditing. He
prefers, however, to apply it in reducing taxation,
in endowments to local government bodies and
other concessions, and in cutting down railway
charges. Still, the fact remains that the funded
debt has been reduced by £33,902. The figures
mean that, in a year of almost unprecedented pros-
perity, the State has paid off nearly half-a-farth-
fng in the £ of its liabilities —say two-fifths of
a farthing, to be fairly accurate. It isn’t a large
percentage, yet the event is so rare and incredible
that Joseph Carruthers does well to be proud of
it. The amount, certainly, wasn’t repaid out of
legitimate revenue, for the Government realised
about £1,000,000 through the sale of State assets
in the form of land, and the reduction of the
public debt only represented about Bd. in the £ of
the money thus obtained. But it is so very, very
rarely that, after selling assets to the extent of
£1,000,000, there is even a diminution of £33,902
in the debt to show for it, that State Premier
Carruthers is still entitled to put on airs to a
certain extent. And, apart from the question of
the sale of assets, there is the fact that the Com-
monwealth, by adopting a tariff policy which
Joseph Carruthers has always described as ruin-
ous, disastrous, foolish and unscientific, has added
about £2,000,000 to the State’s revenue. It has done
this without bringing down on the State any of the
disasters which Joseph Hector Carruthers fore-
told; instead, its policy has smoothed the path of
Joseph Hector O’Neill Carruthers by making
N.S.W. more prosperous than it had been for a
whole generation past. And, through the intense
economy of the Australian Government, it has
been able to give J. H. O. Carruthers the full
benefit of the increased revenue. In return
it has received nothing but abuse from J. H.
Carruthers, and has been called extravagant and
other adjectives. And Joey Carruthers, out of
this £2,000,000 of extra revenue—which lie was
able to annex while absolutely repudiating the
methods by which it was gained, and while calling
those who supplied it “ robbers ’ lias created the
surplus which his friends quote as evidence that
he is a Heaven-born financier. Also out of it and
the £1,000,000 he received by selling the State’s
assets he has reduced the N.S.W. public debt by
£33,902.
* * * *
And if there is one thing of which State
Premier Carruthers is more proud than the fact
that he managed to knock £33,902 off the State
debt last year, it is the other fact that he is going
to abolish so many taxes that it is practically
certain he won’t be able to knock anything off the
debt next year —rather that he will add a whole
lot to the debt. He claims about as much credit
for one thing as for the other, and he asks votes on
both grounds. Apparently he knows that it is a
good, honest, noble thing to reduce the State’sawful
mass of liabilities ; otherwise, instead of boasting
about the mere scrap or fragment he has done in
that direction, he should hide himself behind
the ash-barrel in the yard till his £33,902 blew
over. Yet he is going to take every care not
to repeat the performance. He says it is a grand
performance, and that he won’t do it again. This
sort of thing is calculated to set the public
wondering whether N.S.W. has any fixed policy
or any fixed ideas about its debt. If Mr.
Carruthers will say distinctly whether he regards
it as a better thing to get into debt or to get out
of debt, and under which set of circumstances he
proposes to stick out his chest furthest, he will
do something to clear matters up. A State
with 1,500,000 people, which has piled up
about £85,000,000 of debt in half-a-century, a
great deal of it without any payable assets to show
on the other side of the account; and which has,
at the same time, spent some £50,000,000 or more
of land sale revenue with no payable assets at all
to show on the other side of the account ; and
which has had no wars and hardly any defence
expenditure to plead as an excuse for its pro-
digality, should at least define its policy. To re-
duce the liability by £33,902 once in 50 years or
so, and to be absolutely bloated with hauteur over
the fact, and then to abolish about £1,000,000 of
revenue, isn’t having a public debt policy, any
more than the possession of a crumbling fortifica-
tion, an antiquated cast-iron gun, a black tracker,
a brass band, and a copy of CLesar’s Commentaries
is having a military policy.
* * * ~
The question that the State has a right to ask
its Government is just this : Is it a fair thing to
reduce the public debt, or is it not ? If it is not
a fair thing, why does Joseph behave like a wild
rooster because the debt has diminished by
£33,902—0r two-fifths of a farthing in the £? If
it is a fair thing, why hasn’t his Government the
courage to do it as a steady and regular policy,
instead of an accidental bit of a farthing once in
half-a-century ? The British policy of reducing
the public debt in time of peace is simply not to
borrow, to pay for works out of revenue, and to
set aside, as a fixed charge on the revenue, a cer-
tain sum in reduction of the debt. Britain doesn’t
begin to have a surplus until these things are paid.
The N.S.W. method is to treat about £2,000,000 of
land receipts—half of theamountbeingtheproceeds
of land sales —as ordinary revenue (a very small
part of this is now put into a public works fund,
but as the fund is simply used to pay charges that
used to be met out of revenue, it really makes
no difference); to borrow about £l,3OO,O0O; to
repay £300,000 (this is called a sinking fund, but
as it doesn’t accumulate at compound interest, it
is no sinking fund at all); and then to call any
balance on hand at the end of the year a surplus.
Bor the year just closed there was a nominal
surplus of £1,337,583, but the results panned out
this way: The public debt decreased by £33,902, and
the cash on hand increased by £688,966, and the rest
of the money isn’t accounted for so far. For this
year Joseph Carruthers reckons on reducing
taxation and making miscellaneous concessions to
the extent of about £1,161,000. It things pan out
as he expects, and tins year is as good as the Jftst
(last year was about the most prosperous
one in N.S.W. history), and the ordinary expendi-
ture doesn’t increase, and the public works expendi-
ture is no larger than it was last year, this means
that the cash balance created last year will all be
gone, and N.S.W. will add another £400,000 or
£500,000 to its net debt. If the public works ex-
penditure is much heavier than it was last year
(and considering the railways that have been
promised it seems likely to be a great deal heavier)
the addition to the net debt may be £2,400,000 or
£2,500,000.
-» « * *
In other words, the old process of going financi-
ally to the dogs and the devil will have started
afresh. N.S.W. has had an unprecedented “ sur-
plus ” of £1,337,583 (1) by reason of the Federal
tariff which its politicians denounce so virulently ;
(2) because the Australian Government, by intense
economy, handed it back much more than its pro-
mised share of theproceedsof thattariff; (3) through
treating over £1,000,000 of land sale money and
£l,OOO, 000ofrentsasrevenue;(4)through borrowing
about£l,3oo,ooo. Amid a welterof figures, wearrive
at this patch of firm ground—that in a year of
most unusual good fortune, when the State was
carrying out no large or costly public works, it
reduced its debt by £33,902. And so vast has
been the crowing over this result, and so great the
exhibition of the bogus “surplus,” that a large
proportion of the people of the State really believe
the finances have been put straight, the drift
checked, public affairs placed on a sound footing,
and so on ; and they gladly hear that about
thirty times the amount of the miserable £33,902
is to be sacrificed. This sort of accidental pro-
cess will never make any permanent improve-
ment in the finances. Premier Carruthers first
of all deducts his “ surplus ” from the State’s net
liability, and shows how magnificently he has
duced the debt. Thenhespends his “surplus.” Fur-
ther he almost persuades people that w hen he has
reduced the debt with the “surplus,” and reduced
taxation with the same “ surplus,” he still has the
identical “ surplus ” on hand—and all the while
there was no real “surplus” to begin with, save
the money gained by borrowing and by the sale of
the public assets. Thishard-drinkingprocess which
makes the public see three surpluses where, in all
honesty, there wasn’t even one, is too demoralising.
If N.S.W. is ever going to steadily reduce
its public debt, the sane process is to stop
borrowing; to set aside, say, £1,500,000 a year
of land revenue for new public works, all
patches and repairs being charged to ordinary
revenue; and then to set aside a certain
fixed annual sum for the reduction of old liabili-
ties. When these charges and the ordinary
expenses of government are paid, the balance will
be real surplus. It will be the amount that the
Premier will have to whoopabOutand to reduce tax-
ation with. But all this is a sober, serious pro-
cess, quite different from the dizzy method whereby
the community is so glad that there is a certain
sum available for the reduction of the debt that
it at once spends it twice over and floats a fresh
loan.
* * * *
In any case this paper asks State Premier Car-
ruthers for a clear-cut declaration as to his policy
in the matter of the public debt. If he believes
that it shouldn’t be paid off, why is he posing as
something between a Heaven-sent financier and a
brass band because he has reduced the fixed debt
by £33,902 and has money on hand ? If he
believes it should be paid off, why does he not, by
Act of Parliament, absolutely prohibit borrowing
except for renewal purposes, and provide for
public works out of revenue? And if he doesn’t
know what he believes, why doesn’t he say so ?
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Federal Programme.
The Australian Parliament lias resumed business
at the old address. It now consists of four parties
—the Deakin Government and its followers ; the
Labor party ; the handful of allegedly Free-
trade Anti-Socialists who follow Reid when
he is there, which is seldom, and who admire the
back view of Joseph Cook at other times; and
the Protectionist Anti-Socialists who refuse to
recognise either Reid or Cook, but who have, so
far, elected no leader of their own. No party
has a majority, and no party has any present hope
of one. The smallest party, consisting of the
allegedly Freetrade Anti-Socialists who want
office, lias a good deal to say about the fact that
the second largest party—the Democratic Pro-
tectionists who are in office —hasn’t a majority ;
but no party can get a majority except by selling
some of its principles for the sake of office, as
Joseph Cook did, so the gibe of the smallest
party may pass unnoticed. As Federal politics
stand, the obvious remedy is to abandon the
old unworkable British theory that a Gov-
ernment must have a majority which will
stand to it whether it is right or wrong, and to
adopt the simple process of electing a Ministry to
hold office for the duration of Parliament. When
a Ministry is thus situated, it doesn’t require a
majority. It is merely expected to administer
the departments capably, to submit its measures
to Parliament, and to go on administering the
departments capably, whether its measures are
accepted or rejected. Under such conditions it
doesn’t really matter whether the House nominally
contains one party or 25 parties. There are, in
practice, two parties and only two —the crowd
which is in favor of the Bill, motion or amend-
ment then before the House, and the one which is
against it. Under the system of elective Minis-
tries, all the ingenuity of the devil and his entire
herd of satellites cannot, for practical purposes,
make more than two parties. In the absenceof this
simple and logical device, the Deakin Government
faces Parliament with a fairly good programme
and the prospect of leading a rather scrambling
existence—with the reservation that any other
Government that could be built under present
conditions would have to scramble for its living a
good deal harder. The Deakin programme in-
cludes —(1) High Protection, (2) bounties to aid
Australian industries, (3) transfer of State debts
to the Commonwealth, (4) Federal old-age pen-
sions, (s)anewdefencesystem, (6) the appointment
of a High Commissioner, (7) the promotion of
desirable immigration, (8) the fixing of the
Australian capital, (9) the taking over of the
Northern Territory, (10) the establishment of a
uniform postal system, uniform banking and in-
solvency laws, and other matters. These are all
matters which urgently call for attention. The
pity is that some of the statements are rather
vague, but the vagueness will be cleared up as
soon as the measures get before Parliament, and
they will begin to get before Parliament
as soon as the useless rag-tag and bob-
tail section of Parliament ceases to block
the way by making vague but lengthy speeches
about the vagueness of the Address, under pretence
of debating the reply to the Address. The one
absolutely and definitely regrettable circumstance
is the absence of any suggestion about a Federal
graduated land-tax, to provide money for the
building of the transcontinental railway, and to
break up the great useless estates, and so supply
land for the desirable immigrants which the
Deakin Government is anxious to secure. Prime
Minister Deakin seems to be shedding his lament-
able garment of affability by degrees, but in his
consideration for small State prejudices and big
landlords he is still a great deal too affable.
A Disgruntled Opposition.
There was something very pitiful in the deba-te
on the Address-in-Reply in the Australian Parlia-
ment last week. The subject uppermost in all
minds was the return of the Australian delegates
from England, and none of the Opposition
speakers could avoid a reference to it; and none
was able to express any broad-minded view. The
snarling Mr. Cook set the key with some slight
disparaging praise, flavored with sarcasm and set
around with sneers, and his followers obediently
piped to the same tune. Yet never was there
a clearer call for an Opposition to forget
that it was its business “to oppose.” Two
delegations had just returned from London, after
attending the Navigation and Imperial Confer-
ences. In each case the delegation set out in an
atmosphere of doubt and anxiety. The Naviga-
tion Conference delegates, it was freely asserted
by the Opposition, were ignorant land-lubbers
(excluding Mr. Dugald Thomson, who was ex-
pected by them to be a brilliant relief to the
“stupidity ” of Mr. Lyne, and, as a matter of fact,
was his very faithful and intelligent follower in
the Conference), and even non-partisan critics
felt some doubts as to their success when matched
against British experts. But, as a matter of cold
truth, the Australian delegation to that Con-
ference took the lead from the first, and
Mr. Lyne and Mr. Hughes came back having
secured all they asked for. All that the Oppo-
sition has to say is some petty sneer—with the
brand “ W.K.” clearly on its quarter—about motor
cars and ’buses. As to Mr. Deakin and the
Imperial Conference, the Opposition forecasts
were even more dismal. London would put this
Prime Minister of a minority party, this servant
of Socialism, in his proper place. It was a pity,
they affected to think, that Australia’s interests
should so suffer, but it was a proper punish-
ment for Australia’s folly in not having Mr.
G. Reid in power. As the facts resolved them-
selves, Mr. Deakin’s success in London, both in
the Conference and in the wider field ruled by pub-
lic opinion, was so astonishing as to be one of the
romances of modern politics. Shedding for the
occasion all those limitations which so often make
him in local politics “a beautiful, ineffectual
angel,” he stood out as the leading statesman in
all that great gathering of public men. It was
\ustralia first, the rest nowhere. Canada took a
back, and sulky, seat. Representatives of the
other dominions sank their individualities cheer-
fully to become Deakinites. Judging from the
London press, there was practically no one but
Deakin and Australia at the Conference. In the
streets, in the theatres, in the banqueting
halls, the success was repeated. In those
circles where politics did not enter, Mrs.
Deakin took up the representation of Australia,
and, if that were possible, kept it still more
emphatically to the front. As an ad vertisement
for a continent which wants British immigrants,
*he success of Australia at the Conference was
worth much. As steps towards more complete
national entity, the concessions gained at the Con-
ference were of great significance. As a tribute
to national self-esteem, the Df.akin boom in
London atones even for the defeat of Bill Squires.
And the Australian Opposition—as usual anti-
national—has no tribute to pay except a sneer !
—♦
Lost, a Grievance.
The opening of the new session of the Australian
Parliament found the anti-Federal push in a state
of some gloom. It transpired, in the first place,
that the Government had cancelled the new ocean
mail contract by reason of the unsatisfactory con-
duct of the contractors. The Government accepted
the contract, in the first place, on plain business
principles because it promised to be the best and
cheapest one offering; it cancelled it, in the
second place, on plain business principles because
the wealthy English contractors didn’t keep their
promises, and because George Pile’s countrymen
were, in this matter, a crowd of dodgers and
liars. It was all a plain business matter ; old
J. N. Brunker, M.L.C., never sold a cow on
simpler business lines. Then it was announced,
on plain business principles, that arrangements
are being made to extend the present contract till
January, 1909, to give time for the calling of new
tenders. There is apparently not the least
chance of any such collapse as there was in
1905, when George Reid was Premier and
the Reid-Cook-Pumpkin Smith party found
itself up against a dead wall of trouble over its
mails. These announcements left the anti-
Eederal party with a burst grievance on its hands.
It had been going on in the Sydney papers in a
very large way over that contract. At a time
when every mail was leaving and arriving as regu-
larly as clockwork, when the faked invoice and
the bill for the gold-washed jewellery were never
a day behind time, when all the mail services were
working well, and the nearest approach to trouble
was the fact that one of them (only one) was due
to expire next year, and the syndicate which
had promised to take up the running looked a
trifle doubtful—at this peaceful time the small
anti-Federal push in Sydney raved as if the end
of the world was in sight. It went on in a
way that would hardly be justifiable if
100,000 invaders were in sight of the city. It
professed to think that all was lost. It wanted to
know what would become of us. It put up the
most amazing display of mock terror and sham
distress and bogus heroism. Some crazy human
arabesque wrote to the Sydney Telegraph, pro-
posing the dissolution of the Commonwealth. An
especial and monumental case of hysteria wrote,
saying that the Commonwealth was already in
effect dissolved by reason of this maker. A mad-
dened and terrified solicitor, one A. Consett
Stephen, wrote to the same congenial Telegraph
to say that it was evident we would soon have no
mail service at all—all because one contract was
due to expire a good many months ahead ; and
he urged the State Government of N.S.W. to
illegally make its own mail contract, the only
possible inference being that it was to take
possession of the Federal mails by violence, insur-
rection, and bloodshed, and put them on board its
ships. The Australian Government took no notice
of these silly school boy heroics, but the perpetrators
of them stuck to their grievance, and about three
papers, half-a-dozen letter-writers, and three or
four politicians made as much noise as if a little
possible delay in the arrival of a letter from Eng-
land next year was equivalent to the fall of
Babylon. The hypocrisy of the ill-conditioned
little mob was evidenced by the fact that a real
break-down of a mail service three years ago,
when Reid, of N.S.W., was Federal Prime
Minister, was taken rather good-naturedly in
Sydney ; while now' a mere faint prospect of a
hitch next year, but with Deakin, of Victoria, in
the Prime Minister’s place, caused the six or
eight anti-Federalists of Sydney to perform as if
the Bulgarian massacres had broken out on the
premises. And now there is a dreadful prospect
that there will be no hitch, no break in the
continuity of the mail service, no delay in the
delivery of the faked invoice, no loss of money,
no break up of the, Commonwealth, no excuse for
Mr. A. Consett Stephen to behave like a Cen-
tral American revolutionary general, and perish
on the steps of the Sydney G.P.O. while trying
to capture the mails. And what is life to an anti-
Federalist if he has lost his grievance? Better
the cold and silent tomb.

The Imported Spaniard.
“ Squidge ” (after looking at the sample)
Consider the methods employed by the Colonial Sugar
Refining Co., or its agents, in the importation of those
hundred unfortunate devils of Spaniards who were slung
into Queensland the other day to toil on the sugar plan-
tations ! The original agreement made with the Com-
monwealth Government provided that the C.S.R., in
obtaining contract immigrants, should, if possible, secure
Britons, For this batch they wanted about 120 men, and
I believe they could have got 600 in Lincolnshire alone, but
somebody or other put every man on to a physical exami-
nation of such severity that only eight came through.
Those eight were allowed to contract for Australia.
The C.S.R. was then in the position of having found it
impossible to obtain Britons, so it sent to Spain for the
balance of the men. One Ferrer obtained the job of
recruiting in Spain. It was done just the same way as it
has been done in the islands for the last ever-so-
many years. It is said that Ferrer did not
bother going out of Madrid. He just raked up
100 unemployed by deputy ; there are always droves
of them in Spain—in fact, ’most all Spain is unemployed.
He told them a few glib remarks —the Spaniards call
them lies—about Queensland, and the sort of work they
would be expected to perform, and even, the Spaniards
allege, lied to them over the pay they would receive. The
actual wages to be paid in Australia are 225. lid. a week in
the slack season, and 255. in harvest —with rations, of
course. Ferrer, so the Dons say, worked this 225. fid.
and 255. out in Spanish money pirn l the rate of exchange,
and thereby made it considerably more than it really
represents. Of course, Don Asturias now declares that
he would never have signed the contract had he known.
Caramba !
Then the O.S.R. intends to dock from the first year’s
wages the cost of the pas-age out. This fact is said to
have slipped Ferrer’s memory, as did the rather
inhuman clause that any loss of work occasioned through
sickness or injury should be debited to the laborer —
which, being interpreted, means that if the climate
doesn’t happen to suit the hidalgos, that is
not going to affect the pocket of the O.S.R.
Spain had all this explained to it by the sellers
of wine at Fremantle and Melbourne, and it
became mighty wrath and rolled out huge oaths at the
C.S.R. and at Ferrer. These Spaniards are soft-handed,
soft-bodied, unacclimatised fellows, who cannot hold up
their heads beside the Australian laborer. Probably a
quarter of the physical test which was imposed on the
Ensrlish applicants would have knocked them out.
When the Bulow reached Fremantle some of the Dons
saw men dieging holes in the streets for electric trams,
and repairing railway lines. They said, “Ha! here is
the work we know. We will not go to the canefields.
We will stay here.” Most of them were restrained, but
seven or eisrht deserted the ship, fractured their contracts
with the C.S.R. Co., and obtained work in Fremantle.
This is what will become of all of them. They
will be unable to carry out their contracts, and
will be shot out on to the outside labor market.
They are not prohibited immigrants. They can stay here,
and they will do navvies’ work for a few shillings a week
unless they drift to the pea-nut or the oyster. Anyhow,
this little sample lot of contract immigrants has shown


Australia what to expect. There are, if report speaks
truly, 1050 of them to come. The Government should look
into this matter, and tell the O.S.R. Co. that it will
have to play the game fairly, and that its methods of
proving to itself that British labor can’t be got are most
unsatisfactory.
Beelzebub and Carruthers and the Bloke.
The weepful complaints of a few disordered Anti-
Australians about being under the “Federal
yoke ” are calculated to make the thoughtful
person realise that there is a good deal of “ yoke ”
in the nature of things. For instance, this world
is supposed, with the rest of the universe, to be
under the Divine yoke (it is expressly defined as
a yoke in the New Testament). Australia is under
the British yoke. N.S.W. is under the Australian
or Federal yoke. Sydney is under the State yoke
of N.S.W. The dirty and bankrupt municipality
of Camperdown refuses to be under the yoke of
Sydney, lest it should have to pay for having
itself cleaned. And the Bloke in the back street
of Camperdown hits the sanitary inspector in the
eye when he is summoned for having a foul and
infectious back yard, because his soul revolts at
the idea of being under the municipal yoke.
And from Lucifer, who warred against God,
down to the Bloke in the insanitary
back lane of Camperdown, they are all plain
anarchists, revolting against order. Carruthers
is, after all, only the middle anarchist of the row.
He comes about midway between Beelzebub and
the Bloke. And ever since man got a toothbrush
and a gallows, and began to be civilised, Beelze-
bub and Carruthers and the Bloke have, in one
form or another, been disturbing elements.
The Slanderers of N.S.W., especially one
M'Dougall.
New South Wales gets a mighty poor advertise-
ment out of the members of its anti-Federal
push. Their yap about the crushed and ruined
condition of the State tends to make outsiders
fancy that N.S.W. will soon have to repudiate
its public debt, and to make immigrants think it
is hardly worth while coming to a place which is
in such a fearsome condition. And in other ways
the yapping section throws mud in the eye of Ma
State and casts discredit on the tail of Ma’s
garment. Consider, for instance, this letter
(which is only a sample of many dozen letters)
written to Sydney Evening News by a poor,
hysterical creature described as “ Alderman J. C.
M‘Dougall ” :
ROBBING THE PEOPLE.
Alderman J. 0. M’Dougall writes “The millions of
money, swelling as the surplus of the State, is being
wrung out of the pockets of the people. Have the wages
of the workers since Federation increased ? Have they
had more home comforts at less cost ? Have their rents
been reduced ? These are questions that call for serious
consideration, and yet we hear of more taxation about to
be put on by the Federal Gov rnment before the end of
the present month. Fifty per cent, is spoken of on cer-
tain lines. All to be wrung out of the people’s hard earn-
ings to bolster up some few Melbourne manufacturers.
The Labor party in the English Parliament have soon
found out the policy of Mr. Deakin’s ingenuous prefer-
ential dodge. Yet the Labor party in the States here
want to support him, so as to obtain Socialism. Local
government during 19U8 will also tax the ratepayers of
New South Wales to an extent they do not yet know. All
this is to crush the small and struggling worker ; and yet
he slumbers on, and submits to the extra load. The last
straw will even break a donkey’s back. All this imposi-
tion, upon the people is to help to support the various
Houses of Parliament, High Court Judges; yet Federal
servants, etc., are not to pay income taxes.
Of course, this dreadful person is just the same
unscrupulous inventor of evidence as the rest of
his tribe. Federal servants are to pay State income-
tax where there is one (a Bill to make sure of that
fact is promised this session), but as the N.S.W.
State Government has promised to abolish the tax
on all incomes except those arising from property,
and neither Federal nor State servants will have
to pay anything. Heaven only knows what the
awful alderman is braying about. And there is a
promise that, instead of the burden being in-
creased, a whole lot of other taxes will come off.
Also, local government taxation in N.S.W. will
consist mainly in calling the land-tax a local
instead of a State impost. But these are side
issues. It is the constant wail of people like the
M‘Doug all Creature, that the only possible pur-
pose of a national Australian tariff is to bolster up
“a few Melbourne manufacturers,” or “a few Vic-
torian manufacturers,” that is the curse of N.S.W.
Everyone knows that the tariff applies equally
to all Australia, so the only possible inference to
be drawn from this shabby, mean, grudging little
lie is that the benefit of the tariff is confined to
Victoria because Victoria is the only State
that has manufactures. People who don’t
understand the vastness, and at the same
time the worm-like meanness, of Sydney’s
little knot of anti-Federal perverters, are
driven to suppose something like that. The con-
stant yell about the benefit which “ Melbourne
manufacturers ” derive from the tariff is gradually
creating an impression throughout the empire
that Melbourne is not only the great manufactur-
ing centre of Australia, but also the only one.
It is the biggest advertisement Melbourne ever
got. Wherever the Sydney Telegraph and the
Evening News go, or are quoted from, people
have it dinned into their ears by leading
articles, long and short paragraphs, columns
of Parliamentary comment, and the letters
of people like the M'Dougall Creature, that
Melbourne is a manufacturing city and Victoria a
manufacturing State. They hear so much of it
that they begin to imagine that Victoria is equal
to England for manufactures, and that Melbourne
is something greater than Manchester. And
they conclude that Sydney is a quiet bucolic
settlement, deriving no benefit from a tariff that
fosters manufactures because it has none, and full
of a grudging envy of busy, affluent Melbourne.
They learn only three things about N.S.W. from
the wails of people like J. L. Adams and the
M‘Dougall Creature: (1) That it has been
“robbed”—and people have a contempt for one
who is robbed ; (2) that it is so unimportant that
its delegation in the Federal Parliament really
amounts to nothing, consequently it lias no voice
in Australian affairs and is “under the heel of
the Commonwealth”; (3) that it is so far behind
in the matter of industry that “the manufac-
turers of Melbourne” look great, amazing
and incredible to its eyes,, and it can’t stop
talking about them. Never for a moment
is it likely to dawn on them that N.S.W. has nearly
75,000 employees in its factories; that they have
increased by 25 per cent, since the foundation of
the Commonwealth ; that N.S.W. is now close up
to Victoria in manufactures; that it has about
£8,000;000 invested in machinery and plant; that
the tariff helps N.S.W. industries as much as
those of any other State ; that in 1904 (the last
year covered by the “Official Year Book of
N.S.W.”), while the whole value of all the State’s
agricultural produce was a miserable £5,414,000,
the profits of its manufactures were £9,900,000;
mid that while agriculture, dairying and poultry-
farming, all put together, only yielded that year
j£6 2s. for each inhabitant, manufactures yielded
kfi 16s. lid. How can they imagine any such
(things with the Telegraph and the Evening
News, and people like the M‘Dougall Creature,
telling them all the time about “ Victorian manu-
factures ” as if they were the only ones in the
Commonwealth, and assuring them, by the
constant statement that only Victoria profits by
the tariff, that there are no N.S.W. manufactures
to be profited ? This constant dragging of the
good name of N.S.W. in the mud by a few parish-
minded scandal-mongers has risen to the dimen-
sions of a public disgrace.
The Disheartened Victorian Pedagogue.
This letter, from a Victorian teacher (the Depart-
ment may make unsuccessful application for his
name, if it likes), is one of half-a-dozen or more,
all to the same effect. It is impossible to find
space for the other five :
The evil that Governments do lives after them. In 1894
the Victorian Parliament passed a Regrading Act, which,
by seriously reducing the salaries paid to teachers during
their first 10 years of service and rendering it hard to get
promotion to the princely wage of £3 a week, struck a blow
at the efficiency of the teaching staff. At the same time
heavy percentage reductions were put on all grades of
salaries from £lOO and upwards. And this was done in a
supremely mutton-headed way ; in fact, it was having
sheep’s head for dinner to-day that reminded me of the
whole wretched business and set me writing to you. In
one case—typical of others—a young teacher, drawing £99
and some odd shillings annually, by hard work increased
the percentage of his school at examination, and thereby
raised his salary to £l.OO 18s., whereupon he qualified to
share in the reduction, and his pay, as a reward for
efficiency, went down to under £96. And, later on, the
Irvine Government came along with another dose of
retrenchment.
In the meantime the Peacock Government had created
the directorate, giving over the whole control of Primary
Education to Tate. This gentleman is of undoubted
brain power and of commendable enthusiasm, but
apparently lacks tact, as evidenced by the fact that he
signalised his advent to power by describing the average
teacher as a “ decent duffer” —an injudicious proceeding 1 ,
which hardened the heart of the whole Service. Tate
immediately pinned his faith to young men —particularly
those who were students during his last years as principal
of the Training College. These were sent forth as apostles,
and preached to the country teacher whose heart
was broken by poor pay and deferred promotion,
and whose dander had been “riz” by the “decent
duffer ” episode.
Concurrently with these events, Messrs. Carew-
Smythe and Byatt were imported from England, to
organise drawing and manual training respectively. This
was another blow to the Service, which grew alarmed, then
morbid, at finding preferment given to “Tate’s students”
and foreigners. And the inevitable has happened, which
inevitable is that promising men are leaving and young-
sters won’t enter the State teaching profession. In
answer to a call for 80 junior teachers only 50 have
applied, whereas in 1893, for less than 30 vacancies, there
were 1000 applicants ! Nobody wants to get in, but the
right sort of men are getting out. During the last 10
years two men succeeded in gaining the M.A. degree by
dint of burning the midnight oil after the day’s teaching.
What has the Department done for these men? One of
them is filling a subordinate position at the Continuation
School at poor pay ; the other was snapped up by a
leading private school a few months after taking his
degree. Then there are others : One man (3rd year Arts)
has turned in disgust to a news agency business ; another
(also a 3rd year Arts) is turning, at the age of 36, to Law ;
another has become a hotel licensee.
Meanwhile many mysterious things are happening.
Senior applicants are not getting positions. Teachers
are getting schools and assistantships in ways no fellah
can understand. Perhaps Tate is within his province in
these things, but one fact is peculiar—four of the men
appointed to the Continuation School ivere in the Train-
ing College in one year under Tate. For these and other
reasons, 99 out of 100 teachers are eloquent advocates for
the Bar, the bottle-oh profession, the Church, anything
rather than the Education Department as a career for
their own, and other people’s, sons. And it is the rising
generation of Victorians that is going to suffer the most.
Also another man writes :
The blight of the boom breakage has wrecked the
lives of a generation of Victorian teachers. They had about
13 years of practically wasted time to begin with. Their
promotion was stopped ; their pay was cut down ; their
work was increased through the desire to economise by
not filling vacancies ; and, owing to the ghastly saving
in repairs, they had all manner of small discomforts added
to poverty, hopelessness and overwork. And now that
good times have returned, the Department which, by its
policy, made them what they are, apparently reckons
that they look too old and shabby to be worth promot-
ing. Many of them are really only beginning now, 14
years after the event, to feel the real consequences of
the breakage of Matthew Davies’ land hanks. They
thought, till Tate came, that the disaster meant poverty
and stagnation till the good times returned. Now,
when they find that the benefits of the good times are
mostly for Tate’s fresh young friends, they realise that
that bygone crash means for them stagnation and poverty
for life.

The Sydney Railway Problem.
“ The time has come,” says Minister Lee, “ when
the Sydney city trams must be superseded by rail-
ways.” That is obviously true, but Lee s pro-
posals, so far, are only patchwork. He hasn’t yet
gathered up the courage to say that Owe’Sulli-
van’s white stone elephant of a railway station in
an awkward suburb is already obsolete, that the
main city station should be built somewhere about
Wyriyard-square, and that it is almost certain the
elephant will have to go almost before it has fairly
begun to arrive. By way of avoiding the issue and
postponing the responsibility, there is vague talk
of branch railways into the city, and such- like de-
vices, as if Sydney were a village like Brewarrina,
and only required a branch from the important
city of Redfern to meet its demands. Any such
idea is quite hopeless. The trouble is that the
great bulk of the passengers from the swarming
suburban trains are emptied out a mile short
of where they want to go. The rush for the
tram-cars is like an insurrection in Sheol
with the smallest devil being trodden on in the
scrum, and at busy times, though there are about
as many trams as the narrow streets can possibly
hold, they look like ant-heaps on wheels as they
scurry along. And things are about as bad with
the tram-service of the suburbs that lie off the
railway line. Of course, branches might be built
from the great Owe’Sullivan jest to Waverley,
Bondi, etc., but then people, as a rule, don’t want
to go from Waverley, Bondi, etc., to Redfern ;
they want to go from those places to the city.
And a branch might be built from Owe’Sulli-
van’s Folly into the city, but then there would be
the absurd spectacle of Sydney with a cheap little
congested station, and Redfern with an almost
deserted palace. And if a branch or extension
were built into Sydney the people of the eastern
suburbs would demand that their lines, when they
are built, should connect, not with the palace at
Redfern where they don’t want to go, but with
the city station where they do want to go. Then
the city station, even if it were a mere apology to
begin with, would have to grow to the size of the
present Redfern terminus, whether it professed to
be the main station or not. The situation goes
into these four propositions
(1) There must be a railway extension into the city.
(2) If there is a railway extension into the city, every-
body will demand that the train he travels by shall go
into the city, and the present Central Station will be
almost deserted. With all its glass and marble, it will
sink to the level of Newtown or Burvvood:
(3) If railways are built to the eastern suburbs, the
people, there will demand that they be connected with
the city, and any attempt to take them to White
Elephant terminus will be met with rage and contumely.
So the present Central Station will stay deserted.
(4) In short, when once the railway is carried into the
city, nothing can keep the present Sydney Central
Station busy enough to justify even half its bricks and
mortar. And to try to devise circuitous fancy schemes
for taking the railway into the city, and yet retaining
enough business at the present remote station to justify
its present size and pomp, is attempting the impossible,
and merely laying the foundation for another railway
problem half-a-dozen years hence.
It is a wise man and a wise Government that can
recognise a bad debt when they see it. The Owe’-
Sullivan station is one of N.S.W.’s bad debts,
and the one thing to do now is to write it off.
With a main station at Wynyard Square, the
North Shore bridge would follow as a matter of
course. Then the suburban train that came in
from Parramatta could drop its load, take in a
new one, and go on as the outward train to
Wahroonga, or somewhere else on the North Shore
line. Most of the shunting could be done far
away from the city, at a great saving of time,
space and risk. There Would no longer be the
present absurd spectacle of the engine which has
brought in a suburban train having to loaf use-
lessly in the corner between the train and the
dead end of the line till it is time for another
engine to take the train out again so that the
engine which brought it in can escape. In
fact, there would be a possibility of a whole lot'
of improvements and economies when Owe’Sulli-
van’s bad debt was written off. And it wouldn’t
be all loss. A station about the size of the old
Eveleigh premises would still be required some-
where near the present site, and the present
establishment would make a good quarry.

An Old Mob with a New Brand.
The death of a weary old man near Melbourne
the other day was the end of, perhaps, the last
link of a chain between the present and a remark-
ably interesting period of Australian history.
Transportation ceased in 1840. Those who had
fought for its abolition found it hard during the
next year or two to justify their agitation. AH
they could urge upon the people was that
they should wait, and the benefits which
had been foretold would show themselves.
But the people were impatient—as a great
many people always are. While few, if any, of
the promised benefits were in view, the immediate
ill effects were patent and painful enough. The
money that the British Government sent out for
the maintenance of the convicts and the gaolers
flowed in such a diminished stream that
the colony began to feel a tight pinch. Property
values began to shrink. Just assume of the men
in Queensland predicted that sugar land would
have no value when the Kanaka went, so men
croaked in the late ’thirties and early ’forties that
land in N.S. Wales would have no value when
the convict went. Confidence was therefore
shaken; and what the stoppage of the gaolers’
pay failed to do to complete the crisis, an un-
timely fall in the market value of Australian pro-
ducts accomplished. Sheep sold down to Is.
per head. The Bank of Australia had been
established in 1826. It began badly, for it
made a heavy loss by a robbery two years
afterwards. In 1843—three years after trans-
portation ceased —the crisis arrived, and the
bank had to shut its doors. The situation was
desperate, for ordinary realisation was out of the
question. Even the Bank of New South Wales,
formed in 1817 under more favorable auspices,
had, in this same year 1843, to get relief from the
Treasury, a run having set in on account of a
rumor that the Governor had gone through the
securities and declared the whole lot ‘ ‘ not worth
three straws.” Liquidation in the case of the
Bank of Australia being impossible in the
ordinary way, Wentworth introduced a Bill to
allow the assets to be distributed by lottery. The
Governor would not, however, give his assent,
and the Bill had to go to England. The English
authorities also refused assent. But the situation
was far too serious to be governed by a small thing
like that, and the lottery took place in the City
Theatre, Sydney, in 1845, when 11,248 lots were
disposed of. It was in connection with that
matter that the weary old man who died in Mel-
bourne last week figured. He was Edward
Sandford, who had studied law and was variously
solicitor, secretary, and cashier of the unfortunate
bank. But the crisis did not end with what
Sandford and his colleagues were able to do.
There was a strong party which, connecting the
crisis, not only in the bank but in the whole
colony, with the stoppage of transportation,
buzzed continually and energetically to have the
System started again. Wentworth got a com-
mittee appointed to inquire whether, after all, the
abolition of transportation hadn't been all wrong.
The committee whitewashed the odious System,
and Dangar, MacArthur, and others approved
jubilantly. Happily, the great body of the
people had more sense. They listened to
the counsel of the more far-seeing men who
counselled patience—who urged the colony
to keep its hands off till the paint was dry.
And it did, with the result that there are
perhaps not three reputedly sane men in Aus-
tralia who now want the System back again.
Almost the whole population winces, indeed, at a
reminder that Australia ever had a System and a
birth-stain. Nevertheless, to-day the political
and social party which, a little over 60 years ago,
schemed against the anti-transportation move-
ment, and when only disaster was apparent,
screeched its inevitable “I told you so”—that
unpatriotic party of selfishness and little-minded-
ness repeats the same cry and the same tactics
with regard to Australian Federation. And with
far less justification. For happily no such
adversity, no such avalanche of trouble as followed
upon the stoppage of the System, has come witli
Federation. On the contrary, many actual bene-
fits are evident enough. Aet because the gain
that was foretold has not all been immediately
realised, we have our Salomons and our Car-
ruthers and our jealous little State insects
buzzing and harassing and impeding yet, less
courageous than the Dangars and MacArthurs
and the pro-convicts of 60 years ago, afraid to
strike openly.
There art) indications that, in one respect, Aus-
tralia will not much longer lag behind China. In
various parts of the interior of the Celestial
Empire there are arsenals turning out arms and
ammunition for the equipment of the new army
that the Brother of the Sun is raising to back up
any arguments he may place before t.he Diplomatic
Corps at Pekin. The Bulletin has for years tried
to hammer the necessity for such a factory into
the public mind, and there are indications that
something is going to be done. General Gordon
states that he has a definite proposal to lay before
his chief, and will recommend a site where tHe
raw material for use in an arms and ammunition
factory is available on the spot, and to which more
material can be brought from the interior of the
country, without depending on the seaboard in
any way. This is very gratifying. In war time,
the centre from which thearms and ammunition are
distributed becomes the very heart of the country.
But, till the population increases vastly, it will be
absolute folly to have theheartof the country placed
within striking distance from the sea. Nature
did not place theheartof any animal immediately
under the skin. The General says his proposals
will appeal to the Minister from a business point
of view. This is also gratifying, but even if Aus-
tralia has to pay for its locally-made arms and
ammunition twice or three times as much as the
price at which they could be imported, it is neces-
sary that the money should be paid. An invasion of
Australia in force argues at least temporary com-
mand of the sea by the invader, and, under such con-
ditions, the price of war material would soar up
to a figure that in one campaign would more than
obliterate the savings of half a century of impor-
tation. During the South African struggle the
Boers had to abandon more than one almost
impregnable position, because the defending force
had not sufficient ammunition (theßoers ifnported
their material) with which to stand a regular
siege, and had to get out before they could be
surrounded, as investment meant certain capture.
Australia doesn’t want to see any of its armies in
that position, just to put the profit of making its
war material in foreign pockets. The matter of
defence is one in which old Gorgon Graham's
advice to mind your own business, own your own
business, and run your own business, is worth
pasting in the national hat.
The wild talk of taking a referendum of N.S.W.
electors as to whether the State shall remain in
the Commonwealth or not has produced one good
result. There is now being circulated for signa-
ture a petition to the House of Representatives
praying that the Commonwealth Parliament
“ will cause a referendum to be taken in order
that the following issues may be submitted to the
people of Australia : Are you in favor of the
amendment of the Constitution to provide (1) for
the abolition of the State Governments and
Parliaments, as at present existing, and the trans-
fer of their property and powers to the Govern-
ment and Parliament of the Commonwealth, and
(2) for the establishment of new State Govern-
ments by the Parliament of the Commonwealth,
with such powers and functions as that Parlia-
ment may deem fit to confer upon them?”
The petition points out that when the
Commonwealth was established it was the
popular belief that it would lead to large re-
ductions in the cost of State government; that so
far little or no reduction has been made, and the
State Governments still maintain themselves on
an altogether unnecessary scale; that the exist-
ence of the State Governments with their present
powers prevents national development, and per-
petuates the provincial jealousies which the
Commonwealth was intended to destroy : and that
the only satisfactory remedy is the Unification of
Australia, the State Governments being retained
only for the purpose of local government under
Commonwealth control. People who wish to sign
this petition can obtain copies from Mr. John Lh
Steel, 192 Castlereagh-street, Sydney. Mr. Steel
will gladly forward copies to anyone who writes
for them.
The Lamentations of a Pedagogue
Joseph Carruthers, oppressed with revenue and not
knowing what to do with it, proposes to leave off asking-
rich people for income-tax. I wouldn’t distress .1. Car-
ruthkrs for the world, and I wouldn’t for anything like
the rich people to feel slighted, so I’m going to tell the
Premier something. For I know of at least four methods
by which he can get rid of all the money derived from
the tax, and even if he puts the whole four into opera-
tion he will be doing nothing more than justice. These
methods are—(l) provide up-to-date material in the public
schools, so that parents (most of them poor people) and
teachers (frequently just as poor) will not have to pro-
vide it out of their own pockets; (2) raise teachers’
salaries (admittedly too small) all round ; (3) abolish the
re-grading scheme ; and (4) pay teachers according to
their classifications.
Regarding No. 1, the N.S.W. Education Department
has, since the abolition of school fees, tried to obtain
the fees indirectly by promoting the establishment of
Parents’Associations, the members of which mostly sub-
scribe an amount equal to what they would have paid in
fees, had fees not been abolished. A couple of weeks ago
I heard an inspector declare that, in the present financial
condition of the State, it was impossible for the Edu-
cation Department to supply much of the material neces-
sary for the proper working of the schools. Yet about
the same time Carruthers was blowing about the
finances of the State, and avowing his intention of wiping,
out the income-tax.
So much has been written in The Bulletin about the
smallness of teachers’ salaries that it is unnecessary to
repeat it.
The re-grading—or is it degrading?—system is a mean
and iniquitous scheme, b.v which teachers are deprived of
the increases of salary fairly due to them. The injustice
of payment of teachers by average attendance instead of
by classification may be gauged from the fact that, under
it, a second-class teacher may get a bigger salary than a
first-class one, and a third-class more than a second-class.
According to latest available figures, Jiere are 2451
State schools in N.S.W., and of these about 2200 are in
charge of male teachers. Approximately 1100 of these
have schools lower than their classifications, but are ex-
pected to do work in accordance with their classifications,
and not according to the amount of their salaries. Here
are a few of the worst cases :
A I.A teacher, who if paid according to his classifica-
tion would get from £350 to £4OO, is getting £228 ;
3 I.B’s are getting £192, instead of £3OO ; 7 11. A’s £l5O,
and 2 11. A’s £132, instead of from £228 to £240 ; 10 11. B’s,
£126 instead of from £192 to £204 ; 15 III.A’s and
6 III.B’s £BB, instead of £l6B and £144 respectively.
Women teachers, male assistants, and pupil teachers are
even worse off.
And yet Joseph throws a chest, and declares that he
has so much money that the best way he can think of to
prevent such a thing- happening again is to sweep the
income tax off the face of N.S.W.
