Having succeeded in breaking down Arbitration
in N.S. Wales, the Sydney daily papers and Fat-
man employers have got the only alternative, and it
is to be hoped they like it and what it promises to
lead to. Already the Sydney coal-lumpers have
been out on strike for about six weeks, and in the
course of the argument numerous heads have been
broken, over a dozen policemen are constantly
employed on special work in connection with the
unpleasantness, police courts have been occupied
for days over the incidental troubles, and hardly
a day passes but somebody on one side or
other of the row isn’t lodged in gaol to be
kept at public expense. Yet the unpleasantness
has only just exhibited its nose, so to speak ;
what its whole body may be Heaven only knows,
but already the coal and shale miners are showing

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He loaded up his bullock-dray,
And carelessly he picked his way—
And in the grooves his cart-wheels made
Ten thousand people tread to-day.
All ways were his, with nought to guide ;
The easy, curving track he tried;
And yet behind his thoughtless wheels
He countless generations tied.
As he his way out West essayed
A grey metropolis he made.
Walled in to all eternity,
We follow where his bullocks strayed.
With but his careless whim to serve,
He let his lazy bullocks swerve;
And still between his gum-trees tall
The impatient tram-cars twist and curve.
N.S.W.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.

and tried Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy, which
quickly cured me. Now I keep a bottle on hand,
and take ,a dose at the first symptom of a cold, ;
and have never since had a cold or cough.”

How “ Merchants Have Been Incon-
venienced.”
Every day the Australian Star (Sydney) holds
’• two minute chats with busy people ” on events
of the moment. They are not very bright chats,
as a rule, and often it is hard to guess whether
the “eminent citizen” or other vague personage is
the reporter himself, or the office boy, or some
really eminent outsider. Last Monday the Star
allegedly interviewed a “ prominent business
man ” about the mail contract, and either got
from him, or devised on its own account, such a
very small anti-Federal lie and such a small mean
grumble, that, for their very smallness, they de-
serve a notice :
“And so the Federal Government is going to deal
leniently with the mail syndicate,” was a remark made
by a prominent business man to a Star reporter this,
morning.
“ So it has been said ; but what does it mean ?”
“ That is just what I would like to know. If it means
that the bond of £25,000 is not going to be enforced, you
can depend upon it something more will be said. For
over 12 months all the merchants of Australia have been
inconvenienced, while this syndicate haggled over dif-
ferent matters."
Now, apart from the trouble some of the tribe put
themselves to in making absurd statements and
writing absurd letters, not one merchant in Aus-
tralia has been inconvenienced by the difficulties
between the Australian Government and the
syndicate which failed. (Not that the community
would have cared very much if a whole lot of
merchants had been inconvenienced, the im-
portance of the mercantile person being largely in
his own imagination.) A certain amount of incon-
venience may have been caused to the Star’s
shareholders by the very unpleasant things their
own auditor said about their balance-sheet, but
no inconvenience has been caused to any busi-
ness man in Australia by the Federal manage-
ment of the mail contract. The mails have
travelled exactly as usual, and not one single
hitch has arisen, and the mercantile person
lias got the invoice for the shoddy precisely
in the old way. In any case, the tale
about the suffering importer having been
inconvenienced for over 12 months by a con-
tract which only began to exist about 12 months
ago is a humorless business ; and even the resolve
of a certain small snarling faction in Sydney
to find a grievance in everything the Aus-
tralian Government does or leaves undone
doesn’t justify anybody in being such a dreary
perverter. It was just like the Star, when so
many lies were going around, to get hold of the
smallest and poorest of the lot—one quite unwor-
thy a city of Sydney’s size, and hardly large
enough e ven for Byrock.
Concerning a Trust Co.
Randolph Bedford writes
This is in the matter of a weird narrative calling itself
the Tasmanian Investment and Trust Co., Ltd., whose
directors are mostly connected with a Glasgow co.
formed some years ago to settle Scotchmen on the Roper
River (Northern Territory), presumably as alligator food.
So far as known, no Scotch immigrants have arrived at
the Roper, and the co. has fallen short of its intentions ;
but it was connected with many reputable people in
Glasgow who were likely to give it a run for its money—
supposing it had any money. The Tasmanian solicitors
of the Tasmanian Investment and Trust Co. are Lewis,
Gaut, and Hudspeth. Ex-Premier Lewis is one of that
firm.
The capital of the co. is £25,000, of which, according to
the prospectus, 3500 shares have already been allotted,
and 21,500 are offered. The object is to obtain an Act of
the Tasmanian Parliament for the construction of a rail-
way from the Government terminus at Glenora to Zeehan
via Mt. Lyell and Mt. Reid, thus connecting the West
Coast with Hobart. For building the line, the co. pro-
poses that it shall get mining, agricultural, and timber
rights over 490,000 acres of land, “ the larger portion of
which is situated in the well-known mining districts of
the island ; the right to generate and supply electrical
power to all parts of the island for all purposes to the
extent of 35,000 brake horse-power, abundant water-power
from six rivers being available ; the right to construct
branch railways to Mt. Lyell, to the coalfields of Hamil-
ton, and to Lake St. Clair. The total length of the line
will be about 142 miles. ... A sum of about £50,000
is estimated to have been already expended on the
survey of the line, on earthworks, clearing, and
erecting working bridges.” The £50,000 estimated
to have been already expended was expended by that
impudent Great Western Railway syndicate, which
hawked a railway franchise all over London for three
years or more; failed to find any money to build the
line ; demanded, like the sturdy mendicant it was, ex-
tension after extension of time ; and was at last tardily
disqualified for life. Now this Tasmanian Investment
and Trust Co. has arisen to push through another
Railway Bill prior to doing some more hawking. The
co. thinks so highly of its proposal even at this stage,
when it is only seeking for enough money to get a Bill
through Parliament and pay a small deposit as evidence
of good faith, that it descants on the 490,000 acres it is
to get free of rent or charges, save only 2£ per cent, on
the net profits realised on gold and minerals mined.
The co., then, for risking a non-forfeitable deposit of
£IO,OOO is to have the right to build a railway and work it
for 25 years, when the line may be taken over by the Go-
vernment at a cost “ not exceeding the actual cost of con-
struction, with 20 per cent, added, and, in addition, the
value of all equipment, rolling stock,” etc. Should the
Government not purchase, the concession is to be renew-
able in successive terms of 21 years each. Same terms as
to determination or renewals of rights apply to the half
million acres of land ; and there are no real safeguards
proposed as to electric and water supply rights at all.
Anyhow, imagine a company in possession of electric
rights, mains, power transmission, railways, and of agri-
cultural, timber-getting and mining tenants, for 25
years; and, if the facts are anything as stated in this
precious prospectus, the co. would be as hard to shift as
if it were Rockefeller on the Board of Standard Oil.
Tasmania is to hand over the transport, lighting, power,
timber, mines, and water supply of the affected area;
the Hamilton coalfields are to be swallowed (for the man
wh'o owns a railway practically owns the land that
supplies the freight). Even the tame tourist to Lake St.
Clair is to be netted !
And what are the finances of the geniuses who propose
to swallow Tasmania from Glenora to the West Coast?
The railway is to be 142 miles in length, worth about
£SOOO a mile, or £700,000 in all. Tasmania could make
its own railway, continue to own its half million acres of
rich mineral lands (mentioned in the prospectus), harness
its own rivers, and supply Socialistic power and light to
the island, for an initial expenditure (so far as the railway
is concerned) of £700,000. By giving away all these
rights, Tasmania, if it wants to take over the railway in 25
years, may have to pay up to £840,000, or 20 per
cent, more than construction cost for the same line.
This is assuming that the T. I. and T. Co. can build a line
as cheaply as the Government. But it can’t, because
it will pay anything from 5 to 7 per cent, for its money,
whereas the State, if it must continue to borrow,
can borrow at 3f per cent. Then, while the co. holds
the line, higher fares and freights will have to be
exacted, not only to pay for the dear money actually ex-
pended in culverts and rails and sleepers, but also for the
dead capital which represents the profits of the carpet-
baggers and hawkers of railway franchises. And this
syndicate with the little capital is a whale for profits in
futiiro. Says the prospectus: “The proposed railway
co. will have a nominal capital of £150,000 in ordinary
shares of; £1 each, and power to malic an issue of
£850,000 five per cent, debentures.” That is to say, its
mortgage power is to be nearly six times its subscribed
capital! But this capital isn’t to be subscribed. It’s to
be all promoters’ capital. That is to say, the franchise
syndicate’s capital ; for there is no plunder reserved for
the people who are to take up the £550,000 worth of de-
bentures. “ The R.R. Syndicate, Ltd.”(which may mean
Roper River Syndicate), “in consideration of services
rendered and to be rendered to the Tas. Investment and
Trust Co., in connection with the obtaining of the
Railway Act, is to receive £25,000 in debentures,
and £50,000 in ordinary shares.” The T. I. and
T. Co. will pay this out of the £850,000 of de-
bentures and £150,000 in ordinary shares, receivable by
it from the Railway Co., “plus a sum equal to the
amount of the deposit furnished and expenses incurred
by this company.” But so that the sharks may bite
bigger if the chance offers, the prospectus goes on :
“The above proposed arrangements as to capital, and as
to the consideration aforesaid, are, however, subject to
complete revision, should circumstances require.”
And circumstances will be certain to require revision
in the direction of more plunder to promoters in cash
and ordinary shares, and that will put up the interest on
the debenture issue.
It’s all beautiful from the view point of the T. I. and
T. Co., and it’s all madness from the viewpoint of the
Tasmanian citizen. The atmosphere of the Common-
wealth is almost always fatal to privately-owned lines—
Midland of W.A., Great Southern of W.A., and Midland of
Maoriland are cases in point—because even a principality
cannot stand high interest rates and unlimited pro-
moters’ profits; and the agricultural or mining tenant
cannot exist under the freight rates made necessary
by the high interest and promoters’ profits afore-
said. The syndicate endeavors to trade on the
Southern jealousies of the island, and holdsout the bait of
the centralisation of West Coast traffic at Hobart instead
of Emu Bay (where one struggling private railway now
fights for its life) ; but the freights must be so high to
meet the capitalisation that this centralisation hope is
vain.
The Tasmanian Government that listens to any pro-
posals for reviving the old, impudent foolishness of the
Great Western Railway Syndicate calls for immediate
destruction.
When Alexander Fraser, the “Big Hole”
mine swindler, was brought to trial at the Quarter
Sessions (Sydney) recently, he pleaded guilty to
two charges of obtaining £290 and £IOO by means
of false pretences from two simple-minded
citizens, and was remanded for sentence. When
he appeared to get his “stretch,”
Detective Gallagher stated that he had ascertained
that Fraser had by the “ Big Hole ” swindle obtained
between £BOOO and £IO,OOO. It could be proved that he
cashed cheques himself at business places in the city.
The accused spent money freely, giving dinners to his
friends and acquaintances, and so made himself popular.
He had a wife and 10 children.
The shocked Judge Murray thereupon sentenced
him to five years’ penal servitude. Probably
Fraser deserved all he got —speaking from per-
sonal knowledge the writer thinks he deserved
even more—but that is not the point, The
question is, what did Fraser get five years for?
Was it for the proved offence of swindling two
citizens out of £390 altogether, or because Detec-
tive Gallagher had “ ascertained ” that he had
obtained between £BOOO and £IO,OOO, which
matters were not proved? From the tenor of
Judge Murray’s remarks the heavy sentence was
passed on account of what Detective Gallagher
had “ascertained,” and there are very grave
objections to this brand of justice. The prisoner
had no chance of rebutting anything that Detec-
tive Gallagher had “ascertained,” and although
the detective may be a most estimable officer, aiid
quite incapable of making any mistakes, or ascer-
taining anything but the solid truth, some of his
successors may not have his genius. It is obvious,
if a man is to be sentenced on what the detective
“ ascertains,” that when the brilliant Gallagher
has passed away some awful miscarriages of justice
may occur—-in fact, are almost sure to occur.
Therefore, the system had better be abolished
while Gallagher is still running strong, and
before justice is brought into contempt.

There was a farmers’ convention at Echuca (Vie.)
a few days ago, at which some 180 delegates were
present, and the report of the proceedings fur-
nishes interesting reading. While the convention
was sitting, a wild fight was raging around it for
the representation of Echuca in the House of
Reps., and the Argus was calling upon all the
farmers to vote as one man for Palmer, the Anti-
Sosh candidate. The convention itself was over-
whelmingly Anti-Sosh. Yet these are some of
the things that were done :
Mr. Gooch (Kyneton) said that the Government should
grant wire-netting to farmers on extended terms of pay-
ment; for wire-netting was the only remedy against the
rabbits.
A resolution was carried : “ That Dr. Danysz be given
a reasonable time to continue his experiments ; and that,
failing'success, the Australian Government be asked to
offer a valuable prize for the discovery of an efficient
remedy for the rabbit pest.”
It was decided to urge the Government to increase the
proposed bonus for the improvement in the breed of
horses from £3OOO to £9OOO.
Mr. R. G. Fincham pleaded earnestly for the construc-
tion of good roads in the forest districts. If the State
spent £250,000, in the long run it would be fully recouped.
The key-note of the convention was struck
during the discussion on the horse-improvement
bonus:—
Mr. Cameron : It is a question whether we should
“ snavel ” the £3OOO or not.
Mr. MacDonald : Snavel it, of course. (Laughter.)
The Bulletin doesn’t object to the farming in-
dustries receiving fair Government assistance,
but what it does object to is the gross hypocrisy
of the farmer who, while screaming (and voting)
Anti-Sosh with all his might, at the same time
demands Socialism for himself with both hands
open and tears in his eyes. At the Echuca con-
vention two-thirds of the resolutions carried asked
for both Government assistance and Govern-
ment “interference,” and the asking was naked
and unashamed. At least, there was only one
man that appeared to be at all uneasy. This was
Senator M‘Ooll, who said
He took exception to the word Socialistic as applied
to farmers, for Paternalism was not Socialism.
It would be interesting to have Senator M‘Coll’s
definition of Paternalism.
Dear Bulletin, —A large number of Pastoral-
ists met father last week to see a demonstration of the
Tyree Spray for killing Prickly Pear and Lantana. A
patch growing at the Paddington Tip was sprayed with
Tyree No. 14 Weed Exterminating Mixture. My word !
the result fairly paralysed them. The Prickly Pear was
dead in less than 8 days, and the Lantana in less than 5
days. Father showed those present that land can easily
be cleared of any pest, such as Prickly Pear, Lantana,
Blackberry, Gorse, Blackthorn, Poison Plant, etc., at a
cost of about ss. per acre. The Pastoralists reckoned
that the demonstration would do them a great deal more
good than the Sheep Show. They reckon that the Gov-
ernment and every newspaper should publish the informa-
tion broadcast, free of charge, as it was a matter of
national importance. One gentleman wanted to know if
the stuff would poison stock. Father said that if the
directions were followed, there would not be enough
poison on any one plant to do much harm ; but, it would
be better to keep the stock away from the ground until
the stuff was burnt off. Father says the Factory is still
going night and day, and if you want any information
write to Tyree Limited, 314 George-street, Sydney;
Tyree Automatic Spray Offices, Block-place, Mel-
bourne ; Tyree Automatic Spray Offices, 26 Adelaide-
street, Brisbane ; Tyree Automatic Spray Offices, Way-
mouth street, Adelaide. Yours, Bill Williams.*

♦
The first production of Wagner’s “ Val-
kyrie ” in Sydney (at the Royal) was a humorous
circumstance. That particular portion of the
daily press which has its musical columns written
by people with Souls, affected to regard it as an
epoch-marking event. Time should be counted
back and forward from this tremendous event. It
was to blaze, an everlasting beacon or steeple or
trig-station, to indicate at which exact portion of
our history we emerged from the musical heathen-
dom of the Italian and English opera worship.
It was a new revelation, a second birth, and a
sudden death of old ideals all rolled into one.
* • * *
Being merely Part 11. of “Der Ring des
Nibelungen” series, “The Valkyrie” asastory is
somewhat incomplete and unsatisfying. It begins
with the arrival, in a somewhat damaged state, of
Siegmund Studemund at the house of Hunding
Greder. Hunding is out with his relatives hunt-
ing this very Siegmund, but his wife, Sieglinde
Anderson, is at home, and gives the fugitive
’ mead. At the first glance there are signs of their
falling in love, Hunding being anything but an
ideal husband. The fact that the two are brother
and sister does not count. Being the children of
the god Wotan Mohwinkel, by a mortal mother
who does not appear on the stage, having been
murdered some years before, they are not subject
to the ordinary restrictions of consanguinity.
Moreover, they do not know each other. When
Hunding the Bad Egg returns home he is
annoyed, and promises Siegmund he shall certainly
die in the morning. Mrs. Hunding drugs her
husband, and “carries on” with Siegmund in a
disgraceful manner, showing him a magic sword
placed in an ash tree by her parent god for the
use of a hero who will one day rescue her from
the tyranny of her husband. Having extracted
the sword, which is named Needful, the lovers
elope. The second scene shows the god Wotan
having a bad quarter of an hour with his wife
Fricka, the goddess of marriage. She is not
shocked at the thought of brother and sister
eloping together to commence a life of sin. It is
the outrage on the sanctity of the marriage tie
that worries her. Fricka seems to have been the
foundress of the great Deform party. In order to
escape the terrors of her tongue, Wotan promises
that when Hunding and Siegmund meet, the
magical powers of the sword Needful shall
be suspended, and the ruffian who disre-
garded the sanctity of the marriage tie
shall be killed. The Valkyrie Brunnhilde,
however, though ordered by Wotan to see that
Siegmund is slain, disobeys orders and proceeds
to help him against Hunding, but Wotan inter-
feres, and Siegmund is really killed. Brunnhilde
then protects Sieglinde, in spite of Wotan,
and while Sieglinde flees to save the babe
that shall in due time be born, the disobedient
Valkyrie Brunnhilde stays to face the music of
Wotan’s anger. Wotan deprives her of her stiddy
job as a Valkyrie, casts her into a sleep of years,
and leaves her surrounded by magical fires, through
which only a hero of heroes shall win his way to
wed Brunnhilde. This hero, of course, is to be the
unborn child of Siegmund and Sieglinde, whom
Brunnhilde has ordered to be named Siegfried.
MISS LILIAN ORMOND, the chorus girl who
suddenly became a capable leading lady in
“ The Flying Dutchman ,” at Sydney Royal.
Writer beheld the first act with mixed feelings.
The faces of the audience wore the correct rapt
expression, but in spite of the rolling harmony
and the soulful expressions all around, there was
a dark suspicion that had the same people seen
the same characters in a similar get-up at the
Tivoli they would have howled with mirth. Sieg-
mund Studemund is a fat man. He wore a fear-
some shock of fair hair right down over his fore-
head. His unkempt beard stuck out like that of
a navvy, and his ponderous middle bulk was ex-
aggerated by a rabbit-skin rug tied round him
with straps or thongs. His legs were represented
as being mostly bare, and altogether he looked
like a large house on very insecure piles. Moreover,
the effect was made more ludicrous by the way he
worked his arms. They reminded one of a sema-
phore on the drunk. The whole of this company
seems to be worried with arms, and perpetually
perplexed as to what to do with them. No doubt it
is German high art. Altogether, Siegmund sug-
gested a somewhat bleached chimpanzee. Hun-
ding wore a huge bearskin rug that indicated his
great wealth. Facially he was got up something
like Ingomar, only much worse, and was an ideal
representation of an aristocratic wild man from
Borneo. But he was aristocratic, and very fearful
togaze upon. Wotan was resplendent in mail of
silver and headgear of gold and gems, while the
Valkyries were charmingly arrayed. Fashions in
Valhalla were at least picturesque.
* * m m
The music of “ The Valkyrie,” orchestral
and vocal, is all harmony, without the faintest
suggestion of melody, as we get it in English,
French and Italian opera. For the proper
rendering of the Nibelungen music the full band
of 13 wind instruments requires to be balanced by
16 first violins, 16 seconds, 12 violas, 12 ’cellos,
eight contra-basses, and six harps—a total of 83.
Conductor Slapoffski did not have that number—
which means that he had to cut the score
considerably, and, whether he damaged it
in the cutting or not, there are few people
in Sydney who can say. Anyhow, the
orchestra made a magnificent rovv—a row that
grew upon one till the weird succession of crashes,.
rolls, and lonely wandering notes threatened to
become a vice. In the vocal parts, Mohwinkel,
who had about the hardest part, acquitted himself
very well. There is some richness in his voice.
Poor Studemund’s dreadful get up as Siegmund
was a heavy handicap, and his tenor was not suffi-
ciently glorious to remove the effect, so he is
turned down. The heavy part of Sieglinde was
taken by Miss Anderson, and at the end of the
fearsome task the voice showed no signs of fray-
ing. Greder did not get much show as Hunding,
but made the very best of it, and Miss Johanna
Heinze, as the Valkyrie Brunnhilde, was very
charming.
** * *
On Saturday night, the Grand Opera Co.
took suddenly to comedy. Miss Heinze not
having arrived to take the part of Brunnhilde,
“The Valkyrie” performance came to an abrupt
conclusion with the termination of the first act.
Musgrove held a hurried conference with his com-
pany, and, after listening to many suggestions,
rejected them all with the announcement that he
would play “The Flying Dutchman.” Miss
Anderson objected, on the ground that it would
put her voice to too great a strain, but finally
decided that she would sing if she were paid as
for two operas. This, apparently, Musgrove
refused to do, and Miss Anderson left the theatre.
Musgrove found himself in the ditch. He had
already changed the programme in the middle of
the performance, and it looked as though
it would have to be again altered. Then
he rushed round wildly for someone in the
chorus who could sing at sight, and found Miss
Lilian Ormond—who had been trained in Mel-
bourne, her native town, by Armes Beaumont
and Mrs. Frederika Mitchell—ready to make the
attempt. She scored a success, though she had
to struggle along with the book in her hand, and
sang excellently in the part of Senta, and the
audience cheered her, while the enthusiastic
chorus carried her off the stage in a frenzy of de-
light. The new singer is in private life Mrs.
Gibb, and her first stage experience was with this
company, which she joined in Melbourne. But,
on the other hand, she has been singing on con-
cert platforms ever since she was 11, and it is safe
to say she is twice that age now. In the mean-
time, it is understood that Miss Sara Ander-
son’s name is, as far as Musgrove is concerned,
Mud.
** * &
Familiarity—not too much familiarity —
softens the heart a little towards some of the
ditties in “The Spring Chicken,” and “Oh, so
Gently,” “ Rosalie,” and “ I Don’t Know, but I
Guess,” are now among the things that no well-
brought-up suburban piano is unacquainted with.
Meantime, it is obvious from the crowded condi-
tion of Her Majesty’s that Sydney must have a
winter season of comic opera, and if it can’t
always have a “ Veronique,” it is still prepared
to feel happy with something a good deal less
satisfactory from the musical point of view.
** * *
The tableaux at Sydney Palace Theatre
are drawing big crowds. On the one hand an
astutely impartial management presents Allan
Water; on the other hand, the Killarney Lakes.
Any reference to so much as a dampness in the
famous Boyne River district is tactfully eschewed.
Wherefore all is peace and harmony, even during
this troublous month of July.
*' * * *
“ The Squatter’s Daughter ” is in its
eighth week at Sydney Criterion, and instead of
age having dimmed the brightness of the receipts,
the management feels called upon to start a series
of Wednesday matinees in order to meet the con-
tinuous demand for home-made drama. The star
of William Anderson may set some day—even the
sun of Austerlitz did that ultimately—but mean-
while there are no flies on William’s star, and it
keeps its wicket up manfully. And if in the end
it springs a leak and the referee counts it out, it
will still be remembered that it rode a good race,
and was always on deck when it was wanted.
** * *
Leslie Harris, monologist, proceeds upon
his merry course at Sydney Town Hall. A
good move was the billing of his colleague,
Marshall Crosby, on Saturday night, to sing
classic solos, such as the Toreador song from
“ Carmen.” Lovers of grand opera in this city
are more and more frequenting monologue shows
as the surest means of hearing the music they
admire. On the other hand, to balance matters
as it were, those who like monologues are going
in vast numbers these days to the Royal. There
was a monologue there on Saturday, when George
Musgrove explained why “The Valkyrie”
couldn’t go beyond Act I.

The Svengalis have brought their bag of
tricks Over from Melbourne, and the Tivoli is
open-mouthed with wonder. Probably it isn’t so
prodigiously mystifying an exhibition as that of
the circus-performing Howard Brothers, but
there are more frills about it. Ernest Fitts, about
the most popular musical-hall singer of the legiti-
mate sort in Australia, adds his vigor to the im-
proved bill that was favorably mentioned last
week; and it has to be a very good bill indeed
that isn’t the better for getting Fitts.

cuperation, the Melbourne popularity of Andrew
Mack has a healthy glow on it. Mack is an
Irish master of the ornate method in melodrama—
a decorative actor with a falsetto singing vqice—a

—a floridly natural exponent of all the shining
virtues. Non-shining virtues, such as thrift and
severe truthfulness, don’t come his way in the
world of stage fiction. The attractions of Mack
and the characters he plays are pretty much on
the surface, so they catch the eye—the eye physical
and mental—as easily as his voice catches
the ear. Take him for all in all, he’s
the brightest and most up-to-date Irish comedian
that Australia knows of, and “ Tom Moore” pro-
vides him with a chance to achieve a great victory
over the audience in the fifty-second round of
applause. In fact, Mack gives the audience a
particularly hard knock at the end of the third
act, where he slaps his glove across the gilded
villain’s mouth, and brings the curtain down upon
his wrath. Personally, he wouldn’t pass a critical
examination for his likeness to the original
Tom Moore, but he approaches about as near to the
poet as the drama gets to the story of Moore’s life,
and when he acts in open defiance of history he has
a way wid him which no warm-hearted historian
could resist. Apart from the predominant Mack,
the present co. includes a few members of the
previous co., juvenile and adult—notably Miss
Annie Berlein, as the landlady who is deeply
affected by her lodger’s mellifluous warble, but
remains steadfast concerning her rint. Miss Jane
Kelton is a forceful, but not a very youthful,
Bessie Dyck ; the comic servant part falls to Fred
Cam bourne, and the Prince Regent takes the form
of Geo. Carey, disguised in a pugnacious-looking
black wig. Carey has so many good performances
to his credit that he can afford to be told of his
awful badness on this occasion. He plays the
bloated waster who was afterwards George
the Fourth as though he (Carey) had never
studied the deportment of W. S. Gilbert’s
dukes and earls in “lolanthe.” His want
of mannah and lack of tonnage make him a pain-
ful matter for comparison. The previous Worst
Gentleman in Europe had enough imperial
majesty and royal condescension for six monarchs.
The art of kinging was evidently born in him.
He seemed so keenly conscious of the difference
between the Lord’s Anointed and the other people
on the stage, that when he spoke of “impertinence”
one felt afraid of his dying suddenly from a rush
of blue blood to the brain. A thrill of loyalty
shook the house every time he graciously spoke.
The overwhelming Regent George of two years
ago is an ineffaceable memory, whereas Carey—
well, of course, Carey could never be like him, but
he might try to be as like him as he’s able to be.
The Andrew Mack season, at Her Majesty’s, is a
short one, and if the advt. keeps its word “The
Way to Kenmare” will follow “ Tom Moore” on
Saturday.

At the Princess’s “The Blue Moon” was
somewhat clouded one or two nights last week by
the substitution of Miss Dulcie Murphy for Miss
Wallace in the soubrette character. Miss M.
imitated the other new lady with exactness up to
a eertain point; but she tended to weaken the
performance, which was never too strong in some
of its joints. There’s a young man who is sup-
posed to impersonate a brilliant English jour-
nalist and “critic,” one of those awf’ly clever
johnnies who produce articles and things, and are
sent to roam far-off lands in search of “copy.”
The imported young man would be a somewhat
raw joint in any character, therefore his appear-
ance as a critic suggests that he ought to write
his own theatrical notice and make it rough. All
the same, “The Blue Moon” is equal to the
chronic demand for a lively musical show’, and a
contented crowd of late-comers stood three deep
at the back of the d.c. last Saturday night. The
little comedian who sings “Won’t you make a
fuss of me?” has a sharp edge on his humor, so it
seems likely that Melbourne will make a fuss of
him.
*
At the Atheneeum Hall, the reappearance
of West’s Pictures and the Brescians is in keep-
ing with the surroundings, which have acquired
an air of newness since last year. The present
lot of Pictures are new to Melbourne, the lighting
is brighter even than of yore, and there are many
moments when the inevitable flicker is conspicu-
ous by its absence. Also, the Brescians give an
improved musical entertainment. They w'ere
pretty good in the first instance, and, under the
steady tuition of Father Time, they have become
pretty better—especially the instrumentalists.

The Educated Cats arrived at the Opera
House on Saturday, and during the next few weeks
every Melbourne woman who adores her tom,
or dotes upon her tabby, will want to see the do-
mestic animals that walk in the way of Wirth’s
favorite tiger, and are quiteunusual—forCats. The
solo juggler and the juggling duettists are throw-
ing things about and catching them as usual;
also the outlook is gladdened somewhat by The
Rosebuds, two of the buds being Florrie and
Stella Ranger. Amiel, the amazing contortionist,
will pack himself into a hat-box and take himself
to another city after to-night (Thursday).

“ 0.K.” from Melbourne :
The amusing Dan Barry has at last exceeded the limit
of his self-granted license for audacious showmanship.
Dan’s up-country productionsof hair-raising melodramas,
written by the “Archbishop of Canterbury,” “Charles
Stewart Parnell,” “ W. E. Gladstone,” or anybody else
whose name had a taking appearance on the bill, were
carelessly accepted as harmless jokes, except by an
occasional visiting foreigner, who quoted them in sup-
port of his statements re Australian barbarism in the
backblocks. At one time or other every notable name in
British public affairs has figured as the author of
a Barrydrama, and if perchance the particulars of
Daniel’s latest “spoof” came to town it was nobody’s
business to protest against a policy of extravagant
humbug which, in a measure, invited contempt for Aus-
tralia’s rural population. Of late years the genial Dan
has abandoned gory melodrama in favor of the cinemato-
graph, but his latest show was a combination—a bio-
drama, on the lines of theTaits’ “Kelly Gang.” And he
ran it around the suburbs of Melbourne under the eyes of
the very people who had a personal right to denounce it
as an outrage. Dan’s indiscretion in Melbourne was a
Moving Picture of the recent diamond robbery story,
with the murder of a well-known citizen for the sensation
scene. The advt. bill was a remarkable illustration of a
hard-shelled showman’s belief in his right to turn an
honest penny by any means he can think of
“ The Bauer Murder and Daylight Diamond Robbery.
Produced with Life-like attention to detail, and showing
how some Monster Murderer, some Modern Cain, was
ready to kill his kind in Cold Blood for the Guilty Greed
of Gain.”
This up-to-date Horror, with its “life-like” presenta-
tion of the murdered man, and its fancy portrait of the
alleged murderer, was flickered to crowded houses at
Richmond Town Hall and elsewhere, but when it was
about to be shown at St. Kilda, where poor Bauer had
many friends, Chief Sec. Peacock was called upon to veto
further representations of the unwholesome fake.
“ Mother Goose ” lias been doing her best
to comply with the frenzied popular demand for
her at Adelaide Royal. In her farewell week she
established a local record of three matinees in
four days. Mrs. Carreno has been rather
“ lost in the crush at any rate, her Town Hall
concerts have had audiences that were moderately
good, but not worthy of her almost virile power.
Carden Wilson is giving his imitations at the
Tivoli, where there is also a young juggler named
Sturm, who ought to be heard of in the future.
The Royal is now settling down t'o the. familiar
humor (at reduced prices) of “Fun on the Bristol.”
AT POVERTY POINT.
“L.E.”: Emil Amiel, the phenome-
nal “ bender ” or contortionist at Mel-
bourne Opera House, supplies a turn,
the biggest turn ever turned, that is
far from pretty, but is supposed to
have some scientific value. When a
man twists round his own axis like a
drunk round a post, and dangles parts
of his frame at eccentric angles, and
shoots out his phiz at all kinds of un-
expected places, the more delicate part
of the audience turns aside and gazes,
as in a beer-glass, darkly. This bend
gives it a bad turn. But the anatomist
says there is no danger. Emil’s carti-
lages have been stretched or some-
thing, so that his joints turn freely,
like an importer’s invoice. No organ
is affected. Indeed, the man of
science reckons a race of Emils would
be a graceful and elegant race, full of
lovely curves and parabolas, much
superior ,to the stiff, stodgy George
Reid type of to-day. He recommends
that man’s cartilages should be
stretched when young, when he is
getting cow-lymph squirted into him,
or Bible instruction at Sunday-school.
Then man might be able to soap his
back without twisting his neck, or
climb into a white shirt without
blasphemy. That would be something.
And without shifting his legs he could whip round
suddenly and catch his wife in the act of making a face
at him behind his back.
“ Bill Outback ” : A few notes re the German opera
co. Some of the sausages—singers I mean—are distress-
ingly fat. One tenor wants a concave partner for love-
making scenes. His little waddle is as giddy as Albani’s
wee run on and off the concert platform. The acting in
“The Flying Dutchman” is about as thrilling as the
efforts of the sand and saltbush amatoors in Mean and
Miserable Menindie. Hands up ! Hands down ! One
hand up ! One hand down! Scratch the head! Turn
round twice ! Give a few gasps ! More hands up 1 Hands
down ! Why, I’ve seen better acting by a band of un-
trained brolgas in the plains, past Hungrytown on the
Paroo, on the road to Skeletonville. The birds have some
notion of grace and stateliness. But, of course, brolgas
don’t run to bow-windows, even in middle age ; and so-
wed, anyhow, no more German opera for me this trip.
“Nane”: Pity the sorrows of a German opera im-
presario. Musgrove is on a bad wicket. “ Indisposition ”
is rampant. It is the sickest opera co. with which we
have yet been afflicted. Every other day there are
changes of the cast, sometimes of the opera, and some-
times for a change there is no opera at all. A disgusted
galleryite, coming away from the matinde that
failed' on Friday last, explained the situation. “One’s
sick,” he said, “and the other’s got the needle.”
The whole concern is as uncertain as a pony meeting.
The legend goes that Musgrove, going into the orchestra
room one night shortly before the rising of the curtain,
was asked by Slapoffski, “ Well, what’s the opera for to-
night?” “Don’t know yet,” replied M.; “I have heard
some rumors, but I know nothing for certain.” Matters
culminated on Saturday in a scandal. Musgrove merits
sympathy. He, at least, has introduced one ennobling
feature, which will not die—the four trumpeters who
blow a fanfar j , in herald raiment, from the balcony.
Nothing more beautiful has been seen out of a waxworks.
I am told the fanfare didn’t come off on Saturday, but
that was nothing. Things were so chaotic on Saturday
that I wouldn’t have been surprised if the theatre hadn’t
come off.
“Mel” : I was present in the old Opera House, at Mel-
bourne, in the late ’seventies when Miss Sharp, who
became the wife of the late J. A. Delany, was brought out
as an operatic prima donna by William Saurin Lyster.
Lyster was always ready to give local talent a show, but
he wasn’t always lucky with his talent. Miss Sharp
appeared for one night only as Arline in “ The Bohemian
Girl.” She had been well known at suburban concerts
and those dreadful things called “ Penny Readings,” and
had some ambition, but her gifts didn’t justify it. She
died long since. I cannot remember her Christian name.
She and Delany were not married many years, and it was
said the marriage was not a felicitous one.
“J.A.0.”: “Faust,” by the Musgrove co., was given
in German, with the exception of the choruses, which
were rendered in the approved gibberish or shibboleth
which choristers generally affect. German is a strong,
vigorous language, admirably adapted to abusing objec-
tionable people, such as men with bills or people who play
on the mouth-organ, but it does not lend itself to l.vric
opera. Possibly as a consequence of the unmusical
language, coupled with the not-too-suave voices of the
executants, the opera was not conspicuously enchanting.
One redeeming feature, however, there was —Miss Mia
Barckow’s Marguerite. She is dainty in appearance, and
there is an ingenuousness in her acting, entirely in keep-
ing with the character. Moreover, her voice, though
light and not always strictly in tune, is even and sym-
pathetic, and she has a style which makes me strongly
surmise that she did not receive her vocal instruction in
Germany. (I do not mean to say that she is as good as
we have had—her trill is certainly' not that of the night-
ingale.) There were the usual changes in the advertised
cast. Musgrove’s company is a surprise packet—you never
know whom you are going to hear until you get there.
Julius Riinger (Mephistopheles) has a fairly good sort of
voice for rough work—somewhat woody, yet occasionally
effective. He made some good points, and there was not
much the matter with his acting. Taken all through,
the acting of the company is much better than its sing-
ing. Oeser was not a convincing Faust. He did not
warble the music ; he wobbled it. Throughout the opera
his voice shook like a William-street tram ear. Said
voice is of limited extent in the upper register, and a
weak falsetto is a poor substitute for the ringing head
notes we have heard from other singers who have visited
us. Rupp was not an altogether bad Valentine. The
others were of the con;.run or garden variety'. They
did not do much good, and they did not do much harm.
A peculiarity of the purely German voice is that it is all
the colors of the rainbow —there is no homogeneity' about
it. The timbre changes with nearly every phrase—it may
be agreeable one moment and disagreeable the next.
The language is mainly responsible for this—language
has more to do with voice-production than most people
credit. A language rich in vowel sounds is gn aid to the
voice. When one has to sing, for instance, “Verflucht”
instead of “Maledieo” it requires a strenuous effort.
Musgrove gives us a part of the scene on the Brocken—the
Walpurgis Night or Witches’ Sabbath. Unless lam very'
far mistaken, it did not exist in the original score. When
“ Faust ” was offered to the then Imperial Opera House,
Paris, the sapient direction of that theatre incontinently
refused it. It was then, for the first time, produced at
the Theatre Lyrique, on March 19, 1859, under the
management of Oarvalho. Miolan-Carvalho, wife of the
impresario, was the first, and about the best, Marguerite,
and I remember, some 30 years ago, that there was a
tradition among French singers that the first Mephisto,
Balanquet, had not been surpassed. It was afterwards,
when “ Faust” was transferred to the Grand Opera, that
the Brocken act was introduced as a vehicle for a ballet.
“ G.S.K.” (Broken Hill): Telltit Brune is playing here
at present. At her hotel the other morning she sent for
the manager and asked that the men should be stopped
from whistling in the bath in the morning, as it disturbed
her rest. This is nearly equal to what happened at
Fremantle. The co. went across in the Kanowna. That
noble vessel berthed at Fremantle, and was discharging
cargo with the usual clatter, when Matilda Brown sent
word up to the captain to stop the row. The captain
didn’t stop it. As a result, the manager had to crawl out
in the rain at 2 a.m. to seek lodgings for the spoilt Brune.
Three weeks have elapsed since the Victorian Board of
Health refused proprietor Wilson permission to open his
remodelled Bijou Theatre ; y'et the house was opened,
and has been practically throwing mud at the Board ever
since. If that body has power to enforce its require-
ments, why doesn’t it do so? Or if it has not got the
power, why doesn’t it state its needs at once and announce
that it is going to ask Parliament for the necessary autho-
rity ? If the public gets it into its head that it can treat
the Board of Health with contempt, there will be trouble
for Melbourne, and the trouble will probably end at the
cemetery for some people.
THE 59-YEAR-OLD ELLEN TERRY AND
HER YOUTHFUL HUSBAND.
The name of Luscombe Searelle—familiar to Australia
some 20 years ago—is just now associated with a “ semi-
religious ” play which came to light in New York awhile
back, and will he staged anon at the London Lyceum.
The drama, “ Mizpah,” was written by Ella Wheeler Wil-
cox, and Searelle’s contribution is the incidental music.
“Mizpah” presumably is religious in the Parsifalian
sense of the word, although a plot borrowed from “ The
Book of Esther ” can hardly be so giddy as the parson’s
story of Kundry and her amours. Still, there are hopes
and possibilities about Ahasuerus’ feast, which was a
seven days’drunk “and the drinking was according to
law” (Heaven only knows what the Persian drinking
laws were like, but there must have been jim-jams in
those days); and which ended by the King sending seven
men to haul in the Queen and exhibit her to tile people.
Anyhow, the music by the composer of Estrella (Nellie
Stewart “ created ” the name part in Sydney) is not likely
to have a deep strain of piety in it.

ous blast of appreciation the very small subject will now

Whilst Wirth’s Circus was travelling by boat from
Rockhampton to Townsville, one of the engineers aboard
had a mind to be funny at the expense of an elephant.
Probably he didn’t know that elephants have a deep dis-
like and a darned long memory for practical jokes. Any-
how, he removed some of the inside from an orange,
loaded the gap with cayenne pepper, and bowled the
tempting fruit down to the “ herd” of Wirth’s weightiest
animals. They all reached for it, but Toby Elephant’s
trunk was longest, and Toby got it. In an ordinary way
the beast might have swallowed the orange whole, but he
happened to give it one crunch, and then the ag ny
began. Toby, bellowing with pain, tugged at his leg-
chains in the attempt to get to the water tank. His
brothers in captivity trumpeted like mad things, where-
upon the lions, tigers, etc., in 25 cages roared and
shrieked a chorus. There was a panic among the passen-
gers, and nobody knew how the tumult had originated.
Or, rather, two people knew—the culprit and the ele-
phant ; and two weeks later Toby divulged the secret in
a practical way. The circus outfit was returning to
Brisbane by the same boat, when the orange man passed
within Toby’s sphere of action. In an instant 1 he elephant
had the man in the coil of his trunk, and v. old have
hurled him overboard but for the intervening rigging.
As it. was, the keepers came along before the elephant
could do its worst, and the man, thankful for having
escaped with a severe shaking, owned up to the orange
outrage.
“L.E. Why does Australia hang Irish harps round
Andrew Mack, the Irish (American) comedian ? Why
does a patriotic gallery drop sham rocks on him because
he warbles “ The Last Rose of Summer ” in a silvery
falsetto? His Erinism is as false as his voice. His hero,
Tom Moore, cuts a poor figure in English or Irish or any
literature. He is a cheap Hibernian bard who has been
grossly over-rated by unpoetical patriots. In his own day
he was completely overshadowed by men like Shelley,
Byron, Keats, Coleridge and Wordsworth. The modern
Irish writers, headed by Yeats, reckon him very poor
stuff. Half-a-dozen bards in Australia have written
better verses than those of Tom Moore. Victor Daley
could leave him in the dim distance, but nobody
worries too much about Daley. The real national
bard of Ireland is James Clarence Mangan, who
wrote “Dark Rosaleen,” one of the finest patriotic
poems ever penned, and an infinitely greater man than
the futile Moore. Mangan takes his place in good middle-
weight company, while Tom is only a moderate per-
former among the feathers. And, as a hero, Moore is
ridiculous. He was a toady to English Royalty, and a
lacquey of British duchesses, his most aggressive per-
formance being his rendering of some good old Irish
songs sentimentalised and sugared for English drawing-
rooms. It’s time Australia took a pull at itself and
spread its jam with more discretion. If, for instance,
Albert Dorrington wrote a play (I take it for granted it
would be vastly superior to this American piffle), Aus-
tralia wouldn’t throw wreaths of gum trees and necklaces
of native bears at Him ; it would remain apathetic at the
performance. Also, there wouldn’t be any performance.
“ David H. Richards ” : I struck a new Sydney show.
An enterprising individual had announced a series of
lectures on “ The Physiology of Digestion,” and cordially-
invited the public to come and learn how the stomach,
that didn’t amount to more than a small pair, might
successfully call down the proud lobster, even if that
beast held four aces. Some of him (and her) went, and
were regaled with platitudes about the alimentary canal,
what time a female handed round flaring red handbills.
These proved to consist of various letters of gratitude
from persons who had been cured by the lecturer. One ■
person said his little hopeful, thanks to Mr. Lecturer’s
“ marvellous treatment,” was now able to walk without
iron boots. A she-person certified that her daughter
was now “ able to walk without iron boots,” and another
pathetic epistle announced that the writer no longer
suffered from rheumatism, dyspepsia, dropsy, etc. They
ail said it was due to the spouter’s “ wonderful treat-
ment.” A lot of the audience didn’t stop long, still they
got the handbill about the needlessness of wearing iron
boots. I had no idea there were so many iro n boots in
the Commonwealth.
Evidently the lackadaisical style of acting and the list-
less saunter to which Miss Olive Morell introduced us, is
a popular feature in Cockney' comic opera of late. Miss
Maude Thorne, of Williamson’s new musical comedy co.,
has precisely' the same methods and mannerisms, and the
inference is that both are imitating a greater warbler
with a superior listlessness and an even more convincing
I’d-rather-do-this-in-bed air about her. The I’d-rather-
do-this-in-bed air is considered “dooeed” good form in
London, but as yet Australia is not sufficiently' tired to
appreciate it.

The prices fixed for the “ gala ” opera night at Covent
Garden on June 11 are worth remarking. Boxes—of
which the house contains tier upon tier, running well
round —varied from 50 guineas to a minimum of 15. All
single seats were entitled “stalls” for the occasion.
The best cost eight guineas, but an “amphitheatre
stall ” (which is a book ed seat in the gallery) could be had
for a mere 21s.
“ The Mikado ” was no t the first of W. S. Gilbert’s plays
to be banned by squeamish officialdom. The earlier ex-
tinguisher was put upon “The Happy Land,” a political
burlesque which W.S.G. (“F. Tomline”) and G. A.
A’Beckett founded on Gilbert’s own “ Wicked World.”
The main cause of offence was the introduction of charac-
ters easily recognisable as Gladstone, Lowe, Ayrton, and
other leading statesmen of the period. The play' was
blocked yet again when it appeared in Melbourne in a
painfully localised version ( not by Gilbert), with Graham
Berry and his colleagues substituted for the English poli-
ticians. That was the historic occasion upon which
Tommy' Bent was either on the managerial staff or selling
peanuts between the acts—nobody seems to be sure which
it was. After “ The Happy Land ” had been bumped out
in London, Gilbert, still as “Tomline,” produced “The
Realm of Joy',” a skit on the prohibition, which was
allowed to pass.
One Distin-Maddick, manager of the Scala Theatre in
London (the old Prince of Wales), announceshis intention
of leading the way in a reduction of prices, although his
newly-decorated house is to be “the handsomest and
most sumptuous theatre in the world.” Maddick’s.
highest-priced seats, the stalls, are to be only 7/- each,
and he hopes to see the lead followed. It was at the
Prince of Wales’, about 30 years back, that the half-
guinea stall was firs t started—by the Bancrofts.
Pollard’s Lilliputian Opera Company, lately'reorganised
in Melbourne after its trip to Manila, China, Japan, the
United States, and Canada, is setting out for those parts
again in September. In the meantime it is trying itself
on the dog, so to speak. It began a fortnight’s season in
Brisbane on June 29, in “ The Runaway Girl.”
The torch of Hymen flared up unexpectedly in Broken
Hill, the other day, around pretty Nellie Fergusson of
the Tittell Brune co., and shed some light also on a
youthful fellow-lodger at the same hotel, named Dicken-
son. When the question of age cropped up, the Methodist
parson who had been called in put them both on their
oath, his suspicions having probably been aroused by
Nellie’s youthful face. The bridegroom signed himself 22,
while the lady, who had been playing Mary, Queen of Scots,
owned up to 23 1 The couple had known each other for
seven flays when they became “ engaged,” and the wed-
ding took place three days later. There was a little
comedy in connection with the wedding ring. In con-
sequence of a fancy on Queen Mary’s part, the article had
to be made to order, and the jeweller didn’t get the job
finished till the afternoon of the ceremony'. Romeo com-
missioned the barman of the hotel to fetch the ring from
the jeweller’s, but the barman, after getting it, put it in
his pocket, forgot all about the waiting couple, and went
out to visit a relation. When the hitching hour drew
near, there was still no word of the ring. Just as the
wedding was about to be postponed and the frantic
Romeo locked up as a dangerous lunatic, the absent
barman bethought him of the package in his pocket. A
horse and buggy that could hardly be seen for dust did
the rest. The bride goes on to Geelong and other distant
places with the Tittell Brune Co., while the husband
(who is a timekeeper at the Broken Hill Prop, mine) will
remain behind and try to think out what has happened
to him. The new missus is a daughter of old Colonel
D’Orsay Ogden, and of the lady professionally known to
melodrama as Helen Fergus.
GILBERT’S APOSTASY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. W. S. Gilbert has accepted a Knighthood !—ln-
credible cable item.
He had made us a hundred knights,
And a score of dukes beside,
And foolish things which he labelled kings,
For the great world to deride ;
Their follies and senseless pride—
Their idle boasts and vain—
Peer, prince, and court he had made our sport,
To the lilt of a gay refrain.
The satire that tipped his shafts
Was keener than tempered steel;
How vast his mirth at the claims of birth—
His laugh what a thunder-peal l
If titled fools can feel,
How exquisite the pain
Which through them thrilled when their pomp he
killed,
To the lilt of a gay refrain !
With the shafts of Wit and Scorn
He slew the great god Cant,
And with keen dart pierced Humbug’s heart
Through armor of adamant.
Let his revilers rant—
We shall not see again
His like for wit who so shrewdly hit
To the lilt of a gay refrain !
Remains this one thing more—
A sorrowful thing to write—
He who for years was above all peers,
In brilliance and delight,
Is dubbed a common knight !
What crotch has turned his brain,
That he is made as the things he flayed
To the lilt of a gay refrain ?
Were men but a'pack of dolls,
And Life but a sawdust show,
This would not irk, but men must work
And plan for what they know;
Why, then, should Fraud’s chief foe,
Whom to honor all were fain,
Accept a band from a puppet’s hand
To the lilt of a gay refrain?
N.B.W.
O. C. CABOT.

The Bulletin’s Oldest Inhabitant
writes: —
Bellevue Hall, in apart of Kent-street that is no longer
aristocratic, has been acquired by the City Council, and
is being demolished to make room for additions to the
electric-Hfht power-house. The grounds of the “ man-
sion” are bounded on the east by a piece of the old
cemetery wall. The house itself was built about the mid
’thirties by Edward Hallen, architect, whose name will be
found on the memorial tablet of the Sydney Grammar
School, and who married a daughter of Captain Lawson,
the explorer of the Blue Mountains. When Bellevue
Hall was built the outlook westward over Cockle Bay and
Black Wattle Swamp was extensive. Within a few yards,
on the opposite side of the street, is a still more ancient
house, built by Dr. J. D. Lang, and occupied in the mid
’thirties by Colonel Snodgrass, Lieutenant-Governor and
founder of the Australian Snodgrasses. Further down on
the same side is a third venerable domicile, wherein lived,
on his first arrival in Sydney, Mr. Justice Dowling, in
1828. That house is now the Soup Kitchen manager’s
residence. On the north of Bellevue Hall is an ancient
structure, built by Dr. Fattorini something like three-
parts df a century ago.

Kalgoorlie (W.A.) has been told, on the
. 5 . 1,,'',. .
affidavit of a local slaughterman, that its meat is
absolutely horrible, which, considering the loose
and incompetent habits of the State Government
of Westralia, seems quite possible. The general
experience is that meat is always liable to be
i f; •«, . , c .r\ ,
horrible except where careful Government super-
vision ensures that it shall be otherwise—ditto
milk, wages, back-yards, and many other things,
Anyhow the slaughterman has got a cold reeCp-
tion. The employers threaten to prosecute him
for slandering their blood and bone emporiums ;
l■„ ~ ,i • K ,i ’
his fellow-employees have expelled him from the
union ; and he is unpopular and out of a job.
As a rule, the common or unclean alder-
man veils his love of dirt under an assumption of
opposition to “ undue interference with the
liberty of the subject,” and to “ tyrannical legis-
lation ” generally. Rarely does he come into the
open as an avowed disciple of filth. Yet now and
again he appears naked, smellful, unashamed,
also unclothed and in his wrong mind :
During a discussion by the Warrnambool (Vic.) Hospi-
tal Committee on the question whether the pigsties
should not be removed further away from the wards,
Councillor Russell remarked that “ the smell of pigs was
health}'.”
It is, of course, well known that consumption and
pneumonia patients do not require fresh air ; and
that there is nothing like the odor of a full-sized
pig-sty to induce an invalid’s appetite for break-
fast.
* * * «
At Melbourne City Court, the other day, a
woman in the witness-box refused to give evidence.
The presiding beak threatened her with imprison-
ment, but the lady was firm, and told the Court it
would get nothing out of her. Truly, she did
answer one question as to whether she made a
statement to the police. She admitted she had,
but explained that she was drunk at the time.
Then her husband introduced himself to the
Court, and offered to answer any questions
or to go to gaol instead of his wife. But
the unchivalrous beak coldly went on to order
the woman to be imprisoned. Curiously enough,
for refusing to answer, they put her in; and for
offering to answer anything, they put her
husband out.

‘ ‘ Watch Below ’ ’ writes:—
The British mercantile marine is being brought face to
face with the manning difficulty. So far, the increasing
decision of the British bulldog to let the other chap go
to sea before the mast has been met by the employment
of Lascars and Chinamen in steamships, and of European
mixtures in sailing vessels. But shipowners and ship-
masters have hitherto insisted on British officers, and of
these the supply is also drying up. The scarcity has
only begun to appear within the last few years. So an
attempt is to be made to make promotion as regards
certificates easier, with the obvious danger of depreciating
the whole service. The root of the trouble is the scan-
dalously low rate of wages paid to merchant service
officers. The companies trading to Australia vie with
each other in building bigger and more costly vessels, but
none of them do much in the way of raising the salaries
paid to the men who care for over a quarter of a million
pounds’ worth of their property. A captain may be
responsible for ten times the value that he looked after a
few years ago, but, if his pay has increased, it hasn’t
increased by much. A vessel like a Lund liner, or ship
of that class, pays its junior officers, after seven years’
service, £5 a month—some of them £4. On this
salary the officers have to “ dress ” for dinner and
put on dog generally, so as to impress the passengers
with their importance. Social life aboard these passenger
boats has to be kept up, and the travelling heiress has
become so wary that the off-chance of a wealthy marriage
is too off to be considered. Again, fierce competition
hustles these huge ships out of port almost as soon as they
reach it, and during their stay the officers work harder
and have even less time to themselves than when at sea.
The owners’cry is that they don’t make money. It all
goes in expenses. If this be so, the fact that they risk a
fortune in the construction of a single vessel, and yet re-
fuse to find the money to satisfy the moderate demands
of master, {mates and men, who are together responsible
for the safety and well-being of the floating palace, shows
that private enterprise is running on wrong lines.

The N.S. Wales municipality of Yass—
which has a perverted idea that it
may be the Federal Capital some day
—is announcing that it wants a council
clerk, “qualified in accordance with
the Local Government Act of 1906 to
fill the position of council clerk, over-
seer of works, etc.”; and it further
notifies—
Applicants must be competent to take
levels for road construction, draw up plans
and specifications of works, and supervise
same, understand the manufacture and dis-
tribution of gas, and the reading of meters.
And for the council clerk who is also
to be road engineer and gas engineer,
and to fill in odd moments reading
the meters (nothing is mentioned
about lighting the lamps), the munici-
pality of Yass offers—£l4o per an-
num. Fortunately, the Local Govern-
ment Act does provide something by
way of checking the accountancy of
the accountant who is also road en-
gineer, gas engineer, and meter-reader,
and all thrown in for £l4O a year.
But up to now any old hanger-on of
the mayor got £2 as “auditor,” and
the results were weird. Glassop, for
25 years the town clerk of the big
Sydney suburb of Ashfield, the other
day told the Judge who was trying
him for embezzlement, that he really
didn’t know where the money had
gone; the “social duties” and the
other things he was supposed to attend
to took up so much of his time that
he could only give the tail of his eye
to the small matter of the suburb’s
finances. And yet Glassop didn’t
have to personally stoke the local gas-
works.
“ Quan” holds forth :
Will “ Jimmie Pannikin ” state which of my queries
(B. 30/5/’O7) he considers irrelevant ? And how about his
replies? I asked “Does he know any (inspectors) who
were pitchforked into their positions without ever having
had charge of schools ? ” His reply was —“ There be some
folk who regard every innovation as an improvement;
there be others who like to shuffle along in the old ruts
of a rusty past.” Does that mean yes or no, or a bit of
both, George Reid Pannikin? Again I asked —“ Does he
know any who were never first-class teachers?” He
replied—“ Superficial people think that ‘dambi-extra
drorin ’ and ‘ blasticene puddling,’ and a few other frilly
fads, are the very soul of the New Syllabus. I have
defined the New S. several times for Bulletin borrowers
and readers.” Does that mean yea or nay, Mr. Jimmie
Relevancy? “Jimmie’s” answers to some of my other
queries indicate that, although he is an inspector, he
doesn’t know a good many things that are matters of
common knowledge among teachers, or perhaps he has
found it convenient to forget them since becoming
an inspector. He doesn’t know an inspector with an
extra-violent temper. Well, my informant 'was a
teacher who was a pupil-teacher under him in a big
city school when he was appointed. Perhaps if I
say that he is eccentric, “ Jimmie ” will know who I
mean. And so the two present heads of the Department
believed in schools being “palaces of dear delight,” etc.,
even before the New Syllabus was introduced. Well, in
that case, it seems peculiar that one of them was regarded
as a machine, more than a man, when an inspector. As
for the sheaves of grateful letters, splendid presents,
valuable gifts, and the bathetic reference to historic old
Parramatta, what have they to do with my questions
regarding some of the ways in which inspectors have
been appointed ? By all means let us have the New Sylla-
bus, but also let us have up-to-date inspectors whose
appointments are above suspicion.

* * * *
The scandal of the Melbourne Mint rob-
bery seems to have ended in a conspiracy
of silence among the local police, the
press, and the Bent Government. Here was a
case of £l7OO worth of bar gold disappear-
ing from the custody of a policeman at the
Mint lodge. Said bobby was acting irre-
sponsibly on behalf of the Government when
he received the metal. Of all the recent
robberies which have brought Melbourne’s
police force into contempt, the Mint case
should have been the easiest mystery to
solve; so the fact that nobody, up to time
of writing, is charged with this remarkable
theft is not more surprising than the sudden
cessation of newspaper comment on the
scandal. Will the Government offer no
reward for the discovery of the person who
stole £l7OO in gold, almost as soon as it was
committed to the care of a Government
servant on Government premises, and put
in the Government safe, and locked with the
Government key ? Do the police know
everything about the robbery, or nothing
about it ? —and why this silence as to their
knowledge or their ignorance? And is the
public content to regard a very dirty-looking
incident as a good joke?

An extract from the rules of the Pansy
Quadrille Club, Broken Hill: —
Gents, are requested to wear gloves or use a handker-
chief.
Any lady who refuses to stand up with any gent, in a
dance must not stand up at all in that dance.
All couples must embrace when swinging in the squares.
There is not room for the toffs’ way of swinging.
The Pansy Quadrille exists right through the
year, even though the thermometer may register
110° on top of the iceberg.

BACCHANALIA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
'Tis morn. Your crumpled pillow doth exhale
Odors of midnight revelry and stale
Cigars and stout. Two damaged lobster claws
Adorn the spot where once Her photo was.
The dying tuberose in your evening suit
Suggests a mourning coach. A patent boot
Hangs sadly pendant from the gasolier ;
Last night’s events are but a mental smear.
How got you home ? Where left your trusty pal ?
What of the unconnected interval ?
Oh 1 dimly groping brain, where spectres pass
Of Flossies, p’leeeemen, cabs, and broken glass 1
Is this your head that on the pillow lies,
Or Santos-Dumont’s latest enterprise?
The time ? Eleven o’clock ! Ye Gods ! Alack,
The Boss, the office ledgers, and—the Sack.
To this burnt-umber palate who will bring
Strawberries, lemons, ices, anything ?
The stone that rolled last night and gained no moss
Yearns rabid at the dawn for Worcester Sauce.
What distant cry is that ? What pealing chimes ?
What raucous kid calls out the Sunday Slimes?
Oh ! tell-tale looking-glass. Oh ! face forlorn !
Smile once again, for it is Sunday morn.
N.S.W.

Douglas Story tells in his “To-morrow in
the East ” (which is the North as far as Australia
is concerned) that British passengers arriving by
Japanese boats in Korea have to stand aside till
the first-class Japanese passengers have landed.
The first-class Japs go off in a nice clean tender.
The first-class white trash have to wait for a dirty
tug, and go ashore along with the second-class
Jap passengers and the steerage coolies. Now,
supposing President Kruger, in his palmy days,
had decreed that a British person travelling first-
class by train must not move till the first-class
Boer passenger had left the carriage, and must
have his meal at refreshment stations with second
and third-class Boer passengers, and the inhabi-
tants of the cattle truck !! ! That is the sort of
thing that Britain puts up with from its brown
ally.

When a Dago opens a fish-and-chips shop
in the Maligned State, he touts for custom under
the auspices of the A.N.A. Around Melbourne
there are stacks of A.N.A. fish and oyster saloons,
cooked meat shops, ice-cream warehouses, and
rabbit-oh dens ; and the wine-shops, with Totties
in the hinderparts, are largely A.N.A. on the
outside. The Association is now fetching its feet
down on the whole illegitimate brood, and A.N.A.
is to be registered as a trade-mark.

NEW DAY.
[For The Bulletin.]
Now is the hour when all the dark turns gray
And with a swift, chill shudder pales and dies,
And soft, sweet dreams smile over sleepless eyes,
Sped from the fresh breath of the new-born day.
Now wakes the first bird at the light’s first ray,
The old man stirs and turns and sleeping, sighs ;
The cock crows loud without and the child cries,
And weary lovers kiss and turn away.
Now on the sea the shipmen’s lights are out
And the cold waves take fire, and all t he earth
Stirs after sleep, new-born with keener mood ;
When Night’s black hour-glass freshly swings about
God send me also rest and a new birth,
New light, a riddle solved and strength renewed.
n.s.w. Harry Sullivan.
* * * *
Adelaide is in trouble. It lately whipped
itself up to the point of deciding to substitute for
its gradual horse-trams the whirling electric-car ;
and, when it looks back calmly on its decision, it
wonders whether it really didn’t
do something rash, and
whether it will ever be
able to keep out of the
road of the deadly
machines, supposing
it starts them. It
knows, however, that
there is no drawing
back now ; it will
have to take the
awful risk, having
gone so far. But it
wants to save itself
from being run over
more than possible,
so a majority of the
mostly-municipal
Tramway Trust
favors laying the
rails along the broad-
est and least-fre-
quented streets (the
broadest streets hap-
pen to be the least
frequented), and keep-
ing quite clear of the
narrow and busy
streets —so far as any
Adelaide streets are
busy and narrow. The
narrowest streets are
broader than Georg"-
street, Sydney, is in
various places ; yet George-
street, Sydney, has a double
line of trams that carry more
traffic in a day than any Adelaide
line would have a chance to carry
in a week. But the majority won’t
risk it; it is so deadly afraid of being
run over that even in the remote places,
which look like a cemetery, traffic is forbidden
to go over street-crossings at more than a
funeral-walking pace. And in case the Trust’s
engineer might scoff at these fears and tell
the Trust to take the trams where the traffic is,
the Trust has decided by a majority not to ask
the engineer for any report at all on the question
of routes.
* * * *
The Bulk Shire Council (Vic.) having an-
nounced its intention of giving the young men of
Sun bury permission to play football on a local
reserve on Sunday, Presbyteries and other church
bodies are up in arms against a-loathly attempt to
fracture the Sabbath, and Judkins bids the
Council beware. Also, the Presbytery of Mel-
bourne North declares that Sunday football is
calculated to encourage unseemly conduct. So is
Saturday football for that matter. Maiming
an umpire is unseemly on almost any day of the
week. An even graver charge follows : “Sunday
football is inimical to religious education.” But
Sunday football doesn’t keep young men from
church. It only keeps them from the pub. There
are many side issues about this business. It
seems to raise the question : “ Can a footballer be
a Christian ?”

Do not take a substitute for Chamberlain’s
Cough 'Remedy. It lias no equal.*
Probably the most fervent prayers ever
uttered by Pastor Baglin, of FoOtscray (Vie.),
were put up by him one recent Sabbath evening,
while he was travelling from North Melbourne
to South Kensington. After seeing the collec-
tion safely taken up in a friend’s church at North
Melbourne, he made for the station, and arrived
just as his train was leaving. Rushing down the
ramp he got so much way on that he bumped
violently against the corner of a moving carriage
and tumbled between it and the next. Luckily,
he grabbed the drawbar and hung on. As no one
had noticed the whirling Gospeller, the train pro-
ceeded, and the sky-quartermaster had either
to drop to sudden death or hang on to the
drawbar and to this vale of tears for all he
was worth. So he hung on, coiling his legs
round the bar for greater security and think-
ing hard. His handbag and hat went the
way of all things that drop from a moving train ;
his long coat flapped against the wheels, while the
sleepers and metals tore it to ribbons; blood
streamed down his face from a wound on his fore-
head—-but he still clung, and avoided Heaven.
When the train stopped at South Kensington he
still had strength to clamber on the platform, but
he was a collapsed and washed-out man for many
days after. Even the department’s promise to
summon him for rushing at one of Tait’s trains
while in motion failed to rouse him to any sense
of the humor of the situation.
* * * *
Melbourne’s citizens are beginning to wear
a permanent expression of “weird expectancy.”
Among burglars, pickpockets, garrotters, and the
tricks of the food adulterator and the importer,
they have quite enough to do to keep on the alert.
And somebody sells them a wild cat in between
times, and they have Judkins, Wren, Worrall,
Woodful, Bent, and the landlord on their minds.
But the other day a new terror appeared in the
shape of an alleged “ trance medium ” who, under
the control of his wife and a “lecturer on the
occult,” drove a carriage and pair, while blind-
folded, through the city in an attempt to find a
secreted penknife, or a brick or something. By
the grace of the gods he didn’t kill anybody, but
he wrecked a buggy and drove sundry money-
making schemes out of the heads of several
respectable citizens. Also, when a traffic-regu-
lating constable seized the horses’ heads in an
effort to stop the career of this blindfolded driver,
h:v whipped up the animals and pressed on regard-
less, while the Law pluckily hung on and quoted
bylaw XVIQ. Bystanders yelled to the other
occupants of the carriage-to “throw him out,”
but they didn’t. Yet it would have been interest-
ing to note whether a man full of disembodied
spirits hits the pavement with a similar thud to the
man with bulk rum in him.

The wisdom of “ Lewin Crade ” :
A man has but to make an insulting remark about
Woman, and, lo ! he is proclaimed a wit.
Even the most stupid may excel at silent contempt.
Consider the postage stamp. Its chief merit consists
in sticking to a thing until it gets there.
Victorian Chief Justice Madden has had
before him the amazing handiwork of twoKyabram
magistrates. The trouble arose about an itinerant
book-vend or whom the police, for some inscru-
table reason, aroused from his bed at a local hotel
and arrested on a question of vagrancy He strenu-
ously denied the vagrancy, blit admitted that he
had been, in ignorance, hawking without a license.
He was nevertheless charged next day, not with
the offence which he acknowledged, but with the
original one of vagrancy, notwithstanding that
the police had searched his belongings and dis-
covered that he had 31s. 6d. and a stock of books.
Being very deaf, he was in complete ignorance of
the nature of the case and of the evidence given
in court, and kuew nothing until a constable
roared into his ear that he had got “ two months’
hard labor.” He wished to appeal, but was not
allowed access to a lawyer ; in fact, he had been
one month in gaol before the visiting police magis-
trate and the Penal Inspector-General came
across him. Hearing his statements, they passed
the matter on to the Attorney-General, who re-
leased him. At considerable expense this alleged
vagrant then sought to have his character cleared
by getting the conviction quashed. This his Honor
promptly did, at the same time telling the police
that in the circumstances they had no right to
search him, nor to arrest him, nor to charge him
with vagrancy. Also, his Honor said that the
two amazing Kyabram beaks “apparently did
not trouble to inquire what the law was. They
were told the man had no lawful means of sup-
port, although he had 31s. 6d. on him, yet they
convicted him apparently unheard, and ordered
two months’ imprisonment.... He had thought
at first it was an outrage, a deliberate attempt to
deprive a man of his liberty, but he was now of
opinion that it was a case in which both police
and magistrates had grossly blundered.” Yet up
to now there is no sign that the Government has
relieved the two Solomons of their commissions ;
so that apparently they are to go on “ adminis-
tering the law ” without troubling to find out
what it is, and exhibiting an incapacity so
enormous, that at first Mr. Madden could hardly
persuade himself that the matter was ignorance,
and not malice and conspiracy.
* * * *
That well-meaning democrat, but over-
excited philanthropist, Dr. Maloney, M.H.R..
has this remarkable notice on the paper of the
House of Reps.:—
That, in any scheme of pensions, provision should be
made so that any person risking his or her life to save
that of another human being shall become entitled to a
full old-age pension, and, should the rescuer lose his or
her life in the attempt, his or her next of kin shall become
entitled to a double pension.
For a scheme w'hich opens up unlimited possibili-
ties, that about holds the record. If it were
adopted, a person would hardly get faiily wet in
the waters of Circular Quay, Sydney, before the
feet of about two dozen rescuers would land on
him and give him brain concussion. Then when
they got him out, he would be nearly dislocated
on the wharf in the struggle of his many helpers
to get possession of the wet, insensible bit of evi-
dence, and rush it in a cab to the Old Age Pension
Office. And probably it would prove that the
saved one could swim perfectly, and had only
gone in to recover his hat, and that there was
nothing wrong with him except that he was dead
through his numerous preservers falling on his
head.
