XTOTICE.—If it is possible for Mrs.TOM CHAND-
TN LER, of Argent-street, to act wisely, she will!
accept this warning, and cease talking about me.
Mrs. O. CHANDLER,
West Broken Hill.
They insert notices of this sort every day at
Broken Hill. Nothing ever comes of them, but
they keep on inserting them. Every country has
its own customs. The woman of some parts of
Western Asia is reputed to go up on the flat roof
of the house when she feels she, can stand it no
longer, and on that roof she holds forth. She has
hysterics. She denounces her enemy. She ex-
poses the said enemy. She flings her hair to the
eight winds of heaven, and holds out her arms like
semaphores. She curses the enemy’s ancestry right
back, and his or her posterity right forward. She
foams and performs, and raises Cain and the devil.
And down in the street below no one heeds her.
Men, hags, children, camels, dogs, priests, beggars,
lepers, dervishes, and pea-nut vendors drift by in
a steady stream. They take no interest in the
squalling cataract of language on the roof. Ib®y
don’t care a clod for her, or her enemy, or her
grievance, and she doesn t expect them to. vf 1 ®
is just relieving her feelings, and when she has
emptied the last curse out of herself, and has
shouted herself speechless, she refixes her hair,
and goes down to boil the food for dinner. It is
just a way she has—the same as the woman at
Broken Hill. The Asiatic custom might prevail
at the Hill, but the house-tops aren’t flat there,
and a lady on all fours on the slippery galvanised
iron roof of a one-storey building couldn t hold
forth to advantage—she could barely hold on.

THE HIGH C.S. AND GREAT T.C.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The Town Clerk (Mr. Nesbitt) and the City Surveyor
(Mr. Gordon) are at loggerheads over labor appointments,
the latter claiming the right to engage or dismiss work-
men. Mr. Nesbitt thinks he should be referred to in such
eases, and is supported by the Lord Mayor.— Sydney

“At loggerheads ! ” Good gracious me,
What disrespectful language!
I never thought to live to see
Such TELEGRAPH-ic slanguage !
The High C.S. and Great T.C.
“At loggerheads ! ” My goodness !
My goodcious grayness ! Mercy me !
What unexampled rudeness !
They both should well-respected be,
The High C.S. and Great T.C.
Of course, you’ll guess my deep distress
On learning of this matter !
The. Great T.C. and High C.S.
I have no wish to flatter ;
But gentlemen of courteousness,
Befitting lofty stations,
Should have (at least before the Press)
Harmonious relations!
They should preserve “la politesse”—
The Great T.C. and High C.S.
There is a phrase which 9eems to me,
And always has in one sense,
The height of rank absurditee
And addle-pated nonsense :
“ Better two heads than one ! ” But we
Who don’t let phrases fool us,
In spite of this philosophee,
Prefer one head to rule us
1 won’t say if that head should be
The High CIS. or Great T.C.
Not long ago there was a mess
(Don’t mention names, good Heavens !)
That put the railway bus-i-ness
At sixes and at sevens !
Two men may each achieve success
In any trade or calling,
But someone must be Boss ; unless
That’s so, the friction’s galling 1
And then, of course, we must suppress
The Great T.C. or High C.S. !
The little birds in harmonee
A lesson oft unfold us ;
They in their little nests agree,
As Wattsisname has told us 1
But why do they agree, sirree,
And bickerings forsake ’em?
The mother-bird is on the tree
To boss the nest and make ’em !:
One head should rule, if he or she l ,
Or High C.S. or Great T. 0.. t

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
S. Australia foresees one of those mishaps that
are liable to occur in the worst regulated families. There
was a paragraph in the papers lately to the effect that
the Transvaal, which it was thought would want a trifle
from Cohen this year, had decided to put off the visit to
the pawnshop until next year, when something large in
the, way of a loan will be floated under Imperial Govern-
ment guarantee. Thereupon S. Australia turned up its
bodks and found that it would also have to call upon
Cohen for a large amount next year—either that or it
would have to tell the old Cohen that his bill was renewed
whether he liked it or not —and it also remembered that
it wouldn’t have the ghost of an Imperial guarantee to
offer. When the tight corner into which S. Australia
might be driven was realised, its Government rushed off
the following wire to the other State Governments :
“ Rumored that Imperial Government delaying issue of
£5,000,000 Transvaal loan until next year. Strongly urge
you cable your Agent-General join other Agents-General
to represent in strongest terms that such delay and the
placing of a large loan under Imperial Government guar-
antee in 1908 on London market may seriously and detri-
mentally affect the financial operations of the whole of
the Australian States, owing to Australian loans not being
so guaranteed, and the possibility of having recourse to
the London money market for the purpose of raising
funds towards the redemption of over £10,00,000 of Aus-
tralian loans maturing between January, 1908, and
January, 1909.”
Queensland and Westralia didn’t get this flustered message
because they don’t happen to have loans falling due in
1908 ; but N.S. Wales has £2,865,500 of funded and
£350,000 of unfunded debt to wrestle with (figures are
from the last statement of the N.S.W. Bureau of
Statistics), Victoria has £2,023,869 of funded trouble, and
Tasmania about £788,000 altogether. South Australia’s
own little lot is £1,951,100 funded and £278,200 unfunded.
The fact that the curse would come home to
roost has been known all along ; but every shiftless
Government has continued to add to the number of curses
on the wing, and has cheerfully taken it for granted that
no curse would ever come home to roost at an awkward
time. Yet, if not another loan were issued, there is
hardly a year between now and 1935 when one or more
Australian State Goverments won’t have to pay up, renew
or repudiate; and it is past all probability that there
won’t be some awkward times between now and 1935.
Here, on the very threshold—for the big loans have only
now started to fall due —one State spies trouble ahead ;
and stilly all that there is to make the trouble on this
occasion is one comparatively insignificant foreign loan—
s.iy about one-tenth the size of the loans Britain would
have to call for within 12 months supposing serious un-
pleasantness arose with any of its neighbors.
Barring Westralia, there isn’t a single State
with a sinking fund that you couldn’t lose in a thimble,
and Westralia is wriggling all it knows to escape from the
“ burden ” of adding to its trifle. N.S. Wales, whose fund
was nominally created by J. Carruthers, naturally hasn’t,
therefore, any real sinking fund at all, because as soon as
there is any money in hand it is used to purchase and
cancel bonds, which thereafter don’t bear interest; so
that the sinking fund doesn’t benefit by interest and
compound interest. At June 30, 1906, the aggregate of
the sinking funds and the aggregate of the net debts were
as follows :
Sinking Actual
Funds. Liability.
N.S. Wales .. .. £566,484 £83,306,105
Victoria .. .. 408,561 54,256,919
Queensland .. .. Nil 39,933,935
S. Australia .. .. 251,349 29,413,623
Westralia .. .. 1,320,603 16,202,057
Tasmania .. .. 236,297 9,446,938
Total, all States .. £2,783,294 £232,559,577
No State is therefore in a position to tell Cohen that if he
doesn’t care to renew a large bill he can do the other
thing, and that the State will pay him off out of its own
accumulated resources. In any case, of course, it
wouldn’t be likely to have the cash in hand, but it would
have the securities, and the rest would be easy financing.
And, anyhow, Cohen wouldn’t be likely to be disagree-
able if he knew that he couldn’t make something by being
disagreeable : when a State has to renew or default it is
just the same easy game as a crowd of workmen are who
have to accept the boss’s terms or starve. They equally
have “ freedom of contract.” Yet, if one State struck
the trouble which S. Australia at present sees ahead,
and if it were a trouble of larger size and meant absolute
default, the State that next turned up in the market to
argue with Cohen about a note that was falling
due, would be almost certain to have a cold time.
Cohen’s incapacity to realise that N.S. Wales is the Ma
State, with the added blessing of being bossed by
Carruthers, while S. Australia is merely a poor relation
sitting on the other side of a line that you pass over
without feeling any bump whatever—Cohen’s incapacity
to see the vast difference between the two would be
pretty certain to give Sydney the cold shivers.
Under these circumstances, the sooner the
Commonwealth starts taking over the State debts by
providing a sinking fund to buy them up, the better.
It really doesn’t matter whether all the buzzing State
politicians agree or not. Some of them are for ever say-
ing what it was “ understood ” the Commonwealth would
do, and they call on the Commonwealth to do it; but
nothing was more clearly understood than that the State
debts would be taken over. Yet the same crowd stands
in the way of that. Fora start, it is indispensable that
some body should have means at its disposal to make a
State, or all the States, more or less independent of
Cohen when a loan comes to be renewed. And the only
body to get the means is the Commonwealth. It is
appalling that a State with over £30,000,000 of debt out-
standing should shake at the knees over the renewal of
less than two millions.

Here is the past 10 years’ Customs record of
Maoriland’s ineffective Protection in a few leading lines
of imports:—
Apparel. Boots. Woollens. Iron.
1897 £362,389 £132,203 £293,449 £1,406,111
1898 354,940 130,912 286,911 1,502,794
1899 393,689 151,593 297,387 1,578,855
1900 451,879 194,811 330,713 2,113,219
1901 463,194 211,215 348,666 2,461,696
1902 511,397 202,931 393,033 2,130,243
1903 582,997 245,639 436,161 2,271,923
1904 603,894 256,165 452,271 2,557,869
1905 614,859 282,211 438,480 2,253,574
1906 685,195 300,134 479,986 2,813,096
Iron includes manufactured iron and steel goods.
The record is appalling—in the matter of boots and
woollens especially ; for Maoriland has all the materials
for those particular industries ready to its hand.
It prefers, however, to export its raw products,
and buy back the finished goods made up by labor
which can be sweated without any Arbitration
Court to protect it. Maoriland, in short, is prepared to
sweat everybody and anybody but itself ; it says that its
own people shall not work except at certain compara-
tively high rates of pay, and for certain comparatively
few hours a day ; and then, having put that prohibition
upon its own people, it opens its ports with very little
restriction to whatever the employers of the poorest-paid
and hardest-worked people in the world like to send in.
The further Arbitration and other labor laws push
reform in the matter of wages and hours of work, ob-
viously the higher the Customs will have to push duties
in order to restrain the competition of the people who
are outside the Arbitration and other labor laws ; and
because Maoriland hasn’t done that, naturally' more and
more work goes to tl. foreigner, and less and less is left
for the Maorilander, until the Customs returns show
appalling import figures like the foregoing.

There seems to be a ghastly waste about the
Queensland sugar-growing business as far as it has gone.
Last season, of 8,373,581 gallons of molasses reported as
produced, no less than 4,529,836 gallons were “run to
waste ” ; and it is known that a great quantity which
“ ran to waste ” was never included in the returns at all.
The Moreton district returns, for instance, only show 32
gallons per ton of sugar, whereas the returns from the
Edgecumbe division (Ayr, Bowen and Mackay) show 75
gallons per ton ; and the real figures were probably about
the same in each case. Therefore it is likely that last
year over 7,500,000 gallons of molasses were wasted. Yet,
apart from the fact that good food for man can be made
out of molasses, the stuff has a high value as fodder for
live stock ; and it is a fodder that will keep against the
time when drought strips the country of every' other
sort of edible thing. So long as the sugar busi-
ness had cheap colored labor it didn’t trouble about
by'-products—just as the goldfield doesn’t trouble about
its tailings dumps while it can root out ounce-and-a-half
ore. But the Commonwealth which rebates duty to an
industry in order to keep it in white hands and leave a
profit for the growers, will shortly' begin to see that it has
a very keen practical interest in having the waste and
the scraps used up and turned into money, so that it can
be relieved of part of the rebate, anyhow. Further, the
Australian home consumption having now been passed,
the Australian producer will have to battle for a foreign
market. That market is largely supplied by sugar grown
with the help of about the cheapest labor on earth ; and
the Australian white sugar-grower will have to set his
brains against the colored person’s mule-like toil in order
to wipe out the difference in cost of production.
• • • «
The A.M.P. bonus certificates have been scat-
tered over the six States and Maoriland during this last
week ; and, as about 210,000 of them were sent out, they
have, allowing for duplicates, drifted into probably every
seventh or eighth dwelling, which is a mighty fine record
for any office’s bonuses to put up. And the bonuses are
worth having. The A.M.P., for instance, this year adds
about £1,200,000 to the face-value of its policies ; that is
to say, the society’s profits for the year have bought and
paid for £1,200,000 more insurance than the insurers
bargained for. These are such striking figures that the
insurance drummer finds that he can land a “ case ” with
bonus results oftener than with any other kind of bait.
For once that he is asked" whether this office or the other
one charges the stiffer premiums, he is asked twice
whether this "office or the other one pays the bigger
bonuses. The average insurer, in fact, appears to have
an idea that all insurance offices start off scratch, so to
speak, charging the same premium, and that anything
they pay in bonus is so much clear profit to the man who
insures. There are, therefore, one or two things about
bonuses which seem to be worth saying.
All offices do not charge the same premiums.
Each office charges what it individually thinks will be
necessary to cover the risk it is taking. The premiums
certainly don’t vary much, because most offices use much
the same expectation of life tables; but one may reckon
to earn a little more interest on accumulated funds than
another, or to work at lower cost. Therefore premium
rates do vary. In Australia, for instance, where the
average life is longer than it is in Great Britain, and
where higher interest can be earned, offices generally
feel themselves safe in charging somewhat lower pre-
miums. That is one reason why the English office keeps
clear of Australia ; it would have to reduce its English
tables of rates in order to compete with Australian offices.
That is also why the Australian office, working on its
Australian rates, may expect to come out badly if it does
much ordinary English business. It probably won’t be
charging enough for its risks. Still the A.M.P. is going
to try the experiment. It didn’t know when it was well
off.
Bonuses come from three main sources: (1) More
interest is earned than is calculated ; (2) less is spent in
expenses than was allowed for when the premium was
fixed; (3) people don’t die as soon as had been
reckoned on, and so go on paying premiums, and
the money their widows would draw if they died when
they were expected to die goes on earning interest for
the office. It will readily be seen how important a factor
No. 3 is. Yet it may not show itself for years. Take
two offices : one selects its “ lives ” carefully, and spends
a good deal of money in the process ; the other lashes
out, takes risks almost as they come, and piles up its
figures at a low cost. The claims in both cases are few —
they haven’t begun to come in yet. The policies are
mostly young ; so neither of the offices is making any, or
much, profit out of favorable mortality experience. ' But
one, because its selection is thorough, shows larger
expenses ; therefore it shows smaller profits ; and there-
fore it can only pay smaller bonuses. But, later on, on
account of its better mortality experience, it will most
likely leave the other far behind in the matter of profits
and bonuses. So the bonuses of to-day, after all, may be
no test of what the bonuses of to-morrow will be.
The profit from excess interest earned is of little
consequence for a year or two ; but later on it becomes a
mighty factor. In the first year an insurer has paid in, say,
£lO. If the office has calculated on earning 3 per cent, and
actually earns 4 per cent., clearly the excess earnings on
that year’s premium amount to 1 per cent, on £lO, or 2s.
But after 10 years the accumulation of the annual £lO at
3percent. compound interest will amount to £114125. 4d. ;
and excess interest earnings of 1 per cent, on that
total will amount to just upon £1 3s. After 30 years the
excess earnings from this source—that is, this contribu-
tion to profit available for bonus—will amount to
£4 15s. 2d., or 47'6 per cent, of the year’s premium, as
against 1 per cent, on the first year’s premium. From
this we can see how a young office is handicapped in a
comparison with, say, a middle-aged one. They may be
equally good, equally sound, equally profitable in reality.
Yet in’one all the policies will be young, and the accu-
mulations (or, technically, reserves) will be small, and the
excess interest earnings will therefore be also small in
proportion to premiums received ; while in the other
a large proportion of the policies will be old or
middle-aged, the accumulations (or reserves) will
therefore be large, and the excess interest earnings
in proportion to annual premium will be large. Now,
bonuses are generally reported as so much per cent, of
the year’s premiums ; yet necessarily the young office,
though it may be just as sound as the old one, and
though, in the long run, it may give an insurer as good
results, will, for some years, be able to distribute by way
of bonus a smaller proportion of the premium.
Actual earnings are, of course, not affected a
jot by the rate of assumed earnings. But bonuses are.
The actuary each year—or every three or five years, as
the office’s rule may be—values the office’s policy liabili-
ties. That is to say, he takes a policy which has been in
force 10 years, and whjch, according to past experience,
ought to continue in force for 15 years more—in other
words, an average healthy person of the age of that insurer
is found to live 15 years more. The amount accumulated
is already, say, £llO. To pay the policy in 15 years,
assuming the £llO and the subsequent 15 premiums go
on earning 3 per cent., there need, however, be only
£lOB in hand. The difference—£2—is the amount avail-
able for bonus. Obviously, if the office assumes that
4 per cent, will be earned, there need not be as much in
hand to pay (along with subsequent premiums) the policy
in 15 years'. Ten pounds put into a fund every year, and
improved at 3 per cent, compound interest, reaches £lB5
19s. and some odd pence in 15 years ; at 4 per cent, it
would reach £2OO 4s. and some odd pence. The lower
thqassumed rate of interest, therefore, the higher the
reserve (or undivided accumulation) must be, and, there-
fore, for the time being the smaller the bonuses. Con-
sequently, the assumed rate of interest has to be taken
into account when comparing bonuses; for an office,
by assuming a high rate of interest, could pay large
bonus in its earlier career, but it could only do so at the
expense of bonuses later on.

“ Itan ” : When Premier Kidston said Queens-
land was “ on her own ” concerning the mail-service con-
tract (B. 4/7/’O7), he was not hinting at a State Frights
crisis. The solution is probably this : Queensland for
years enjoyed a Torres Straits service, and Queensland
political parties have for some time agreed that some
such arrangement should be reorganised. It will be more
of a cargo than a mail service, since the steamers serving
the southern States would deliver English bags more
quickly at Brisbane than they could be delivered by the
North Australian route. The far northern centres would,
however, probably get the benefit of a quicker service, if
there was any great advantage in that. But the idea
is not to gain any benefit in that direction so much
as to secure a line of steamers which would pick up and
drop cargo at Gladstone, Townsville and Cairns. At
present the coastal ring can impose its own rates on cargo
for transhipment at Sydney and Brisbane, to say nothing
of the cost of the double handling. North Queensland must
yet become a great butter-producing centre, but if the
butter has to be sent down the coast in the coastal ring’s
steamers, much of the cow-man’s profits will trickle into
the fatman’s pockets before the butter gets aboard the
deep-sea ship. Then there is the rapidly-increasing
export of minerals, which is in the same position now as
the other produce is. Kidston’s idea is in no way
parochial. He is too far-seeing to meddle in a petty
way. It will be found When he plays his card that he
has in view a double string to Bananaland’s bow, and the
new string will be really the chief string for the rich
districts of the Far North.

After the comparatively small trouble which arrived at
this bank’s doors at the time when most Australian
banks were being visited, the North Queensland let
several years pass by without paying any dividend at
all; and when it did begin again in 1900 —after writing
off £3 out of every £5 of capital paid up —it was content
to pay a modest little 2J per cent. Meantime, on account
of the poor times Queensland was having, it apparently
still found plenty to do in the way of writing down values
out o€ undisclosed profits—setting, in other words, profits
made in one direction against losses made in other direc-
tions. With a mostly-Labor Government and the aboli-
tion of the Kanaka, t here should, according to the pro-
phets, have been further heavy losses. Fortunately,
however, the reverse has happened : and partly on that
account, and partly by extending its business southward,
the bank is at length in a position to raise its dividend to
4 per cent. This comparison shows the progress in some
detail in the past 12 months :
June, 1906. June, 1907.
Deposits .. ..£366,885 £475,374 Inc., £108,489
Bills in circulation, etc. 53,289 43,367 Dec., 9,922
Liquid assets.. .. 151,425 169,662 Inc., 18,237
Advances .. .. 363,797 446,553 Inc., 82,756
Premises .. .. 28,497 29,068 Inc., 571
These, in proportion to total figures, are very fine move
ments ; and they explain most of the increased profit
shown. Indeed, the improvement in the Queensland
position is so great, that it is quite likely that the banks
doing business there at present find theifiselves able to
put aside some undisclosed profits to contingency account,
as provision against the droughty times that are certain
to arrive sooner or later. The profit as disclosed is at the
rate of £4 17s. 9d. per cent, on capital and reserves. This
compares with recent results in this way :
Dec., 1903 .. £4 3 0 p.c. Dec., 1905 .. £3 16 11 p.c.
June, 1904 .. 316 11 ~ June, 1906 .. 4 1 4 ~
Dec., 1904 .. 4 2.0 „ Dec., 1906 .. 4 6 7 „
June, 1905.. 316 0 „ June, 1907.. 417 9 „
The advances show a large increase ; yet even so the
liquid assets repr sent 33 per cent, of the debts to the
public. Probably beyond this it would not be safe to go ;
that is to say, to make further advances on any large
scale, it would probably be necessary to get in further
deposits. And, as in proportion to shareholders’ funds
the deposits are still small, there is plenty of room for a
safe expansion of business in the direction of new deposits.

Though nothing is being written off the large goodwill
item, there is a steady accumulation of liquid assets :
of the formal reserve fund of £55,000, the co. has £43,910
in fixed deposits and Melbourne Board of Works deben-
tures, and the balance in freehold. (In the foregoing this
reserve fund and the undivided balance are deducted
from the deficit represented by the goodwill item.) But
in order to increase the ordinary dividend to 12 per
cent, a good deal less than the average is this half-
year added to the liquid fund. Meantime, however,
apparently relying upon the maintenance of the increased
dividend, ordinary shares (which are about the only
newspaper shares regularly quoted on ’Change) have been
pushed up from about 355. to 40s. Yet the formal divi-
dend is kept at 8 per cent. ; the extra 4 per cent, is dis-
tributed by way of bonus —which may mean that the
directors don’t want to give the impression that they are
satisfied they can continue the larger payment. The
market value of the concern is now about £400,000
(125,000 preference shares at 245. 6d. and 125,000 ordinary
shares at 395. 6d.). The substantial net assets are as
follows
Freehold property and fittings .. .. £31,360
Machinery 29,309
Stock 9,091
Board of Works debentures .. .. 4,909
Deposits, book debts, etc. (net) .. .. 36,190
Total £110,859
So that the market values the goodwill at £400,000 less
£110,859, or at £289,141, which is a pretty large price to
pay for such a doubtful and insecure and impalpable
substance.

This co. deals in city, suburban, and country lands—buys
wholesale and sells retail on time payment. Its paid
capital is now £88,369, and its reserves are as shown
above ; but in order to carry on its extensive financing
operations, it has borrowed £37,619 from depositors, and
it owes £56,475 to its bankers and others, or a total of
£94,094. These amounts are represented on the other
side mainly by sundry estates (£123,969), city properties
(£38,373), and advances (£62,080). The last item, which
consists chiefly' of unpaid balances on property sold, has
increased latterly, while the value of estates in hand is
shrinking ; which apparently means that this co. thinks
the present is a very good time to sell land. Possibly it
also reckons that the present is a very good time to buy
land for subdivision ; but its hands are fairly full—after
providing for the current dividend there were only' £1037
of liquid assets left. It is a business involving a good
deal of risk; apart from the fact that nobody can tell
what the £123,909 of estates will sell for, the ultimate
value of even the unpaid balances is in some doubt.
Presumably the co. has some undisclosed reserves against
these possibilities ; if it hasn’t, too much of the profit is
being absorbed by dividends.

The 10 per cent, dividend, being only paid every other
year, works out at the butter cos.’ usual 5 per cent, per
annum. In addition, however, an unusually profitable
half-year just completed allows of a distribution to sup-
pliers who are also shareholders at the rate of jd. per lb.
of butter supplied. As in the case of most of these cos.
the capital (£2437) is small considering the size of the
turnover ; but the cash position is at present very good,
and since this co. doesn’t undertake export business its
risks are less than those of some others. Also the depre-
ciation allowance (10 per cent, per annum) is substantial.
* * * *
The Fingall (W.A.) reef is still looking well in
the north end of the 13th level; the shoot has now been
exposed for a length of over 100 ft., and looks like con-
tinuing. The management thinks well of the discovery,
but is afraid to say too much about it for fear the values
will peter out in the next cut. Development is well worth
watching, as, if the shoot proves to be a permanent body,
it will quite alter the mine’s future, which had of late
days been desperately gloomy.
• • •
Boulder Perseverance (W.A.) is still looking
very sick in the bottom. The management does not say so,
but progress reports tells their own tale. Prices are being
kept up, and may go higher, on the strength of present
profits and yarns spun by optimists who have had a peep
below on visiting days. Nearly every other day in the
week the man in the street at Kalgoorlie is heard talking
about the richness of this or that face in the bottom
workings of this mine ; but as the assay plan is the only
guide to the value of Kalgoorlie ore, and as this precious
document is not shown to visitors at the Perseverance
office, the street-corner information can only be taken
for what it is worth—which is very little indeed.
* • * n
Much of the talk about low working costs in-
dulged in by Westralian mining cos. is mere flam. Thus
a certain Kalgoorlie co., which plumes itself on
economical working, charges ore obtained from driving,
rising, and winzing, to development, which is a capital
account. Having done so, it lumps the ore obtained
from this source with the stone drawn from stopes, and
figures out the costs on the total tonnage. Working ex-
penditure is thus made to look small for the time being ;
but the secret is betrayed at the end of each financial
year, when the accounts come to be made up, and the
heavy charge for development is shown. Yet the audi-
tors, knowing that the cost of development footage is
nearly double what it ought to be, gravely wink at the
whole farce.
* * * *
Free gold and telluride are showing in the ore
coming from 1350 ft. (or bottom) workings of the Kalgurli
(W.A.). The lode is 10ft. wide at this point, and published
assays are much below actual figures. Attention recently
directed to the development of new ground in some of
the higher levels of this mine has in each case led to the
discovery of fresh bodies of payable ore.
* * * *
Flotation of the balance of Kaufman’s Phillips
River (W.A.) specs still hangs fire. People at ’Ome are
afraid of the price of copper coming down with a run,
and there are so many better-looking plums ripe for
picking, that Kaufman may have to wait a long time
before a favorable.opportunity presents itself to get his
ventures off.
* * ■* *
Lady Shenton, Menzies (W.A.), is not worked
out yet. Two years ago it was acquired from the liqui-
dator for a few hundred pounds, and at that time it was
looked upon as worthless. The new owners, however,
have since taken out 3000 or 4000 ounces of gold, and
reckon on getting a lot more. But Menzies’ reefs have a
weakness for going wrong at shallow depths, and those
now being worked in the Lady Shenton will probably do
the same.
* * * *
Gwalia South, Leonora (W. A.), will reach the pro-
ducing stage in August. The co. has spent about £26,000
since acquiring the property, and will be about £2OOO in
debt by the time its new 10-head mill starts. Its main
shoot passes into the adjoining Sons of Gwalia mine, at a
vertical depth of 600 ft. or so from the surface. This
means that, unless a fresh find is made in the interim,
Gwalia South will be worked out in a few years from now.
Scientific and economical mining demanded the absorp-
tion of this lease by the Sons of Gwalia Co., but it is
stated that the Gwalia South people asked too high a
figure. With its fine surface equipment and financial
strength, Sons of Gwalia Co. could treat the ore much
more cheaply than the Gwalia South management can
ever hope to do, and if an amalgamation had been effected
there would have been much more profit to cut up.
* * * *
W. L. Baillieu has now gone off the Zinc Cor-
poration Board —possibly because practically all the
shares have been passed on to J. Bull. Quotations
(middle) are down to about 6s. 6d. ; they were last year
boomed up to 40s. R. Sticht, of Mt. Lyell, has been
visiting Broken Hill with Loring (of 8., M. and Co., the
managers) and the secretary of the co., so presumably he '
has been called in to see if he can give any hints that
may help the co. out of the hole into which it rushed
with so much enthusiasm.
* * * *
The “Sink” Corporation, Broken Hill, is at
present in the position of the man who, being blind-
folded, is told to turn round three times and grab. If he
catches anything, well and good. But he generally
doesn’t grab what he would like to. The jumbled-up
experimental plant on the British lease has been stopped
for the greater part of the past fortnight, and while it
was running, only 160 tons of tailings per day were being
put through. The Ullrich magnetic machine, which for a
long time tried desperately to justify its existence in
the building specially erected to accommodate it and
five more like it, has gone. It was intended for separating
the lead from the zinc concentrates produced by the
flotation process, and so it did ; but the Corporation
didn’t want to be in the position of the mountain labor-
ing to bring forth a mouse. It is also stated that the
Corporation purchased the six machines before it knew
much about them. In spite of all, however, the Corpora-
tion is pushing on with the main plant on the South
Blocks hill.
* * * *
The feeding-bottle stage of the big new mill
which the Sulphide Corporation has put up at the Cen %
tral mine, Broken Hill, seems to be passed, and all*
four sections are now doing good work. The new
slime-treatment plant, in which the Cattermole
granulation process is to be used, is about ready
for service ; and what with this and the other two
granulation plants, the magnetic plant, and the big lead
mill, the output from the Central should soon reach tall
figures. It certainly is time ordinary shareholders in the
Sulphide Corporation had a look in.
* * *
Development work at the north end of the
Junction, Broken Hill, where the ore body coming in
from the Junction North was tackled at the 900 ft. level,
has gone a long way towards proving that the latter mine
did not follow the true course of the ore body at the
937 ft. The lode seems to have taken a more v csterly
course, and co be making fairly big on the Junction side
of the boundary. If that turns out to be the oase, the
Junction is assured of a fine wide body from the 690 ft.
level down, and a good chance of a continuation south-
westerly towards Browne shaft. All the Junction ore
bodies run nearly east and west.
* * * *
The Junction North is the only co. along the
line of the Broken Hill lode which professes not to care
whether Broken Hill has a supplementary water scheme
or not. Having, as a regular thing, 50,000 gallons of
water from underground per day, and the means of
raising 70,000 gallons if need be, the Junction North pro-
fesses to view the situation with sublime indifference. It
is foolish, for everybody knows that it wouldn’t affect
this silly attitude if it had the money to help the supple-
mentary scheme.
* * * *
The mil] at the South Blocks, Broken Hill, is
now fairly at work, and for June the product was valued
on the mine at £12,661, from which £7463 working


expenses has to be deducted, leaving a profit of £5198.
The output was 6910 tons crude ore, so profit was 15s.
per ton. The concentrates (1297 tons) were worth 64J
per cent, for lead, or a total of 836 J tons, so on that basis
the profit was at the rate of £6 4s. 6d. per ton of lead.
As lead averaged something over £2l for the month, this
means that, so long as silver keeps its present price, the
co. can work without showing a loss on a lead market
running down to £l4 16s. per ton. There is nothing very
cheerful about that ; the margin is still much too small.
There are 200,000 shares in the co. ; and, as the past
month’s profit was at the rate of £62,376 a year, this
means 6s. 4d. per share. The present quotation is 355.
Evidently the market either miscalculates the present
position or expects the mine to do a good deal better in
the future.
* * * »
The Broken Hill mines have now over 9900
men on their local pay rolls ; these are exclusive of the
men at the Port Pirie and other smelting works. The
number easily constitutes a record for the silver-lead
industry in N.S. Wales.
* * * *
The earliest ex ploration of Lobb’s Hole (N. S. W. )
by the new co. hasn’t given very cheerful results. The
main level (120 ft.) was in 460 ft. west and 323 ft. east when
the co. took possession. West the vein, always narrow,
had pinched to a film, but east there was good ore in the
face. At 331 ft., however, the r, ef cut out, and though
14ft. further has been driven there is no sign of it making
again. Work laterally has therefore been stopped, and
preparations made to sink the shaft a further 100 ft. ; and
this entails a new' hauling and pumping plant. Presum-
ably the failure to get ore in the old level to help keep
the pot boiling will mean calls at an earlier date than had
been expected.
* * * *
Superintendent Patterson, of the New Hillgrove
Prop. (N.S. W.), values 25,000 tons of Eleanora stone at
£3 a ton ; costs he estimates at 305., so if the new mill
can keep step with the co.’s large share register, dividends
should eventuate. A small milling capacity with low grade
ore is obviously no use to such a big co. ; even the rich ore
from the New Hillgrove didn’t produce dividends quickly
enough w'ith a 10-head mill. For the time the New Hill-
grove seems to have become second fiddle to the Eleanora.
There is one thing to be said in favor of the New Hill-
grove—its rich patches can hardly be measured by an en-
gineer’s foot rule ; there probably is a lot of the yellow’
metal down below'—the metal that shareholders have
been dreaming about. Patterson’s opinion of the
Eleanora certainly sustains the idea that some of the
other reefs are w’orth another trial. Electric power and
modern machinery alter the conditions for the working
of mines, and it cannot be fairly said that Hillgrove deep
levels have proved disappointing, although the patchy
nature of the reef should curb any enthusiasm.

The much-threatened Broken Hill Water Supply
Co. —a Melbourne co.—feels a bit better again. Some
time ago a Bill was passed to constitute a Trust, formed
of the municipality and the mining cos. The Broken
Hill Prop, and Block 10 would not, however, come in ;
and when trouble fell on the Zinc Corporation, and the
Junction North didn’t burst out into the expected
affluence and dividends, the Trust found that the neces-
sary capital w'asn’t forthcoming to carry through the
Umberumberka scheme. Block 10, it was supposed,
wouldn’t join in because its Board is very friendly with
the Water Co.’s Board; the B.H. Prop.’s attitude was,
however, less explainable. But now it is pretty plain that
the big co. stood.out because it reckoned, that the rest
would get into ditficulties without its help, w'hereupon it
would be able to command its ow'n terms. Its own terms
turn out to be separate representation on the Trust ; and
the chances are that it will be granted. That sort of thing
is very' characteristic of B.H. Prop, management. It is a
giant, and uses its strength like a giant.
♦ * * *
Queensland dividends for the first half of this
year amounted to £215,713, or £31,045 less than for the
first half of 1906. In the first half of 1903 the dividends
reached £463,109, and in the corresponding part of 1904
they were £429,680 ; so that the past half-year’s divi-
dends are barely half of those paid in January-June,
1904, and a good deal less than half of those for January-
June, 1903. There are people, though, who think that
Kidston has had all the luck. He certainly has benefited
by pretty good seasons in the pastoral country, but the
mining fields have gone back on him very badly.
• * * *
Stannary Hills Co. (Q.), having got rid of most
of its floating liability during the past eighteen months,
is now turning its attention to its short-dated first-mort-
gage debentures. At the end of March, 1906, the former
liabilities (mostly loans issued on the security of deben-
ture stock) ran to £43,264 ; at March 31, 1907, they were
down to £14,913, and that lot has since been wiped out.
There still remains, however, £17,375 of the first mortgage
stock ; and as it carries 10 per cent., it is a burden well
worth getting rid of. Fortunately, the co. can get rid of
it any time, and profits are being saved up now for that
purpose. By the end of the year it will be wiped out; it
will nominally disappear before then, but the co. will
have to borrow for the purpose. After that, however,
there will still remain £63,850 net of 6 per cent, first
mortgage debentures ; and they do not fall due till 1921.
In addition to interest, they early a sinking fund
which will call for something like £4OOO a year. The
recent profit has been about £23,500 a half-year, after
paying interest, but without allowing anything for
depreciation. And now the sinking fund before men-
tioned will have to come out of profits. The capital,
apart from debentures, is £458,150, which is a frightful
burden. Even 8 per cent, on that calls for £36,652 a
year, and, especially during a tin boom, 8 per cent, is a
ridiculously low rate for a mine which can’t see further
ahead thaii Stannary Hills. Of course shares are not
at par nowadays : £1 paids are worth about ss. Bd. But
there is a large risk even in that.
* * * *
The camel is a much scarcer nightmare on
North Queensland tracks than he was a while ago.
Though he had the advantage of being managed by
Abdul YVade, who ought to be the most experienced
camel-man in Australia, the pathetic-looking beast was a
failure up there ; and the O.K. Co., which first used him
on a large scale, has discarded him altogether. Even
Abdul Wade, before he left, had put on eight traction
engines to perform his carrying contract; and when
Wade left in anger and bitterness, the co. bought his en-
gine team, and has since put five more on the awful
track, which is a foot deep in dust when it isn’t two feet
deep in mud.
* * * *
The war of the rival routes for the Etheridge
(Q.) line isn’t over yet ; though so far there seems very
little doubt that the Almaden to Charleston route, which
will serve the Einasleigh mines, will be adopted. The
difference in distance between the Charleston and
Brodie’s Gap routes is something like 40 miles in favor of
the latter, and in estimated cost about £80,000; but
reports as to probable traffic favor the longer and more
expensive line, and anyhow there is much more influence
that way. So, according to Chief Engineer Pagan, of the
Queensland railways, the Chillagoe Co., which has to
find the money in the first place, will probably adhere to
the Charleston and Einasleigh route.
* * * *
The Mitchell River Co. was formed over six
months ago to work a long-known-of big gold-antimony
body in the remote baekblocks of North Queensland—at
a place so remote that places like O.K. seem, in compari-
son, to be near the centre of things. Over half the time
since the co. was formed the wet season has made
Mitchell River more remote than ever ; so, between the
long delay and the slump in antimony that has happened
meantime, the 10s. shares, which were about three times
over-applied for when the co. was formed, have shrivelled
down to 6s. 6d., or thereabouts. The whole thing rests
upon the success of a process for separating the antimony
and the gold, which the McArthurs, the Glasgow metal-
lurgical people, have evolved, and for which this co. has
the Queensland rights; and the McArthurs are heavily
interested in the co. So far only preparatory'
work has been done on the claim (the plant
is to be erected there) ; tracks have been cut, lime and
bricks made, and a main shaft started. There is prac-
tically no doubt about the ore being there in quantity ;
but there has been some doubt abput the high gold
assays reported. Yet a sample (taken from the place

Reports are current at Cloncurry that Mt.
Elliott, at 185 ft. level, is on top of a fresh make of ore of
fair quality and quantity. The sinking of the main shaft
from the 185 ft. level has just been commenced.
* * * *
“Quartpot ’’ : At the Queensland Exploration
Co.’s Ct. Australian Mine, Cloncurry, operations are of
the antediluvian type. There are about 10 bosses to
some 40 men. The two winding compartments of the
shaft appear to be of different dimensions, and the haul-
ing is done in a very primitive way. Also there are pro-
bably safer ways. At present there are no signs of
modern methods; so that between this mine and the
exploded Lady leases expenditure must run high, and
a big hole must already have been dug in the £65,000
working capital originally provided. The diamond drill
is now at work on the Great Australian, and is down over
100 ft. Should this prove the existence of copper under
Mount Pisa, it will give a much-needed lift
to the field. Great secrecy is being preserved
with regard to results. A 7ft. galvanised iron
fence has been erected round the drill. It re-
minds one of the early Coolgardie boomster methods.
This is in marked contrast to the way things are run in
connection with the Freeholds Co. Small prospecting
shafts to prove the Argylla and Just-in-Time lodes are
being sunk, and others are to be put down on the Cru-
sader and Dobbin. Manager Nicolson (brother of “ Bob,”
of Westralia’s Ivanhoe) also does his own secretarial
work.
* * * *
Last Vulcan (Q.) tin crushing returned 17 tons
of concentrates, valued at £1570, from 556 tons of ore ;
and there is nothing overpowering in a return of less
than £3 a ton, with costs on rather a high scale, and with
264,000 shares to divide profits amongst. The mine has
had its labor troubles, and that may account for the small
output ; but labor troubles don’t reduce the tin value of
the stone. Last year some cr'ushings were worth £6 per.
ton. The output, under normal circumstances, is about
800 tons a month, which is small for a co. in which it
takes £3300 to pay threepence per share. The 1050 ft.
level (the deepest) is turning out some fair ore for the
work of 11 men with a machine drill; but ore from the
deep levels has to be roasted, as it contains pyrites.
Some exceptionally good specimens of tin ore from the
950 ft. level are in J. Moffat’s collection. There has been
some enthusiasm, backed up by N. Queensland, about
the Vulcan, founded, of course, on its past history ; but
there is no easier way of falling-in in mining than to
back past records.
* * * *
Great Fitzroy copper mine (the old Mt.
Chalmers, which Doolette lately got big money into) is
about two miles from the railway line that runs down
from Rockhampton (Q.) to the mouth of the Fitzroy.
The show is big so far as quantity goes ; the cone of ore
seems to widen out as it gets deeper, and estimates are
based on measurements 260 ft. by 120 ft. Costs have been
reckoned as £1 17s. 6d. and £2 by different experts ; but
if the pyritic and silicious ore is as rich and as easily treated
as alleged, why costs should be so high is a puzzle. The
average value of the ore has been estimated at £3 2s. per
ton, with copper at £BO ; it would seem likely that the
estimates of value are as optimistic as the mining con-
science will permit. Working assays from the mine’s
pyritic ore run about 4 per cent, copper and 4dwt. of
gold, but the width of ore from which the assays are
taken is limited. Ore valued at about £3, and costs esti-
mated at £2, is riot too attractive a proposition, even
when value is based on copper at £BO a ton.
* » * **
Wolfram has again climbed to a big price ; sales
have been made “ori the ground” in North Queensland
at £l7O a ton. The Pascoe, in the Far North —Cape York
way —is producing some wolfram, and as there is talk
that the field is good, the Mines Department is sending up
Warden Cherry to have a look.
* » * *
Mount Morgan (Q.) is one of the few mines
whose reports go on improving. On the misleading
statements that were published about the exaggerated
deal in the shares six months ago, the quotations touched
925. ; but that was mere froth, and in a survey of the
movements of the stock may be disregarded. In Novem-
ber, when most Australian mining stocks were at their
top, the best price for Morgans was 775. 6d. —a fine
steady improvement of 10s. or 12s. during the year.
Since November, while nearly all other shares have
weakened, Morgans have firmed, and a sale was made at
88s. at the beginning of this week. This was partly on
the strength of the annual report, partly on an increase
of 55 tons copper and 4980 z. gold in the last monthly
out-turn from the copper smelters compared with the
previous month’s record. The year’s revenue (the co.’s
year closes at the end of May) was £934,432; compare
that with the value of the output for the past six years
1901 .. £693,273 1904 .. £467,428
1902 .. 570,337 1905 .. 518,115
1903 .. 566,401 1906 .. 482,801
The expenditure amounted to £571,619, leaving £362,813
profit, less depreciation. During the year, £162,500 was
paid in dividends, and £62,500 (Is. 3d. per share) is now
to be paid, making £225,000 for the year ; and £50,000
goes to a formal reserve fund, and £30,000 to create an
insurance reserve.
• * * *
From tbe S.M. Herald, summarising the Mt.
Morgan (Q.) report:—“ The creation of an insurance fund
will enable the co. to carry its own risks in the future,
and save large sums, paid hitherto annually in premiums.”
Yet when the Government of N.S. Wales, which engages
in a vastly bigger business than the Mt. Morgan Co., de-
cided to “ carry its own risks in the future, and save large
sums,” the S.M. Herald said it was absurd; and when
Carruthers collared the accumulated fund, and knocked
the State’s self-insurance scheme on the head, the S.M.
Herald had no brick to throw at him, but instead made
the usual remarks about Socialism and its dangers.

Net proceeds £36,520 £96,326 £168,123
Looking at this table with a cold eye, it can only be said
that the co. has more to thank the price of metal for in
the matter of profits earned than it has to bless
the mine. In the short space of about two years
tin has gone up £5B per ton. It can therefore be
truly said that Providence has tempered the wind
to the lambs of Bowes Kelly’s flock, which rushed
into tin after that shrewd, shock-headed Irishman had
shown the way by buying about 30,000 shares on his own
account. The co.’s figures are a little complicated,
because Briseis takes 52 per cent, of the gross earnings
from the Brothers No. 1 as a return for working that
claim. The 1906 results from all sources, however, were
£168,123 net. Mine costs panned out at £2B 2s.
per ton of metal —this includes cost of removing over-
burden —or about £3 per ton less than in 1905.
Many Briseis’ supporters had fondly imagined that, now
that the big lump of overburden on their own mine is
pretty well off, the overburden costs would fall with a
pleasant thud. Nothing of the kind ! The co. is now
going to pour water on to the newly-bought Ringa-
rooma bank of basalt and clay—as big a job as
ever Lewis, Hawley and Clark had to tackle
when they faced the overburden on the Briseis
and Brothers Home claims. Consequently, the re-
duced costs on the Briseis’ side will be more than
counterbalanced by the outlay on the new block.
Figures given show that there is 1,700,000 cubic
yards of drift left in the Briseis. Taking 410,000
cubic yards as the average year’s work, this is
four years’ life for the mine. Dividends are 9d.
a quarter with tin at £lB4, so that on the
basis of what is in sight the present price of shares
(12s. Bd.) is full value. This is throwing in rich
drift to bring up past average returns. Then there is the
fact that the forward market for tin is so much lower
than the spot price, which marks an uneasy state of
affairs in the metal market. The Board disbursed
£112,500 in divs. (and a ninepenny bonus) last year, and
also wisely put £20,000 to reserve, and carried forward
£42,000. Financially the show is sound enough, but it is
the high price of tin that makes the prudent buyer
hesitate.
A wail of woe has, been heard from J. P.
Madden because Mt. Lyell Extended (Tas.) had its lease
forfeited. The Extended has been shepherded for the
best part of this century—at all events, since Lonergan
ceased to make the market hum. If it had been
in almost any other State save Tasmania, the claim
would have been on the scrap heap long since. \\ hat the
co. has been doing it would be hard to say ; of mining
there certainly hasn’t been anything worth mentioning.
It has been a case of “ wait on my neighbor the Consols,”
and the Consols hasn’t got enough ore proved to be worth
mentioning either. So far as demonstrated worth goes,
the Extended is in a worse plight. So why should any
hardened old miner drop a tear over the loss of pro-
prietorship ? t
The Colebrook, a Tasmanian co., formed to
work a copper show near the Emu Bay railway, has got
tram connection nearly made with that line ; and, vvhile
development work is proceeding at the mine, excavations,
etc., for smelter are being made. But it looks as though,
except at the top of the market, work will have to be
done on a larger scale than is contemplated in order to
show a profit on the comparatively poor ore so far ex-
posed. And the co. hasn’t yet satisfied everybody that
it is in a position to bustle on with the smelters.

“G.L.M.”: Be Magnet (Tas.). The capital of
the co. is £4OOO, of which nothing has been called up;
the £15,000 which Bulletin mentions was raised by sell-
ing 15,000 shares at a premium of £1 to raise funds to
build a tramline to connect with the Emu Bay railway.
When that line was completed the co. had an overdraft
of £7200 ; it wiped that off in 12 months, with silver at
Is. 11 Jd., and lead at £ll 9s. 2d. With respect to pro-
spective profits, as the co. has made considerable profits
with silver and lead at these low values, on ore won from
the comparatively small ore bodies of the upper levels,
and without the assistance of the concentrat-
ing mill, it is feasible to deduce that, with
a lode 40ft. wide in the present lowest levels,
and containing proportionately richer ore, there should
be no difficulty in keeping up profits, especially as work-
ing expenses have been reduced some £l3OO per annum
bv the installation of a high-level water scheme, de-
veloping 200-horse power. The upper levels are still pro-
ducing ore, and have been stoped for a distance of 1300 ft.,
with ore still in the faces going north and south. The
Magnet has produced and sold over £200,000 worth of
ore, and is in the unique position of havingpaid for its
own outfit (except the tramline) out of the profits, which
record is second to none among the silver mines in this
State. Moreover, with a capital of £4OOO, of which no-
thing has been called up, the shareholders have received
£lO,OOO in d vidends, with another £2OOO payable this
month. . In addition, the mine never had so much ore
actually in sight as at present. The gross value of last
week’s output was £lB4O. The pay sheet equals £26,000
a year, and the conveyance of ore another £12,600 ; and
there is the usual large outlay on general requisites. In
short, it is a pity Tasmania has not a few more little
Magnets. Therefore, although the Magnet has never been
what may be called a brokers’ mine, there appears to be
no reason why shares should not be good value at 30s.
* * * *
“Coot”: Tasmanian Copper Co., Rosebery
(Tas.), may develop into something big. The diamond
drill indicates that the ore-bodies at 400 ft. maintain their
width and improve in quality. Contracts with the Tas-
manian Smelting Co. have been met mainly out of
development work. Three levels are opened up, and very
little stoping has yet been done. Another adit is being
driven conjointly by this and the Primrose Co., which
will give another 140 ft. of backs. Deeper levels cannot
be got without sinking, and it is proposed to go down on
the underlie from this level. At present metal values,
ore is worth about £4 to £6 for gold, silver and lead ; the
zinc (£5) and copper (10s.) are not allowed for.
•** , *
Like the dove that was sent out from the
ark, Barrambie Ranges (W.A.)is looking for a refuge, and
there is none in sight. To date the co. has not put up a
record that says much for the vaunted system of sampling.
Had it done ‘so, there is no telling where the bears
would have been. As it is, they are licking their chops
in sweet content at the marrow they have extracted
from the bones of the co. ; for shares which they sold at
£1 they can pick up at 7s. Four stages marked Barram-
hie Ranges : (1) The flotation, slow and doubtful; (2)
the report of Lynch and Thompson ; (3) the retirement
of manager Aldridge ; (4) his reinstatement, and re-
iteration that this was a mine of 3oz. stone. Lynch and
Thompson visited the mine twice, and on the second
occasion in no way modified their first eulogistic report.
To their credit, though, they said more development was
wanted. The market evidently thinks that it is still
wanted. Shareholders, meantime, are brandishing the
axe for the man who estimated that £90,000 worth of
gold was in sight. His only chance to stay execution
would be to prove that the difference between the gold
already won and that which he said was blocked out is still
underground, or has been taken by thosegold-thieves whom
so much has been talked about. Melbourne broker Hamil-
ton, who was attacked for casting doubt on the mine, has
his clients out, no doubt, and so he can, after all, smile
the smile of the man who has hit the bull’s-eye. The
Board’s duty is, as Aldridge repeats his belief in the
mine, to have his estimate checked by some competent
outsider—Oowper, of the Black Range, for instance.
* * » *
New Dempsey, Gaffney’s Creek (Vic.), has hit
the reef in the old shaft, but has not hit the gold. It
may be got by driving, but there isn’t the same confidence
in the staff as there was when it got flooded out of the
winze. Thus another slap has been given to a sturdy lot
of investors who planked down their coin bravely.
* •» * •
On the Walhalla-road there is a blood-curdling
drop, which travellers know well, and near it is the copper
mine on Thompson River. Here are the remains of a
show worked 25 years back, from a tunnel driven from
the bank of the Thompson, as it cuts its way through a
vast gorge to the sea. The side of the hill is copper-
stained, and there are stories that 5000 tons of 10 per
cent, ore came out of the claim. Walhalla people have
taken hold of the old mine, reopened the tunnel, and
got “Geology” Dunn to come along and in-
spect. He tells them they have a chance not
only with copper, but with platinum in situ.
The gods at Walhalla have followed up Dunn’s report
by getting Bendigo and Melbourne capitalists to fork out
£3OOO to revive the old show. The cash is little enough ;
still, water and fuel are cheap. So if the ore is there,
Victoria will not be long before it can add another metal
to its rather scanty list.
* * * *
South German, Maldon (Vic.), is heaping up a
lot of disappointment. The reckoning is, nevertheless,
slow, because Maldon backs its views against the market,
and, as the bears cannot get scrip, values hold. Looked at
from east, west, north, or south, there isn’t a reason why
the co. should stand as it does. Nelson’s, or the w'est
reef, pans out about 4dwt. dirt without counting pyrites
and gold in sand. With these thrown in, there,
have not been divs. enough to provide a dead-beat with
a week’s shelter at a lodging-house. If good stone had
been hit in the 2000 ft. crosscut, at least one good
word could have been said. But it is as hard a
job to get the reef there as it is to find consist-
ency in G. Reid’s politics. The crosscut went West
about 300 ft. and hit nothing. Then they came
back, followed a leader south, and again got nothing.
Now it is proposed to dog’s-hind-leg from this level so as
to get under the winze from the 1900 ft., on thechancethat
a lode track which was passed through in the crosscut at
277 ft. is the missing reef. This grop ng recalls the former
search after the main German reef, when it was cut off on
the German Deep boundary at about 1700 ft. There may
be more than a coincidence in the difficulty of hitting the
two parallel formations at about the same depth.

Agricultural Minister O’Lougblin (S.A.) threat-
ens to take action if the alleged wheat ring, which is
keeping down the price to farmers, continues to pay less
than the bucolic person can get in other States. There
is talk of the State acting as wheat buyer, and shipping
the grain to England, to be disposed of by the Govern-
ment produce agent there. The Westralian Government
feels the same way, and is fixing up a scheme for making
advances against shipments—which is a mighty risky
business, by the way, as Win. McMillan and his Farmers'
Co-operative Society (N.S.W.) could tell, if they felt like
recalling unpleasant memories.
The Aerial Tramways which have been erected
for the British and Sulphide Corporation (Central) Mines
at Broken Hill are fine examples of present-day skill and
technical knowledge. The Mines will effect considerable
saving by means of these Aerialways as compared with
any other known mechanical contrivance for handling
ores on a large scale. The British Mine will economise
to over 2s. per ton. These two plants have already been
subjected to severe tests, and have amply demonstrated
their worth and ability. Both Aerialways were supplied
by W. & J. Lampriere, of Melbourne, who represent A.
Bleichert & Co., of London, the largest firm in the world
for the manufacture of transport appliances.*

Two sets of Improved Plunger Pumps, Cornish
type, are at present being constructed for the Victorian
Deep Leads G.M. Company (Messrs Bewick, Moreing and
Co.). Each set will consist of two lifts 250 ft., the total
lift being 500 ft. The Plungers are 26in. in diameter, and
each set of two delivers into one rising main, the dis-
charge per day 7 being 5,000,000 gallons. The valve boxes
are made in separate pieces, and the valves are of the
double flap type. The total weight is 150 tons. There is
no balance weight in the bob, but the one set of pumps
balances the other. The Austral Otis Engineering Com-
pany have this work in hand. This Company has con-
structed and erected most of the big volume, deep
alluvial pumping plants now working in Victoria.*

N.S. W. Commissioners of Taxation (here-
after called “The Tax”) v. Mooney is'the latest
example of the lust for litigation which fills the
N.S.W. State Departments. Mooney sold a mine
fur £9OOO. He seems to have been an impecunious
beggar with asuddeu stroke of luck, for not having
had an income when he sold the mine he didn’t
send in a return,to The Tax. The Tax, however,
claimed income-tax on the whole £9OOO. Mooney
had an idea that something was wrong. He had
sold his only asset, and he thought the money was
capital—riot income. He reckoned that if a tax
commissioner sold his house and garden he
wouldn’t regard the entire proceeds as income.
Anyhow, Justice Simpson rejected the demand
for tax on £9OOO. So did the Full Court. So did
the High Court.. Against this unanimous Aus-
tralian verdict The Tax appealed to the Privy
Council on the other side of the globe. The P.
Council has now given an extraordinary decision
—a nominal verdict for a nominal amount, leav-
ing The Tax to pay all the costs. The verdict is
for a ha’penny or so, and costs are said to be'
something awe-inspiring.

“ Hamer ” : Somebody mentioned recently
a down-trodden eat whose outcry led to the cap-
ture of a burglar, and suggested that cats should
be attached to every window as burglar traps. I
know a watch-cat, which is equal to any dog on
earth in this line. The owner, exhibiting the
creature’s powers, told it in a casual way to “ turn
me out,” and that cat was an agitated mass of
extended fur, rolled round an excited seething of
swear-language, in about a second. Also, it made
a dash to annihilate me, and its owner saved me
by a short head. Have a wholesome respect for
dogs; but I fancy a cat in a fury, and sustained
by a noble sense of duty, would be a more uncom-
fortable circumstance than any mere canine.

Item from the Richmond River Express :
Out at Dandaloo some years since they had an aboriginal
tracker who wore the orthodox policeman’s cap. One
day in the bar of the local pub a compatriot was playing-
a concertina when the tracker came along and said,
“Here, stop that playin’.” At the instigation of the land-
lord, the instrument was laid aside, the darkies had a
drink and the tracker started to go —but before he left
the bar he said, “ You can go on with the music now-
just wanted to show my audority.”
In which respect he was just like .Toe Carruthers
over the Australian capital, hut Joe hasn’t the
honest frankness of his black brother.
“ Janor” : “G.S.K.” presents “Back-
blox” with a hunk of truth (B. 13/6/’O7) re tariff
of wayside hotels at the back of Broken Hill.
“Backblox” should get more in touch with his
pen-name, and then he’ll learn to bite the half-
crown meal. He will meet it in the far N.-West,
and, once seen, it is easily recognised, for there is
a strong family likeness discernible in the half-
crown lay-out of the back wilderness. Its chief
features are damper, black tea, and tinned mis-
sionary. I struck the limit in hotels in that
region. Its tariff was: Meals and beds, 3s. ;
drinks, Is. ; bottle of beer, ss. The house con-
tained three rooms. Sleeping accommodation con-
sisted of bag-stretchers lined up in a bough shed.
This hostelry and a store and post-office constituted
the township. During our prospecting operations
in the vicinity it was our postal town, and once a
month a delegation from the camp rode in to get
the mail. Also it got beer and the three bob tin
menu. First trip, asked Bung why he didn’t get
beef. There was no one to send the 40 miles that
intervened between beef and beer, he explained.
We had plenty of meat in camp, for we were
buying it alive from a station in the neighbor-
hood. We knocked a “fat” over with the rifle
as required, and broke it down, and salted it in
the hide, and at times made biltong. So the
mail messengers regularly bore to the township a
huge round of beef, and handed it over to the cook,
who was also the barman, and the licensee, and
the boots, and during our stay we feasted on it,
and the ptomaine and its cousins left not the fast-
nesses of their soldered tins. That was our only
gain, for in spite of the cheap beef we supplied,
Bung’s charge to us altered not. When Bung’s
attention was drawn to this anomaly in the
tariff, he snorted, “Garni ain’t I got ter cook
the blooming stuff ? ”
* * * *
“ Gidgee ” : “G.S.K.” (B. 13/6/’O7) re
hotels met with in Outback coach travelling, must
have Wandered from Broken Hill to Wompah in
the pre-Adamite age. I back up “ Backblox” and
say there are half-crown hotels along that route.
I frequently travel the road. At most of the
hotels coach passengers are charged 2s. 6d. I’ve
paid it at Euriowie, Fowler’s Gap, and Bancannia,
and 2s. at Iduna Park, Cobham Lake, Milpa-
rinka, and Tibooburra. There is only one hotel
on the road where Is. is charged—Yanco Glen.
Private dining-rooms don’t exist at Yanco Glen,
Tarrawingee, Euriowie, Fowler’s Gap, Bancannia,
Iduna Park, Gobham Lake, or Yalpunga. Ask
for a private room at some of them, and they’d
send for the police to have your bumps read.
“ G.S.K.” is wrong again re gates. Between
Wompah and Tibooburra there are four gates,
Tibooburra and Milparinka/owr gates, Milparinka
and Cobham Lake five gates, and this number is
the average between stages. The coach passenger
every time opens the gate. The camel teams have
not their own “ pads.” Ask Phil Tuck, Paddy
Holmes, or Billy Cranston, the three coach-drivers
on the route from the Hill to Tibooburra.
“ G.S.K.” wants to get up and contradict before
he knows his subject. Some of the hotelkeepers
do not study the wants of the travelling public as
regards liquor. At Milparinka did a “ perish ”
several times at one pub—no whisky, no nothing,
except flies and goat. Wanted other things than
goat; was offered tinned dog or kangaroo rat. At
Cobham the only liquor they had was Row’s
bitters, and the driver and I each had two for
novelty's sake. They had had nothing else on
sale for a fortnight. Now, “G.S.K.”, contradict
this, or take a back seat with your pal Judkins !
*•* * *
“Judex” : The greatest curse out-back
(and inside, as well) is the blighted and dissolute
lunatic who, in reply to a stranger’s query, says :
“ Foller the track yer on, an’ take the fust turnin’
ter the left. Yer carn’t miss it; there ain’t no
other road.” The pilgrim does as directed, and
finds that the first turning takes him three miles
to a dead end ; it is simply a wood-carter s track.
He returns, and takes the second turning, going
for two miles to the stump of a very large tree,
and lots of chips and boughs. Comes back, and
takes third turning, with same result. Camps
there, as it is now too late to get back to the
main road, and his evening prayer is an earnest
supplication for the blood of the director.
At daylight, a hungry and angry man and a
starving horse regain the main road and take four
more left turnings, three of them leading to large
stumps, and the fourth bringing him to the wood-
carters themselves. The latter give the informa-
tion that “these ’ere tracks of ourn ain’t con-
sidered turnin’s; the right branchin’ spot is two
mile furder on, an’ is plain enough ’cos there’s the
skelletin uv a bullick’s ’ead stuck in a gidgee
there.” It is to be sincerely hoped that the wraith
of the man who tells you to turn to the left will
be specially cared for in Hades, and that he will
for all eternity be misdirected as to the road to
the tank.
m * * *
“ Scipio ” : I have a nomination that
knocks everything else in the bush pest competi-
tion into a cooked belltopper. It is the Dago
who hawks his cheap poison—called “ pinky ” in
these parts—among the men on big construction
works in the north-east of Victoria. I have been
working on a big job in the north-east, where we
had 200 men employed, and their camps were
dotted at intervals along 20 miles of line. My
particular pest had a vineyard, and he used to
make stuff that would promptly turn the quietest
man into a dangerous lunatic. He knew better
than to come on to the works when the bosses
were about, but he used to drive along to the
men’s camps, and leave the stuff in their tents,
whether they had ordered it or not. The men,
coming in tired from their work at 5 o’clock, were
almost sure to drink it. It was not at all un-
pleasant to take, but the after effects were deadly.
I have seen some of the poor devils violently ill
for three days after a night out on it. The Dago’s
own countrymen knew too much to tackle his
“ pinky,” and it was generally the Australian
bushie that was his victim. And to see two or
three of these Dago wine-merchants following the
pay cart on the first Saturday in every month
used to remind me of half-a-dozen crows following
a sick sheep.

LADY OF THE HOUSE (who has been roused by a terrific hammering at the door): “ Wot
d’yer want?"
WANDERING DRUNK : “Could yer (hie) kin’ly tell me the time?"
LADY OF THE HOUSE : “ Wot! Ye’ve ’ad the check to mist me hout o’ me bed at twenty
mit dies past five ter tell yer the time! I wont.”
Ramsay Smith, head of the Health Depart-
ment of S. Australia, is finding out things about
the mosquito for the special benefit of the insect
in the Northern Teiritory. The particular little
beast that transmits malaria is the anopheles;
he has a fragile body, spotted wings, and a long
and thick dart, and on a flat surface stands nearly
on his head. He usually works by night. He is
susceptible to colors —prefers navy blue, dark red,
brown, scarlet, black, slate-grey, dark green,
violet, leaf green and blue in that order, and hates
white, orange, and yellow. That is why theTJ.S.
army has abandoned the regulation shirt of navy
blue” in favor of white for service in malarial
districts. Also the little brute knows at least
one note of music ; Hiram Maxim used to sum-
mon a congregation of male mosquitoes (but never
a female) by sounding a note similar to that
shrilled by the female mosquito. The female
mosquito is the vocalist. In fact, the male (except
for being a male) is a harmless sort of beast. He is
almost exclusively a vegetarian. It is the female
who, besides disturbing his rest, sucks the blood
of man. And experiments show that she often
carries on business for a year at least.

“A.G.,” from the south isle of Maori-
land :
Cocky wanted bridge over small creek near his shanty.
Arranged with two “ bushwhackers ” (who were felling
his timber) to erect same, in spare time, for 30s. Bridge
being half-finished, he spake:
“Thirty bob’s too stiff for that bit av a bridge, b ys.
I’ll give ve a quid.” Agreed, after demur.
Bridge completed. Cocky interviews builders thusly :
“ Begad, b’ys, that quid’s no use to you chaps. I’ll act
the square thing by you. You can have the run of that
turnip-field—till ye go.” As the bushwhackers couldn’t
afford law, this was eventually agreed upon, with a great
deal of demur.
Bridge erected, at practically no cost to cocky, turnips
being a drug in the market.
County Council’s accounts for that half-year show :
“ Contribution towards bridge over Cocky Creek, £5.”

“ Ses ” : The spread of modern fashions.
An aborigine entered a lawyer’s office in Grafton
(N.S.W.), theotherday. “Well, Jacky, what’s your
troubles?” Jacky spat sideways on to the floor,
put his hands on the table, and crossed his feet.
“You know my gin?’’ he asked. “How the
devil would I know her ? ” Gent One demanded.
“Baal yo’ been meet her longa” “No,
I haven’t met her.” Jacky laughed good-
humoredly. “ That one named Biddy.” “Well,
what about her ” “ Yo’ see, when I been marry
it Biddy, we make um ’greement.” “What
agreement ? ” “We ’gree Biddy hab it only one
pfeller piccaninny. Dis country too much
plurry no good keep it big family. Yo
understand?” “Well?” Jacky spat impres-
sively on the other side of him. “Now,
Biddy cubbon sick yesterday, an’ she catch um
two pfeller piccaninny.” Jacky held up two
fingers. “ Two pfeller in a heap. I yabberlonga
Biddy: ‘What for yo’do it like it this? Who
gib it yo’ p’mission hab um two pfeller?’ She
laugh, an’ say dat pfeller dwin.” “Yes?”
“ D_— dwin!” J; cky went on. “Baal mine
want it dwin.” “ Well, what do you want?”
“ Mine want it d’vorce—all same white pfeller.
She been ruin it me.” Jacky straightened, and
hitched up his bifurcated garment. “By golly, I
tshake it her up. Yo’ take um steps now, Boss,
quick’n lively. I bin teach her to plurry well
disobey me. My word ! ”

“ Pluto ” : It is not necessary to rush off
to our limestone caves to study stalactitic forma-
tions, for we have on view at our own doors
stalactites up to 2iin. in length. Under the
arches of the stone bridge of the new Sydney
railway station numerous hanging pendants or
stalactites may be observed. They have been
formed by water carrying in solution the lime in
the mortar through the interstices of the masonry.
When the drop of water reaches the surface
it pauses, prior to falling on the floor, and
the pause is sufficient to precipitate a
small portion of the carbonate of lime, and
in course of time a small hanging stalactite
is formed. Cave-guides are very fond of im-
pressing on a visitor the appalling length of time
necessary to form these stalactitic growths, some
of their estimates running into millions of years ;
but, with these examples before us, they will
have to considerably curtail some of their esti-
mates, more especially as at the Bondi sewer we
have stalactites hanging from the roof a foot or
more in length, formed since the opening of the
sewer 14 years ago.

“G.S.K.”: The Afghan may be insani-
tary, but, according to his lights, he is truly
religious. Was staying at Mount Poole station,
on Sturt’s Pocky Glen, not long ago. There was
a camel team loading at the woolshed. The cook
of the camp had made his “ batch ” in the camp
oven usually carried by the team, and had left the
whole to cool ; but the homestead pigs happened
on the.cooling stuff, and soon after the Afghans
discovered the unclean animal with its collective
nose deep in their camp oven. The Afghans de-
stroyed the remainder of the bread, and came
straight to the homestead in search of a new oven,
for which they paid liberally. Yet I didn’t even
hear them swear. They took it as the inscrutable
decree of Allah.

“WHAT’S IN A NAME?”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Some folks is alius full o’ fads and fancies
In this funny ol’ world to-day,
Erbout the names they’ve got ter worry on with,
The pickin’ of ’em turns ’em gray.
It don’t do nowadays ter be pertic’ler,
I’m sure that you will say the same,
But I’m satisfied that Mrs. Benno Wiggins
Is a terrabul pretty ol’ name.
There’s a fambly with three ol’ maids already,
Livin’ in a back street in town,
The whole crowd jolly nearly took hysterics
At the cheek of a chap named Brown.
He had the hide ter come an’ arst fer Hilda,
She threw a seven on the spot,
But if he’d had a name like Montmorency,
He could ha’ scooped the bloomin’ lot.
There’s girls in town is losin’ heaps o’ chances,
No matter what their mas may say.
Because a chap ain’t got the name they fancies,
They tells him he can slide away.
Blokes haven’t all got names like Aide Deakin,
Georgie Reid is toffier’n some,
But when a girl is risin' nine-an’-twenty,
She’d better take ’em as they come.
A feller’s got ter take the name they gives him,
They never lets him take his pick,
So what’s the use o’ havin’ fads an’ fancies,
Jes’ now, when fellers ain’t too thick.
It don’t do nowadays to be pertic’ler,
I think that you will say the same,
But I’m sat’sfied that Mrs. Benno Wiggins,
Is a terrabul pretty ol’ name.
N.S.W.
