“ Hexapoda ” :
The following in support of David H. Richards’ ant
campaign yarn (B. 13/6/’O7) :—Spent Christmas holidays
(’O2) at Sutton Forest. Used to g o i n daily to post-office by
a bush track skirting- a succession of beefeater ant-beds.
Noticed one morning that numbers of the beefeaters were
engaged in a death strife with myriads of the tiny black
ants so often met with in our jam-dish and sugar-basin
I passed on, feeling sorry for the little ants’ chance, and
mentally paid a tribute of admiration to their insan°
pluck. That afternoon, passing- the same way, I found
the track thickly strewn with the corpses of the beef-
ants, whilst the small species were evidently in possession
of the neighboring nest.
A regular war was in progress, which lasted for many
days, and hours of my time were spent observing it. The
little ants invariably commenced the attack. The
instructions issued to them by their commander were
simple : “ Get hold of the end of a beef-ant’s leg, and hang
on.” The procedure always caused the beef-ant the
utmost amusement —at first. Leaning well back on his
other five legs, he smiled good-humoredly at the audacity
of the little cuss, and after gratifying the youngster’s
curiosity as to the formation of his toes, advised him to
go away and play. Suddenly he realised that the young
’un meant to be Vicious, and as the matter had advanced
beyond a joke he sat down and proceeded to give the
cheeky attacker some much-needed chastisement. His
mandibles did splendid execution, but still the little
wretch hung on. Presently there was nothing but a
chewed-up mass f sugar-ant, and this the Other tried in
vain to detach from the end of his leg. Finally he re-
signed himself to the new condition of things, and pro-
ceeded about his original business, cursing volubly, and
dragging his loaded limb clumsily along. Scarcely had
he managed to recollect the errand on which he was bent
when to his horror he found his remaining legs furiously
seized. The struggle then assumed magnificent propor-
tions, and beads of perspiration stood on the beef-ant’s head
as he finally found all his legs thrown out of action, and
swarms of fresh assailants crawling up them. A presenti-
ment of the end seemed to come over him. But no
Roman gladiator could have died more gamely. To the
small ants, death was not even a subject for considera-
tion. As fast as the leading assailants were reduced to a
state of glue, fresh ones took their places. Finally the
executioners were called up, and, amidst wild cheers,
climbed on the heroic old beef ant’s back, and adminis-
tered the coup de grdee. Every such contest finished
alike, th - beef-ants being always conquered by the weight
of numbers and their inability to free themselves from
the sticky remains of their deceased enemies. What the
quarrel was about I did not learn. There were no par-
ticulars published in the Daily Ant-Heap.

“Pick”: Be. places with a plurality of
names (B. 13/6/’O7). Between Cooma and Bega,
on the Brown Mountain, is a village which, in
the Lands and Postal Departments, is called
Bemboka. The stage-coach men call it Colombo,
and the oldest inhabitant refers to it as Lyttelton.
Travellers name it Windy Hollow; and when
the bush fire of January, 1904, went through
there, residents called it Hell.

“ Janor ” : Re strange deaths of animals.
In the deep, straight-walled ravines that abound
on the slopes of Mount Kosciusko it is not unusual
to see the complete skeleton of a bullock suspended
in the branches of a snow-gum. The beast had
been grazing along the edge of a gully, and, stray-
ing on to the deep, soft snow overlying the chasm,
it sank until caught in the branches of a sub-
merged tree. Subsequent thaw and sunshine did
the rest. It’s an uncanny appearance the whitened
bones present gleaming high up amid the green
foliage.

“Hector Wiregrass ” : Why should “G.
Morton ” pour ink into the well of Truth with his
yarns about the industrious Maori? Let him
look at the streets of Gisborne any day of any
week, and out of every three loafers and leaners
two are Maoris, and their loafing is
wonderful to witness. Yes, the Maori
works hard—three times a day.
The industry of his jaw is mar-
vellous. And “G. Mor-
ton ” says the Maoris
have cleared
50,000 acres
of land in 15
years. Well,
thatis rather
over 3000
acres a year,
and if you
get enough
Maoris on
the job, and
there isn’t
much to
clear, they
may do even
that without
tiring them-
selves. I once
saw a Maori
clear an acre of
land in a week.
There was one dead tree
on the acre, and, after thinking the matter over
during Sunday and Monday, Jack Maori put a
lighted match under that tree on Tuesday. It
was pretty well combusted by Saturday, but the
Maori was nearly worn out with sitting down
watching it burn.

“ Town Gurr ” : In my opinion, one of the
most useful pets to have—if you are not a chicken-
raiser—is a tame sparrow-hawk. The one that I
once possessed, was annexed from his parents in
the downy stage, and reared on raw meat. As
soon as he began to find his “ sea legs ” and sa.il
about, the sparrows, who were busily engaged in
building operations up the household spout, and
under the verandah, began to leave without draw-
ing their wages. Very soon a sparrow about the
premises was a rarity, and I was exceedingly
sorry when the pet took on the responsibility of
supporting a Mrs. Hawk, and left my employ.
I offered him a rise of salary and quarters for self
and family if he would stay.
“ Col. Squirt ” writes from Fiji, and
makes certain statements on his own responsi-
bility :
I noticed in a recent Bulletin some reasons given by
an Indian officer for the preponderance of Indian increase
over Fijian in Fiji.
He is wrong. It is not the luxuries of the “ papalangi ”
that are responsible.
In the old days, when a man and woman married (more
or less) and a child was born, they separated for three
years. The man went to another village in the same
Buliship, and again entered into the “ holy bond ” with
like results, eventually returning to the original lady.
She had in the meantime remained quietly with father,
who supported the grand-kid. The latter, having had
full maternal nourishment, became a fine, vigorous young-
ster. But the missionary said such things were an
abom. so He dwells at home with Her, and when an It
has arrived, she has recourse to a friendly root, which
produces a particularly violent form of abortion, after
which Rebecca was an emblem of maternity, by way of
comparison with her. So one of the physically finest
colored races in the world dies out.
* * * *
In the Fitzroy Gardens, right in the heart
of Melbourne, there are many ’possums, both the
ring-tails and the brush-tails, and it is surprising
how very tame they are. Familiarity with the
proximity of the two-legged animals, who haunt
the darkest spots at night-time, has made these
little furry Australians bold and adventuresome.
They play hopscotch and honeypots in the glare
of the gas-lamps, and chuck acorns at one another
with the freedom of beings who live in a world
of peace and harmony.
* * * *
“A. ” : The other day, in a South Coast
(NIS.W.) town, two sewer rats wandered into the
lobby at the public school and ate a hole in a
youngster’s lunch bag, leaving much hair around
the aperture. The news spread far and wide. An
alderman took the matter up, and wrote to the
chairman of the School Board, asking what he
was going to do about the rats, and what steps he
proposed taking to keep the vermin in their
proper places. The chairman is a busy man, and
notified the alderman that he hadn’t time to go
chasing rats. Next the teacher received a com-
munication from the Municipal Council asking if
it were a fact, that about eighty-five rats invaded
his school, ate up all the children’s dinners, and
seriously interfered with his discipline, and
upended his New Syllabus for the day. Also,
he was asked to say if the rats were much
swollen and diseased looking, and if they had left a
lot of whisker behind in the raided bags. The
teacher replied that two rats —not much swollen or
diseased-looking—from the Council’s filthy sewer,
opposite and adjacent to the school, had visited
the place and had partaken of light refreshments
in the lobby, but had taken their departure with-
out causing any disturbance, and without damag-
ing either the discipline or the New Syllabus to
any noticeable extent. Further, the ped. in-
formed the civic fathers that no rats had been
seen on the premises before or since ; and that, in
his opinion, the only rats he knew of, worth men-
tioning, existed in the minds of the said aider-
men. The teacher has been informed by the
Council that it is quite satisfied with his ex-
planation.
“ON THIS OCCASION.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
With some regret I beg to state
The she-oaks do not sigh,
The Southern Gross (en passant)
Is absent from the sky
(On this occasion).
The river doesn’t seem inclined
To murmur on its way ;
To be precise, that noble stre am
Is mostly yellow clay
(On this occasion).
The sun had not sunk redly,
As it always does in verse ;
For wear and tear, I think that i
Looks very much the worse
(On this occasion).
The willows are not weeping ;
But with some relief I see
A look of wild hilarity
Upon this maudlin tree
(On this occasion).
The curlew isn’t mournful,
He’s at present on the jag;
Tiie gloaming isn’t gloaming,
And the hours do not lag
(On this occasion).
n.s.w.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
There is no good ground for confusing
Joan of Arc, the saver of France, with Joe the
Nark, the raver by chance.
Paddy Slavin, ex-Australian pugilist, is
reported to be becoming one of the Fat Men of
Dawson City, Alaska, where he has a flourishing
timber business.
Recently-returned visitors from London
describe B. R. Wise as slowly recovering from a
very serious illness, out of which he was dragged
by the devoted nursing of his wife.
It isn’t often that an alderman does any-
thing remarkable, but Thomas Glissan, who has
been mayor of Ulmarra (N.S.W.) for 16 years,
deserves a notice for long continuance. He
dropped his weary, protracted job last week.
Ulmarra has 1700 people and two pubs.
LITTLE MA THOMPSON,
GRANNY “ HERALD’S ”
MUSICAL BOY.
A correspondent writes at some length to
point out that the full name of Queensland
Senator Chataway is Thomas Drinkwater Chata-
way. What.if it is! It wasn’t he who sat on
the crocodile in the Northern Territory, anyhow.
The deceased Peter Collinson Close, of the
Crick-Willis land cases, Was in worse health than
the public imagined during the proceedings in
court. There is no doubt that those proceedings
hurried the end. Though his grandfather fought
under Wellington in the Peninsula, Peter’s mili-
tant qualities were not the sort to carry him
through such a campaign of litigation as opened
before him.
Bill Keast, of the Dandenong, who, a little
while back, was known as “I and Bent,” threatens
to make a noise when the Victorian Assembly
meets. He complains that there has been no
reform to speak of, and that.the Ministry has only
acquired a reputation for economy in order to
cloak its extravagance. Keast recently boasted
that he often rides in his own motor car with his
own “shofuer” past the place where, a few years
ago, he worked with a pick and shovel.
REQUIRES NO EXPLANATION .
Colonel George W. Bell, ex-United States
Consul, died, on Sunday, at his residence,
Edward-street, North Sydney, aged 69 years.
He was something of an orator—the silver-tongued
variety, with a tendency to spread-eagleism—and
he was lecturing almost to the last.
A significant trio at an Edinburgh Uni-
versity meeting the other day were Prime Minis-
ter Deakin, Premier Jameson (“ Dr. Jim ” of the
Raid), and Premier Botha. “ After me will come
Dr. Jameson,” said Deakin in his speech, “and
after him will come General Botha—not for the
first time.” And the students roared.
One of the quieter humors of the recent
Australian invasion of England was Tom Bent’s
speech to the Oxford University undergrads. In
one of the old grey halls of that lichen covered
institution he told them that what Oxford wanted
was chairs of mining and agriculture. The
students roared at the “joke.” But perhaps the
laugh was against them.
One of the blithest members of the S.A.
Assembly, Country-party leader Burgoyne,
passed his 80th year a few days ago. His col-
leagues gave him a case of pipes in honor of the
occasion. The veteran has been a good pro-
gressive Liberal for 23 years of political life, and
he was a member of the Cockburn Government,
which introduced the first really Radical policy to
the S.A. electors in 1889.
Although heaving seas, “cockeye bobs,”
and bad whisky had no terrors for Corporal (or is
it Field Marshal or Honorary Sentry-Box ?) Neild
during his recent visit to Westralia, the nerve-
racking, rough-riding Kalgoorlie express proved
too much for him. He got out at Clndlow’s Well,
and journeyed back to Perth—4o miles distant —
by the next train. After all, there are more tests
of courage than the battlefield, and a warrior has
to balance at times on a wilder animal than the
horse.
Louis Brennan, whose mono-railway is now
being talked about, was the son of a photographer
in Brunswick-street, Fitzroy (Melb.). He served
his apprenticeship as an engineer with S. K.
Smith, who, 30 years ago, was a well-known
identity of Melbourne, and was mayor of the city
one year (three different Smiths were mayors of
Melbourne). Brennan’s torpedo lay for a long
time in the yard of Melbourne Parliament
House, when Brennan was buttonholing members
and supplicating the Assembly to help him with
his invention. Parliament, after long supplica-
tion and much talk, voted him some money, and
then Brennan disappeared to England, where he
got six figures for his implement.
The fact that the late Dr. Adam Jameson,
Transvaal Minister for Lands, was formerly
Westralia’s Minister for Lands, caused a Sydney
daily to institute a comparison with Robert Lowe,
on the assumption that Lowe held Ministerial
office both in Australia and England. But Lowe
was gone from Australia before N.S.W. pos-
sessed Responsible Government. No N.S.W.
ex-Minister attained Ministerial office in England.
Childers, who was a member of the Haines
Government in Victoria, did so, becoming, like
Lowe, Chancellor of the British Exchequer.
Childers was English, and his little spell in Aus-
tralia was only an interlude in his life.
“Diamond Jack” Woods, of Melbourne,
who displayed his blatant jewellery at the race-
courses and other haunts of the Dangerous until
three impoverished punters garrotted him in his
own garden, is just another ludicrous result of the
“mislaying” of old Jimmy Tyson’s will. Mr. John
Woods,otherwiseDiamond Jack, isaTyson legatee,
or the husband of one. Anyway, it was the famous
estate which accounted for the 200-guinea rings on
Mr. John’s fingers, and the appalling breast-pins
and watch-chains with which he invited trouble.
One would like to meet the spook of deceased
Jimmy at a seance, and ask him what he thinks of;
the results of his long years of money-grubbing.
James Mills, of the Union Steamship Com-
pany, recently knighted, started life in the offices
of John Jones and Co., Dunedin. A romantic
firm was that of Jones and Co. John Jones was
the third son of Thomas Jones, one of the early
settlers of N.S. Wales-, and was born in Sydney in
1809. Early in life he entered into commercial
speculation, and was part-owner of several whalers.
In 1839, he bought an extensive property from the
Maoris at Waikouaiti, and in the following yfear
induced thirteen families to leave N.S. Wales and
settle in Maoriland. He was thus the original
first settler of Otago. He continued to live in
Dunedin until his death in 1869. In 1830, he
was married in Sydney to Miss Sarah Sizemore.
Before the settlement at Otago by the Scotch
immigrants, John Jones was known as “King”
Jones ; he owned almost everything in what was
afterwards Dunedin, and his word was Jaw. He
founded the Harbor Steam Navigation Company-
which was the precursor of the Union Steamship
Co.
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy not only
stops a cough, but removes the irritation which
causes it. For sale everywhere, Is. 6d.*
The Mayor of Yass (N.S.W.) fulfils the
traditions of the aldermanic order. Lately he
attended the local public school and presented
the pupils with an enlarged photograph of himself
and other aldermen, to be hung on the pre-
mises as a bright example and a spur to ambition.
The mayor’s name is Grogan. Which recalls that
Yass is the place that advertised the other day for
a man who would be town clerk, road engineer,
and superintendent of the gasworks combined for
£l2O a year. And Grogan was the name at the
foot of the advt. To be able to offer such a job at
such a screw is something for the local youth to
aspire to.
Medwood, the youth who carried off the
amateur middleweight championship of New
South Wales at the recent boxing contests, is still
a sctioolboy in his native Hobart, and not yet 18.
His genius in the art of stoush was discovered by
Tasmania’s Chief Health Officer, Dr. Elkington,
himself a keen judge of expert pugilism ; and he
persuaded Medwood to test his ability with the
cracks of the mainland. The Tasmanian proved
himself a game, tricky, and hard-hitting practi-
tioner. It is to be hoped that he will look along
again for the Australasian championships, in
which the middle-weight division will probably
contain the Victorian top-notcher, Leith, a nug- 4
gety fighter of the Squires type.
The death of John Acraman, who in 1854
started football in Adelaide with a ball imported
from England, has brought out reminiscences of
those beginnings. A veteran writing to the Regis-
ter recalls that “Harrow rules” were in force,
with a round ball, and goal posts 9ft. high, the
ball having to pass under the crossbar (as in
soccer). A catch and drop-kick were allowed,
but holding the man was not. The most interest-
ing point put forward is one of etymology, the
suggestion being made that, in the frequent
matches, Garrison v. Civilians, the enthusiasm of
the soldiers’ comrades round the ground gave to
the word “ barrackers ” its present unfavorable
meaning. Anyhow, John Acraman is dead.
Some old Victorian identities went over
last week. One was John Hewitt, who had
resided at Ararat for 50 years. He was 25 years
a member of the council, and was Mayor when
“ Eddie and George” visited Ararat, yet he was
not knighted. He passed out at 84. Parson
George Horrocks, nephew of the calico man, died
at Beechworth, aged 77. Mrs. Mary Harvey
Coade, who had lived for 50 years at Beechworth,
flickered out, aged 89. At Chiltern, the soul
of James Skerry wandered off the earth, the
body being aged 81. He reached Chiltern in the
’fifties, and was shift captain of the old Sons of
Freedom mine. Also Mrs. John Senior, who for
40 years was matron of Woods’ Point Hospital,
passed in her checks, age not given. Woods’
Point had just begun to exist when the lady went
there, and she saw its rise, its greatness, and its
fall.
N.S.W. Premier Carruthers gives £5OO of
public money to assist in laying oiit Belmore
Park, Sydney, conditionally on a Melbourne
man being brought to do the work. The
Melbourne man is possibly a one-time
Sydney man named Guilfoyle, who was born
at Chelsea, London, in 1843, and came to
N.S. Wales with his parents in 1853. His father,
Michael Guilfoyle, established an “exotic nur-
sery,” at Double Bay, in 1868. Young Guilfoyle
accompanied the Challenger expedition to the
South Seas, and subsequently cultivated sugar
and tobacco in Queensland. In 1873 he became
director of the Melbourne Botanic Gardens in
succession to Dr. Von Mueller. He has written
several works on botanical subjects,'and is reputed
to have onCe hammered a Victorian Governor
under circumstances which would have justified
him in treading on his Excellency when he was
down. In the ’7o’s the nursery at Double Bay
was carved into building allotments.
Mr. Thomas Allwrighfc Dibbs, general
manager of the Commercial Banking Company of
Sydney, completed his 60th year of service with
the bank on Saturday. “ Tom Dibbs ” entered
the bank when 14 years of age. On his golden
jubilee he told a congregation of his colleagues,
whom he was feasting at hisNorthShorehome, that
'when he joined he was “exchange boy,” and that
he and a porter carried as much as 8000 sovereigns
to the other banks to exchange for notes. The
risk wasn’t what it might appear, for in those
days every “crook” was known—the population
was limited, and the chance of escape about nil.
Mr. T. A. Dibbs was born in George-street,
almost opposite the present bank building. When
he joined the institute the manager was Leslie
Duguid. The bank was then within two
doors of King-street, in a structure erected in 1835
by Mr. R. Sullivan, draper, and for which a rent
of £285 a year was paid, with the option of
purchase at £1950.
YV’oods’ Great Peppeniiint Cure won’t cure
Your broken leg or hearts,
Nor will it act, as a matter of fact,
As a recipe for tarts ;
It won’t cure neuralgia, temper, squints,
For workingmen or toffs ;
But Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure will cure
Your colds and cure your coughs.*
Max Hirsch, the quaint believer in an
exploded fetish, has at last worked the oracle to
some profit—£loo a year profit, to wit—and
becomes Victorian correspondent of the British
Board of Trade. Hirsch, German by birth, Jew
by persuasion, and Australian only because he
has naturalisation papers, should make a desirable
correspondent for Britain’s foreign-trade Govern-
ment.
Solicitor William Hilson Pigott, who
quits the N.S.W. Upper House in his old age,
was born in Edinburgh, but has passed between
60 and 70 years in N.S.W. He was brought up
in the region of Miller’s Point, Sydney. Mr.
Pigott claims a lineage, and possesses a coat
of arms, on which figure some things that look
like three pickaxes and a greyhound. As Parkes’s
nominee, he went to the Council in the same
batch as C. J. Roberts, Ebby Vickery, and other
shining lights.
“4.25 ” p.m. writes :
N.S.W. public servants woke up with a start one day
last week, when Premier Joe announced that he hail
appointed Stipendiary Magistrate Wilshire to the vacancy
on the Public Service Board. Several reasons are put
forward for the selection. The most probable is that
Wilshire’s name is so much like that of Wilson, whose
place he takes, that Joe naturally thought of him
first. Another isn’t that the one Stipendiary on the
Board is a success. The training on drunks and dis-
orderlies absorbed by Pa Delohery may have been a
good training, but Pa doesn’t show it. Also, we
thought a man outside us would get the job.
Personally, I lost the price of a counter lunch
on George Pile. What with two “beaks” on the
Board, and a Controller of Prisons as a temporary help, a
chap to-day has to have a pretty clean sheet to stand a
chance of getting on in the service. They tell me that
Neitenstein bolted back to his gaol because his
locum tenens on the job was breaking up. More likely
it was that the funny stories that Saunders used to teil
him while the Board was hearing our appeals were too
much for him. Anyway, it’s after four, and I must stop
work for the day.
“Billy T.” : A recent waterspout of
adulation gushes in each Melbourne paper over
ex-P.M. Panton, who has just been retired, after
30 years’ service as a magistrate, and 50 odd as a
well-paid civil servant of one kind or another.
But the ordinary citizen remembers Panton as a
tall, bony, bustling person, making a big aristo-
cratic pretension, and upholding it by a display
of ferocity towards the “ lower orders,” and a fine
show of fellow-feeling towards those as socially
exalted as he wished to appear to be. The dailies
even enthuse over Panton as an artist, but as a
painter and draughtsman the P.M. was always
an awkward and angular amateur without a gleam
of real ability. In the time gone the Age, that now
finds him angelic, derided his art, and severely
criticised his errors of judgment, tact, and taste
as a magistrate, but evidently it is the order to
speak no ill of the superannuated. Australia will
never, while Panton lives, quite forget the Sievier
incident. It revealed the magistrate as a man whose
reverence for a very small and dilapidated scion of
the British aristocracy—a scion who didn’t even
pay his gambling debts promptly—surpassed that
of many beadles.
NOT KNOWN.
Mr. Reid has issued circulars calling a meeting of the
Opposition for to-morrow. Owing to his case in the
Arbitration Court, in Sydney, he will be unable to be
present. The conviction is being forced on to the Oppo-
sition members in both Houses that they have been
placed at a serious disadvantage through the continued
absence of their accredited leader. He is half the session
a stranger, and the other half a leader.—D.T.
Some day a stranger will be ejected for trespassing
on the floor of the House.
Captain John Thomas, a very ancient
citizen of 92, died lately at Fremantle (W.A.).
He landed at the mere tent and packing case,
which were then Fremantle, in November, 1829
the year in which the colony was proclaimed, and
very soon after the first settlement was made at
Albany. He was 14 then, and he promptly
started to lead the strenuous life. At the age of
17 he went on the land in the Kelmscott district,
with one Gaze as partner. The natives promptly
killed Gaze, but Thomas formed himself into a
hollow square, and fought a long rear-guard action
as he retreated towards civilisation. Besides the
clothes he had on, he brought away the wreck of
his fortunes, including a pocket handkerchief and
some small change. Everything else was de-
stroyed. He took to fishing after that, and with
the fish money he built a 20-ton cutter, which
traded between Fremantle, Bunbury and the
Yasse. In 1845 heopenedatrade with Singapore,
and lost his speck of a cutter on the Ahrolhos.
Then he built a vessel of 125 tons, which baby
craft became known on the coasts of Africa, India,
Eastern Asia, and round a good part of Australia.
Its captain and owner was a trader in everything
that turned up, and made money at it. In his
declining years he left the sea and again went on
the land. Finally, being fairly affluent and 61, he
settled down at Fremantle to wait till Providence
should call him, which it did after a decent
interval of 31 years.
THE BEKNIGHTED JESTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Among the “ Birthday Honors,” lately gazetted in
London, appears the following:—“Mr. W. S. Gilbert,
dramatist and author, to be a Knight Bachelor.” —Vide
cables.
Never mind the why and wherefore,
He’s a humorist, and therefore
'Tain’t surprising he should care for
What you’d think he would despise 1
’Tis beyond the Legislature
To increase our mental stature,
Or to alter Human Nature
In its inconsistencies !
Creatures human,
Man or woman,
Folk of high or low degree,
Sick or healthy,
Poor or wealthy,
Reek of inconsistencee 1
Here is evidence, my brothers,
That which I condemn in others
Will be found, though conscience smothers,
Just what others see in me !
He who scoffed and sneered and slighted
To an audience delighted
Has himself become beknighted 1
O, for inconsistencee !
After writing
Ballads blighting,
Operatic ribaldree,
Fancy pictures,
Striking strictures,
He, himself, becomes K.B. I
x.s.w.
W. T. GOODGE.

THIS represents the Myers’ Bulldozer Power Pump as made in its largest size. This pump is built with a 0-inch
cylinder, and 12, 16 and 20 inch adjustable stroke. The main body of the pump forms the cylinder, which is
first accurately bored and then brass lined with a heavy, seamless drawn brass tube, which is forced into
place by screw power, working' under many tons pressure, after which the ends are swaged out, which ensures
it being permanently located
The pumj> is fitted with 12 valves formed by using a brass valve seat screwed into the pump proper, and high
duty quick closing rubber valves, 3| inches in diameter. The valves are fitted with a spring which assists in closing
so as to reduce the slippage of water to the minimum when working under high pressure. These valves are all
located on top of the pump, immediately under the air chamber cap, so they can be readily gotten at for repairing
when required.
The suction and discharge are fitted with a flange union securely bolted to the pump. They are each 4 inches
in diameter. The suction is immediately under the centre of the pump, thus doing away with elbows, etc., as the
pump can be located directly over the well.
The gears are heavy and substantially made. All boxes are split and babbitted, and so arranged that the
wear can be easily taken up.
The piston rod guide is attached direct to the head of the pump, thus always insuring a correct alignment.
The shafting and wrist pins are steel.
The entire pump is mounted on one base, thus making it easy to set. The outer end of the pulley shaft is
supported by a bearing with adjustable box ; has brass-covered piston rod. The pump is equipped with tight and
loose pulleys 30 inches diameter with 6-inch face.
Its special construction, great power and wide range of WQrk recommend it for general and export trade, for
the reason that it can be packed in a smaller' space than any other pump of the same capacity known to the trade.

THIS represents the Perfect Barrel Spray Pump, which is considered the best barrel
spray pump ever offered, it being single acting in the suction and double acting

It has an exceedingly large air chamber, which is very necessary (especially when
two leads of hose are used), in connection with the mechanical construction above
described, which enables the operator to work this pump under pressure with much
less exertion than is required with other large spray pumps.
The main body of the pump is all air chamber , excepting what is taken up by the

base is separate and can be removed. All parts can be removed by disconnecting the

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
.Sydney, July 9, 1907.
My Dear Arini,—
The old guard lined up before their Northcote
Excellencies and saluted. The smallest guards-
man was about 18 inches high, and the tallest
reached her Ex.’s shoulder. She picked the biggest
and waltzed with him ; then she selected a brown
boy-butterfly, about the size of a loaf of brdad.
The gleaming aides and secs, took partners
reaching to about their uniformed knees—
tiny grandmothers, lovely fisher girls, and
all manner of small beauties. The little
Belisario and Rodd boys were “The Princes
in the Tower,” after Millais’ picture, in
black velvet doublets, silk hose, and jewelled
orders. Foregoing, it may be explained, refers to a
children’s fancy-dress party given by Lady North-
cote this afternoon. It was a dream of fair
children.
Nary a woman in the Royal on Saturday night
but would have given in sympathy half her box
of chocolates to Mr, George Musgrove when he
paced to the front of the stage to an-
nounce another disappointment. “ The Valky-
rie ” was programmed, and the first act
had been sung, with Studemund, Greder,
and Sara Anderson as Siegmund, Hun-
ding, and Sieglipde respectively. The inky-
bearded and black-browed, hairy Hunding
vyas sleeping his drugged sleep, and the Thor-
like, fair-haired Siegmund, the heart under his
lion-hide garment beating wildly for love of the
golden-haired, blue-eyed giantess, Mrs. Hunding,
crushed her against his wild-beast robe, and
together singing, they did a rush for the
open door and the blue, calm night. The
house was crowded' from gallery to stalls, and a
complaisant writer would describe the audience as
brilliant. Down fell the curtain on the huge
oak and the guilty lovers pausing in the back-
ground. When it went up in response to a tumult
of applause—Mr. G. H. Reid, in the manager’s
box, smote his “Yes” and his “No” to-
gether manfully to help the appreciation along—
poor Musgrove was there with a sheet of letter
paper in his hand. “Don’t congratulate me,
ladies and gentlemen," he said. And the audi-
ence guessed what was to follow.
He read a doctor’s certificate stating that
Miss Heinze was unfit to sing. Miss Heinze
is almost the whole of the second act
of “The Valkyrie.” It was she who had caused
an audience to be sent empty away after it
had taken its seat for Friday’s matinee.
This certificate had only reached the manage-
ment at 10 past six—4o minutes before the
updrawing of the curtain ! The audience,
while sorry for itself and Musgrove, and
Miss Heinze, was thinking of its scamped
dinner. “The question is, what is best to be
done?” continued the manager. “This opera
cannot proceed” From the gallery came a
loud, clear voice, “Let the opera proceed, and the,
orchestra play Brunnhilde’s part.” A burst of
applause showed the suggestion to be not un-
welcome, but another voice burst out “Get George
Reid for the part ! ”
The evening was started afresh—so to speak—
with “The Flying Dutchman.” Some of the
audience wasn’t taking any more Dutchman, so it
got its “Valkyrie” act, and its money. But the
substitute ran no more smoothly than did the
unhappy Dutchman’s love-making. Miss Ormond,
a chorus girl, came on to sing the principal soprano
r6le, reading the music from the score. Miss
Anderson, the Senta, had struck at the last
moment over a question of salary. But the audi-
ence had no more gasps left. It applauded the
scratch Senta.
The family-circle folk at the last “DieWal-
kure ” performance didn’t yawn during one, at
least, of the dreaded 25 minutes’ intervals. They
were too anxiously wondering whether the young
man, who was returning with the coffee-pot full
of boiling liquid, was about to spill it down their
backs or not. He didn’t. He arrived bravely
and safely at his destination amid guffaws of
laughter. There was something of the magician
or ex-waiter in that pale young man, for, after
landing the hot coffee pot, he dived into space and
hauled up a bag, out of which he produced
victuals, cups, saucers, spoons, serviettes, and a
tiny cardboard box full of sugar. Then he dis-
pensed hot coffee and sandwiches to his three
lady friends amid applause. “Is it ’ot enuf,
Miss?” said a voice. “Parse around the sand-
widges,” said another. But the party ate and
drank swiftly and copiously, and the deft young
man had the dishes put away just as the curtain
rose on Act 111.
Mr. Regnes (husband of Mrs. Sara Anderson,
prima donna of the Royal Germans) has come
somewhat into prominence through the fierce
light that beats upon rows between managers and
stars. He is young, and dark as a Spaniard, but,
like his wife, an American. Also he reminds
somewhat of Julius Knight as we first knew
that artist, and has the same fine, resonant
speaking voice. He is a basso, and looks
as if he’d make a great Mephisto. Regnes
is not singing with the co. He just came
out with his Vikingess. She is the fairest and
bluest-eyed of warblers, with infantile flaxen hair.
Looks a regular daughter of a sea king. The
wedded basso and soprano go out only for exer-
cise. She conserves her voice. A Fourth of July
dinner with friends has been her only dissipa-
tion. Regnes is smitten with admiration of the
Australian voices. He calls them, amongst other
things, “good-sized, soulful, sympathetic, and
well-placed.” He heard the opera chorus in Mel-
bourne thirteen weeks ago. when it was untried,
and he opinionated, “Nowhere else in the world
would one hear such voices.”
Mrs. Willie Mitchell Goodwin met her Hart,
it appears, at sea on a liner. Presently I’ll write
something about “The Husband as a Marine
Product; Should He be Canned ? ”
Good-bye violets and daffodils nearly smothered
the Waldrons (he is the actor, who takes an Aus-
tralian bride with him) and the Harold Waring
honeymooners who left by the Manuka, for
America, yesterday. This bride is in mourning,
and the bridegroom had the funereal symbol on
his light overcoat. There was a smart damsel in
a grey-green tailor-made and a red hat amongst
the crowd on deck. But Mrs. Walter Marks,
brown-haired, brown-furred, and with cherries in
her hat, took attention from brides or anyone else.
Count Morner, the moderately-new Swedish
Consul-General in Australia, drops a large lump
of sugar into this country’s cup ;
The Australians are a remarkably well-educated, well-
informed, and much-travelled people. One can discuss
with them all sorts of topics, and be sure of an intelligent
attention to the subject. In two months spent here I
have learned to know more people who. have visited
Sweden than I did during two years’ stay in England, and
It is astonishing what a wealth of information these
travelled Australians bring back with them, and what
they have time to see and learn during their travels.
The people as a whole are, in my opinion, more kindly,
more graceful, freer, and more polite and obliging than
in the mother country, England. The new type of the
race is, of course, as yet in the making, but it ought to
become one of the soundest and most harmoniously-
fashioned recorded in the nation’s history.
The translator isn’t too sure of the last remark j
he says it’s “ sundaste och mest harmoniske ” in
the original. But whatever it means, I’m sure it
must be something with syrup in it.
The English notability, who is said to be en-
gaged to an Australian pen woman, is not “ Lord
Donahue ” as stated the other day, but Sir George
Doughty, M.P., a man of some years and much
wealth, who has raised himself from the humblest
ranks. He is rich enough to keep a dozen motor
cars, and to supply his wife with a diamond
tiara for every day of the week, if he Wants to.
Miss Amy Sherwin has got almost as, many
pupils as she can take at 10 guineas a quarter.
She sings at the reception to the Maoriland foot-
ballers, next Thursday, at Sydney Town Hall.
Mrs. Ernest Watt, an absentee Sydney beauty,
sister to Mrs. Neville Dangar, cables her family
of the advent of an infant daughter. The stork
left the package last week. Some time ago,
baby’s pa “ broke out littery,” and did it well,
but since then he seems to have lapsed into the
sportsman.
The Mahaffy, who is Something High, or some-
thing Specially British in Fiji (I think they call
him Colonial Secretary, and the initials that run
behind his chariot are 1.5.0.), and who married
a Melb. girl, is in Sydney for a few days. On
Sunday he ate his bit of luncheon cereal with the
Northcotes.
The Beautiful Fiend took a pair of long-fingered,
finely-nailed, nervous hands out of her Russian
sable muff, sank them in the fur, and, turning up
a pair of large dark eyes to the lighted ceiling,
began to rave about Sydney. “ I went out in a
motor car yesterday, and every time I caught
sight of a lovely view I screamed with joy. At
last the lovely views became so numerous that my
admiration resolved itself into one long scream.
And what weather —this honey-colored, dry,
cold atmosphere! I’m so glad it will be
cold while I’m playing the Beautiful Fiend.
I don’t know how I could suffer the heavy
velvet gowns and furs I wear in that awful
piece if the temperature were up to 70.
Awful drama ? Yes, it is to me, because
I’ve played it for two years running. Imagine
having to be fiendish and work woe to all the good
characters six nights a week for two years!
Scientists say that the nature of one’s work is
written on one’s face. If I look like the charac-
ters I do best I ought to wear an iron mask
when I’m let loose amongst my fellow creatures.
‘The Great Awakening’ is as big an audience-
drawer as the Fiend. Yes, I collaborated in the
writing of it, and with both dramas I’ve made a
lot of money. After this tour—it is to be a year
long—l’m going to retire, and grow orchids, and
raise canaries, and catch eggs—anything, in fact,
that means being out of doors and in the country.
No, I didn't know any Australians till I came on
the Macedonia. Of the artistes who have played
here I saw Maud Jeffries. I shall not soon forget
her as Mariainne in Stephen Phillips’ ‘ Herod.’”
Miss Hildyard is tall, very slight, and awfully
emotional, with a long-oval, clear pale face, and
a lot of soft brown hair, padless, curl-less,
untinkered at, and coiled low on her neck. She
reminds of Mrs. Pat Campbell, and doesn’t look
as if she were out of the twenties.
Mrs. Blanche Arral is back in Sydney, and will
most likely add to her artistic triumphs of some
months ago. In Maoriland the French warbler
did splendid business, taking over £3OOO, which is
some set-off to being charged 9s. 6d. for two days’
meals for the Arral cat and dog. That befel in
one hostelry in a certain town.
Mrs. James Mills, now a Dame (commonly
called a Lady) by act of the King (some persons
think the acquisition of a knighthood equal to a
visitation of God), is still in London. The
casualty of the title which has just befallen her
husband, James Mills, managing director of the
Union S.S. Co., will certainly be resented by her
clan. She comes of an untitled Irish totem of
incredible age and haughtiness. A correspondent
tells me from London that the visiting yellow-
haired Maorilander (by adoption) is faithful to
emerald-green head-wear. The Mills are return-
ing to Maoriland shortly.
Writes a Voice, crying on paper in the Fog;
“ One doesn’t realise how enchanting distance (in
your case 14,000 miles) may be until one sees at
close range a famous beauty—some beauty who
has been famous ever since we can remember, and
over whose loveliness ‘ M. A. P. ’ still gets periodic
attacks of the ‘ O mys! ’ While the wheel of
entertainment to the Premiers was still going a
hundred revolutions to the second, I had the
privilege of gazing, at close range and at leisure,
upon the Duchess of Devonshire. When I used
to read, in my far-back station home, of
tbis wondrous survival, and gaze upon the
calm beauty and intellect in her photographed
face—it never looked more than a 28-year-
old face—l used then to glance at granny (Pa’s
mother) and wonder whether, if she had been
born an Englishwoman and destined to be the
wearer of a ducal coronet, she would have kept
the youth and beauty which, report says, gave
the young squatters heartaches. Well, now I’ve
seen her Grace of Devonshire. She can’t compare
with granny. Granny is 80, but looks 70, and is
as gay as a magpie, and dresses decently. An
old identity told me that 40 years ago the
Duchess (she is twice duchessed, first time of
Manchester) was a most beautiful creature.
Now she’s a little, round-backed, shrivelled
yellow party who looks all the burdqu of her sack
of years—Bo of them or so. If she dressed
appropriately, none but a Hoggenheimeress would
comment on her appearance. But her gowns are
cut down at the back to near her waist line, and are
appallingly meagre in front. They display folds
of parchment skin. , , Her wig isn’t grey, and
she blazes with jewels. Wasn’t it someone in
your paper that said, ‘Jewels on an old woman
suggest a lantern on a wreck ’ ? . . She put up
her long-stemmed monocle and took a survey of
Miss Botha and Miss Ward, then looked away
suddenly. Seemed to me, with a ‘O-God-put-
back - Thy - Uni verse - and - give - me-yesterday ’ ex-
pression. What anguish old age must be to the
woman who has been beautiful, and is still rich
and a duchess, and hanging on to the withered
tail of her vanished youth ! ”
The name of a man we know —know personally —
in a current novel always strikes sparks of in-
terest out of us. In that remarkable book,
“When It Was Dark,” the writer gives a
vignette of a millionaire scholar’s library. “A
few pictures here and there in spaces between the
shelves —the hectic flummery of a Whistler noc-
turne, a woman with cherries by Manet, a green
silk fan painted with f&tes galantes by Conder—
alone broke the many-colored monotony of the
books.” Helps one to realise that the one-time
Australian Conder has arrived. He might have
been a very temporary Australian, but we are in-
terested all the same.
Several references to food seem to make inti-
mate some of the characters. Another scholar
and critic —one of the greatest of the age—
addresses himself with reverence to a lady:
“You must be most careful in the preparation of
really good crayfish soup. The great secret is to
flavor with a little lobster spawn, and to mix the
crumb of a French roll with the stock —white
stock, of course—before you add the powdered
shells and anchovies.” This man, whose strong
point is the Higher Criticism, is a favorite at a
rather fluffy club because he has invented two
new omelettes and a white soup. Another
character, Father Ripon, a High Churchman,
after working pretty well all day without food,
asks for “coffee, bread and oranges.”
Talking of feeding, a new and glowingly-
artistic supper-room has been opened at the Aus-
tralia Hotel.
Waitara Foundling Home Ball will occur at
Sydney Town Hall on Wednesday, July 17. A
most worthy charity. Excellency and Miss
Rawson and the Peacock’s Tail of aides and
private secs, will grace the assemblage. So will
the Darleys, the Thomas Hugheses and the Warrior
Gordons.
Astonishing what long memories for the affairs
of their neighbors most persons have. At the
reception given at Baumann’s on Saturday by
French Consul-General Pinard to the commandant
and officers of the cruiser Catinat, Mrs. Wilson,
widow of a once well-known Victorian squatter,
appeared in subdued splendor. She attracted
notice chiefly through her daughter, who is
married to the captain of the white, shark-
prowed warship now lying in Farm Cove. But
in twenty minutes half the crowd had been in-
formed that Mrs. Wilson was Eugenie Legrand, a
French actress who starred in Melbourne, and
had there married Kyrle Bellew. Wilson, the
second consort, left his widow and children pretty
well gilded. Captain Buchard is a fair warrior,
verging on red. His wife is like her mother—of
good presence, dark, and with somewhat Asiatic
eyes. (I am aware that this statement is vague.)
It was one of the brightest of things Froggy—this
reception. Venerable Pinard said Je suis enchante
de vous voir as if he really meant it, and his vice-
consul Deschars wore a nice tie, and the smile
diplomatic. The rest of his attire was, of course,
also conventional. All the leaders of the French
colony in Sydney were present, and courteously
tolerant of the French the Australians tried on
them. Trying it on the Frog ! The Durans
(he’s of the French Bank), the Shards, the F.
Learoyds, the Moreaus and Rogets helped to
make the men-o’-war feel that they were not in a
hostile land. Mayor and Mayoress Hughes, of
Sydney, drifted amiably about amongst the
gold lace, swagger and pointed beards, and ate
the entente cordials sandwich.
That sweet Irish balladist, Marie Narelle, who
has had such a bad illness, is recovering in St.
Vincent’s Hospital. But it will be half a year or
so before she is again on the warbly path.
.Jessie Maclachlan, the Scotch balladist, drifted
through Sydney the other day. Her pretty face
and “ advanced” figure, and her wild and haggard-
looking pianist, and the rest of the co., were bound
for country centres. They go to Maoriland this
week.
The W. P. Mannings—He, She, and little
daughter Beatrix—are back after an eight months’
sojourn in foreign lands and on strange waters.
They had some causerie with the Pope, and stayed
awhile with the millionairess widow of Peter
Russell, and with Rev. Francis Galton. The
little girl was to have put up her hair and
hooked a long frock up her back, and (spon-
sored by Lady Crewe) bent her spine before
Majesty, but as Sodden Society and “M.A.P.”
and kindred papers have told us again and yet
again, the Queen had to be out of England with
her second daughter, Princess Victoria. It was
this Princess’s winter for causing “her royal
parents serious anxiety about her health.” The
evergreen Queen’s absence during the visit of the
extraneous Premiers rather saddened their wives.
No understudy, however well she may read the
part, is an adequate substitute for the principal
royal tragedienne.
Miss Marie Lohr — daughter of Miss Kate
Bishop, a one-time beloved actress in these parts,
and Louis Lohr, the popular theatrical manager,
who now runs a hotel somewhere down by Gipps-
land’s stormy lakes—is, according to her portraits,
a pretty youngster. Marie’s father was a fine-
looking man, and her mother was a beautiful
woman before Stoutness, like the villain in the
melodrama, overtook her while still quite young.
And even after Fat overtook her she was dis
tinctly handsome. Marie appears to take after
her parents save in the tragedy of circumference.
She is now at the London Haymarket, and her
gowns worn in the piece, “ My Wife,” are worth
columns, and seem to come up to the standard of
“creations” :
Her tailor-made suit of the first act is composed of
plaided biscuit and blue cloth trimmed with biscuit
soutache upon the skirt that boldly outlines the blue
motifs upon it. A most becoming little loose coat, with
postillion tabs at the back, has a blue vest touched with
soutache, and the costume is completed by means of a
bronze straw hat massed with white wings in front.
A cover-all cloak is worn over the costume when Miss
Lohr makes her entrance, built of earth-of-Egypt cloth (a
new shade of tan) lined with white satin and trimmed
with turquoise blue and a suspicion of gold. t
The second act finds the charmingly pretty Trixie in
one of her trousseau frocks, a little white cloth suit with
a pleated coatee fascinatingly trimmed with ball fringe ;
and the third reveals her in a becoming pearl grey chiffon
dress, with a band of embroidered lisse and filet lace upon
the skirt, and a Japanese bodice with the characteristic
short sleeves of that genre, from which protrude the white
lace and point d’esprit elbow sleeves that appertain to
the chemisette. The bodice is touched with tomato
velvet very sparingly, but most effectively, and the belt
is a curious Eastern-looking of three colors —old-
blue, dull red, and gold.
The phrase “built of earth-of-Egypt cloth” is
good. Simple “tan” faints before it.
From Brisbane :
Glorious sunshine and a wilderness of roses marked the
belated vice-regal garden party on Thursday. Bright
colors broke out on every side, and for once the frocking
suited the weather. It was decidedly a novelty to find
the usually energetic Governoress receiving sitting down,
and being carefully wrapped up in a smart coat of grey
cloth and fur soon after three, while a sunburnt husband,
looking as if the Northern winds had blown a new lease
of life into his shadowy frame, stood up and did the
bustling.
Languid, graceful sister Villiers (who with hubby is
examining Australia from the heights of the official resi-
dence) lounged through the ordeal in a wonderful gar-
ment of shot blue and green chiffon gauze, over pale
green glace, topped by a hat whose mouse-velvet crown
covered several moon-blue plumes. Among the smartest
froekers (excluding a pink brocade lady who looked like a
hilarious lamp shade having a day out) were Mrs. P. A.
Blundell, wearing grey crepe de chine ; Mrs. Cecil Palmer,
whose prune-tinted velvet looked as if the handsome
wearer had been moulded into it, and whose Gains-
borough velvet hat was at quite the right angle; Mrs.
Harry Cohen, in a glove-like tweed ; Mrs. Lily Moore,
wearing white crbpe, hand-painted in heliotrope roses ;
Mrs. William Collins, in gray chiffon and fox-furs; and
pen-driver Una Kidgell, in cream and black, and a pic-
ture hat. The future hopes of the Chelmsfords worked
like Trojans, handing round “real ice cream,” and at
5 p.m. her Ex.’s tired face moved the party off without
the usual broad hint of the Anthem.
On Saturday Lady Chelmsford shed a brief light upon
the Bowen benefit race meeting. The meeting was
without bookmakers, and a big crowd attended, so
that, on the tote takings, the hospital should do well. A
lively west wind made the frocking heavy and sombre,
and hot tea was at a premium.
On Thursday next Government House gives an evening
at home, and on the same afternoon Lady C. presides at
the Lamington Hospital Annual. This effort will be re-
peated pn the following Monday at the Lady Bowen
yearly gathering, and after that it will be time to rest (at
Toowoomba, I bear) until Show time, 13th August.
Mrs. James Cowlishaw departed Bullwards by the
Orontes on Saturday, and the sprightly Davenport widow'
and her rapidly-blossoming chick went by the same boat
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.
Melbourne and other Australian cities
may expect something particularly catchy in
cricket at the end of the year. A Fijian team
(with a royal captain) promises to pit its brown
valor against Australian players, under the pro-
tection (in all probability) of the M.C.C. They’re
great athletes, the Fijians, and the picked team
will probably be made up of goodly-looking
savages of well over six feet in height. Their
captain is a- Personage—a weighty chief —by
name Khan Davu, or Davu Khan (it’s twisty work,
spelling Fijian). He has just cabled excitedly,
and at great length, to a Victorian lieutenant of
Island-visiting tendencies, to announce his arrival
as advance agent. The men will be wildly
delighted at the prospect of the trip. It is the
ambition of every Fijian to go to “Sydney,” as
they call Australia. It is the land of enchant-
ment, of all delight.
Another of Melbourne Harbor Trust’s im-
ported dredges—the Andre Reboucas—“went
mad ” the other day. Its engines refused to go
astern, and drove the tub clean through a Wil-
liamstown wharf. The Trust thereupon held a
secret inquiry, exonerated everybody, and
proffered the quaint explanation that “the en-
gines had refused to go astern because there
wasn’t enough steam in the boilers”—though
there had been quite enough to drive them ahead
through the wharf. The party really responsible
is, of course, the Trust itself, which imported the
foreign rattletrap instead of getting a workable
dredge made here by the competent Australian
engineers who are looking for jobs. After all,
Geelong had some luck. Its two imported
dredges never arrived. The sea swallowed the
crazy things. If it hadn’t done so they might
have climbed into Geelong, and dredged up and
down the streets till they knocked the town to
pieces.
The habit that the sensational paper has
of putting a cross in the picture to show where
the body was found may have been partly re-
sponsible for the second half of this week’s double
tragedy at Katoomba (N.S.W.). On Sunday,
young William Lannen (aged 13) was scrambling
over a mass of ice at the top of the frozen falls
when he slipped and dropped 200 ft. On Monday,
Miss Henrietta M'Aviney, aged 22, wanted to
see the place (the sort of place that is marked
X on the regulation evening-paper picture)
where the tragedy occurred, and slipped on
the same piece of ice, and went down the
same 200 ft. drop. With these two events and
the midnight murder of a solitary old woman who
lived alone in a big house at Enmore, Sydney has
started out in active pursuit of the Melbourne
horror record. But there is a difference; the
Sydney police made a promising arrest and found
some of the stolen property within a few hours of
the Enmore crime. They at least caught some-
body. The Melbourne police never seem to catch
anybody but a homeless old woman these times,
or anything except a cold, acquired while standing
at a corner waiting to hear from somebody con-
nected with Wren. By the way, the Enmore
prisoner is said to have confessed to the murder,
and meanwhile the humorless law has fined him
ss. or the rising of the court for being drunk !
What use has he for the rising of a mere police
court ?
As it was in the beginning. London Art
Journal for November, 1849, was thus driven to
italics by reading the “Rambles in New South
Wales” of Joseph Phipps Townsend :
Mr. Townsend may well call it a “ startling fact,” that
in two years 10,000 persons left the colony ; but he asserts
his belief that many persons would have remained if they
could have obtained land, the last thing we should have
supposed it difficult to procure.
The land-grabber and the land hunger began very
early. Truly has it been said that ‘ ‘ there were
giants in those days.” For instance, one mon-
strous land-famished person of early N.S.W. con-
ceived the idea that all Maoriland was just about
enough for one landlord, and after a hurried
voyage to the isles he came back with a document
which purported to show that he had bought
the lot.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, July 8, 1907.
My Dear Yarrie.—
Flemington was no beauty show on Saturday.
Blue cheeks and pink noses were the ruling com-
bination with those who believe in letting Nature
alone. Those who assist that good lady came off
worse still, for they assumed the purple tint of
the apparently drowned. There’s a fortune
awaiting the somebody who invents a make-up to
defy south winds at Flemington. Even the
plump Madden family looked tucked up and
dejected—always excepting Mrs. Frank. Cham-
pion defier of the element was Mrs. > Ernest
Brookes, wearing a cloth gown that wasn’t straw-
berry, nor Burgundy, nor common tile red, but
a pleasing mixture of the lot. The one great
stroke on her gown was a sublime scarf in
cream lace, draped picture fashion in front, and
descending to the hem of her garments at the
back. Mrs. George Chirnside made a good
struggle to support a well-deserved reputation for
chic. Her mauve cloth gown furnished a worthy
background to fascinating grey furs lined with
ermine. Ermine linings—as opposed to fraudu-
lent imitations—hall-mark their wearer. The
sign of the Golden Fleece is stamped upon her, so
to speak. Women are wearing as many curious
feather fixings in their hats as South Sea Islanders.
Most of them stood on end in Saturday’s breeze.
One society dame suggested the Valkyrie, whilst
the large majority looked like infuriated hens.
Most of the Really Truly affected the uncom-
promising trottoir. Mrs. Edward Miller “cleared
the ground ” in mauve frieze. Mrs. J. L.
Lempriere did as much in velvet. A large
edition of Lady Northcote a good-looking
visitor from Sydney, who, with her husband,
attracted a lot of admiration at the Deakin
welcome last week —was one of the best
tailor-made dames on the terrace. The frail
beauties who courted neuralgia in their elbows,
and trailed taffeta in the tan, didn’t join in the
tea-chat under the vines.
The wives of the Federal Ministers were con-
jointly At Home at Parliament House, on Tues-
day, that they might say a public “ How d’ye do ”
to Mrs. Deakin. Bigjohn’s wife made a pleasant
and successful hostess, as is her wont. Mrs.
Deakin looked radiant, with a bright color in her
cheeks and her eyes glowing with smiles. In the
smartest of black gowns, with touches of
black and white about the “kim” sleeves,
she looked young enough to be the sister
of her tall tailor-gowned girl, Mrs. Brookes
—sister-in-law, by the way, of the new
world’s tennis champion. The Prime Minister
had a handshake for everybody, and was button-
holed and talked to very seriously at every turn,
what time a hidden orchestra made subdued
music somewhere—apparently behind the cur-
tains of the upper gallery corridors that overlook
the hall. The dressing was particularly blossomy.
Green was the keynote, though. Mrs. Senator
Keating wore a daring Paddy emerald in soft
chiffon velvet. The hats were tremendous. Mrs.
Justice Isaacs led off with a huge “natural”
feather curling from forehead to neck, and making
a fine frame for the dark beauty of her face. And
Mrs. Queensland Groom scored with rosy plumage.
A few folk wandered into the trim gardens, but
after sampling the chilliness outside, they joined
in the general trek to the library, to view a golden
casket holding Mr. Deakin’s “Freedom of the
City of London.” He has handed the bauble
over to the Commonwealth, since it came to him
as Prime Minister. The casket is a gorgeous
thing of solid gold. The Librarian does not feel a
bit happy at having the custody of it. He dreams
of the recent Mint robbery and the missing mace
of olden days.
The party was such a success that some of the
hostesses (the energetic Forrest dame in the
centre) had their heads together at the finish
planning further shivoos for the near future—
with a probable alternative of hostess and a sort-
ing out of guests “in turn,” to keep the parties
jolly and small. Even the good sprinkling of the
frock-coated did not look as if they “ wished they
hadn’t come ” in the bored way of normal man-
kind at an afternoon tea-and-talk. There were
many interesting women to talk to, and a pleasing
dearth of giggle.
Saturday saw Mrs. Bland ’Olt’s departure from
Melbourne for London, via U.S.A. With her go
friend Lucy Coppin and a large Australian-made
collection of everything nice—from lingerie to
fur-fitted overcoats. By another route a cargo
of pictures of ’Rooland origin, table-centres,
locally-woven fabrics, stuffed bunyips, Australian
gems, and platypus furs travel to the same des-
tination.
The lady’s last appearance in a Royal cast drew
an overflowing house, a speech or two, and some
hundredweights of vegetable compliments. The
gallery hurled kindly laments at her, and bawled
farewell with much regret. The Bland’oltian
employees also took the parting in serious vein.
They presented the cheerful lady with a com-
prehensive collection of ironmongery, some toilet
things, and an oppressive load of good wishes.
For all of which she said “thank you” very
nicely, with a broad and cheerful smile.
The loving souls, forced these frosty nights to
find a cosier setting for their amourettings than
the bleak sea-shore, or the gas-box, or the Fitzroy
Gardens, are apt to go to the Town Hall,
on Saturday night, and hear Dr. Price play
the organ. They get into a warm, obscure corner
of the sixpenny seats, and there He murmurs “ I
love yer, Liz,” and She coyly retorts “ Garn ! ” to
their callow hearts’ content. They never heard
good music before, and it moves them strangely.
The surging passion of a “Tannhauser” scene,
rolling down to them from the great organ, and
the exquisite idyll of the garden act from
“Faust,” as it is sung by the vox humana in
Dr. Price’s arrangement, implants unsuspected
flowers of sentiment and weird fungi of
yearning upon their unthinking flirtation.
Before the winter is half over He finds
no pleasure in the meaner delights of holding
hands and chaffing lightly with his girl, and she
has developed a novel shyness and a touch of
hysteria, and insists that they shouldn’t ever miss
“old Price” and “his horgan concert.” The
glamor of the music rushes them into an engage-
ment, and then the Town Hall loses two steady
customers, for they are saving the pair of weekly
sixpences to buy furniture with.
“ Tryphena” writes from London re Clara Butt
and husband
The large impressive warblers who shortly pack their
trunks and batch of babies for Australia, are, away
from the platform, the fondest doves in this Cold
Country. This isn’t a land that runs to many loving
linked pairs. To be linked here is mostly to be drab and
resigned or divorcey and fast. , The Rumford doves are a
beautiful and tranquilising exception. Lunching at
Compton Lodge in the leafiest, breeziest quarter
of St. John’s Wood, you settle with a sigh of
relief to the glad conditions. (I speak with feel-
ing, having been at several swagger feasts where
my smart host and hostess snapped and snarled and
sneered at each other whenever there was a wretched
gap in the tiffin talk. Horrible 1) The only break in the
glad course of a Compton Lodge meal occurs when one
of the babies upstairs crows in the wrong key, or cries
out of tune. Then one of the doves sheds the serviette and
tears upstairs to restore the atom to tunefulness, or
check it with a series of overwhelming hugs.
“Excuse me,” baritoned the large Clara once, “ I hear
Joy crying. I can’t bear her sobs.” And the feast was
virtually suspended while baby was comforted. Everyone
resumed operations with gusto when the colossal hostess
trumpeted forth contented talk from her end of the table
once more.
Clara Butt is not actively glad, or fussily cheerful, or
exuberantly gay—she’s just happy in a large, heavy, con-
tented form. The explanation is not temperament, but
intellect. There’s very little intellect behind the good,
big brow of Clara Butt. The mind is heavy. It’s built on
a scale proportionate to the rest of Clara, and is burdened
probably by its own massiveness. Consequently, the
greatest contralto in Europe doesn’t effervesce at talk.
One just rests placidly in her splendid presence,
and offers no resistance to the slow course of
table or tea chat. But Clara Butt is mighty
and maternal and good. The whole six-feet-two
of her impressive stature is an argument for wholesome,
happy motherhood. Clara Butt looks a mother, and
deep in her large bosom lurks a desire to abide always
somew'here near the nursery, instead of revolving among
the exalted of the globe.
Yet in song the large olive countenance of Clara glows,
and a slow warmth kindles the brown eyes and some-
times emotion gets them brimming. “ I used to make
myself ridiculous” she told me, “quite ridiculous by
letting my feelings overwhelm me. I would be standing
before my audience with the tears streaming down my
face. But not now. It took too much from my' art. I
couldn’t afford it.”
The contralto’s throat is the most wonderful organ
imaginable. It’s not only massive, it’s muscular and
assertive. It’s not beautiful—it’s extraordinary. Every
word uttered by the giant artiste is almost like a billed
performance. It’s a deep, slow, musical note—deep and
full.
Clara Butt is not a mistress of the art of how to dress,
but she’s the owner of some of the most gorgeous clothes
in the Big Smoke. And that’s a violently bold thing to
assert. For hereabouts the passion for clothes rages and
moves the entire sex to risk its soul for its chiffons.
Clara has put i fortune into her platform raiment, and
audiences are left staggered by the splendor of her
creations.
The way Clara stalks or rolls or strides on to a platform
is a matter of big attractiveness in the eyes of this Fog-
town, though it’s u Fogtowm that likes (openly and
frankly) few natural charms in the sex. It prefers its sex
displaying the calm and coldness of a frozen cod. Clara,
sometimes in an absent-minded moment or On the rolling
tide of song, squats on the lid of the grand piano and
completes her lilt in that free-and-easy attitude. And
London likes it. At a recent sing-song, the platform
manner of Clara as compared with that of Albani and
Ada Crossley (our own Ada) came in for vast and flatter-
ing comment. Each artiste was handed up a towering
basket of flowers. Albani was the first on whom the
trophy was served. She took it up, smiled coldly (in an
explanatory way) at its circumference, and left it on the
platform. Ada gave one of her best deportment bows
from the meridian, and summoned a male to march
the monster souvenir off with her. Then Clara was heard
in an organ wealth of melody, after which she stooped,
took a mighty grip of the basket, and slung it (navvy-
fashion) overher massive shoulder. And this brought the
crowd to a great gush of delight and laughter. The
snortful few availed nought.
If Clara leap-frogs (for a short cut) over a reception
committee, don’t be surprised. She’s the most unaffected
natural star on the whole stage or platform of Europe. ,
Kennerly-Rumford is a beauty-man, and he has manners
to match. There’s a delightful charm about Clara’s
mate. People hereabouts talk haughtily of the K.R.’s
and their good breeding. It’s understood that when
the starring vocalists elected to duet it domestically for
ever and aye, with a ring and a church blessing thrown
in (duets in the Smoke thrive without either, and quite
in a swaggah atmosphere too), the Cold Country gasped
“Impossible!' The Kennerly-Rumfords are so particu-
lar. They’ll want birth.” Clara was alleged to have
none. No family-tree—not even a small twig. But the
duet came off, and the two are the happiest in England.
The Willie A’Becketts, who began their
married life with a Maoriland tour, are tired of
their honeymoon finale at Menzies’. They are
now entering quite as gaily into housekeeping,
and have taken the Ricardo house on Toorak-
road for a year. Hubby, in his days of irrespon-
sibility, had a big place of domicile in Dande-
nong-road, but the little bride declared it too
single-manlike for her fancy. “The dining and
smoking rooms are fine,” she declares, but the
rest of the house is weird and uncultivated, and
altogether unsuitable —for a married man. What
she might add, perhaps, is that the reminiscences
which cling to the dining and smoking rooms are
even more unsuitable for its one-time bachelor
occupant in his new—and happier—state. Let
not the bridegroom dwell in a reminiscent atmo-
sphere, where the odors of bygone tobaccos may
corrupt, and visions break through and steal, saith
the preacher.
Some of Melbourne’s smartest drapery “hands”
and figures are going to hold a winter show on
their own. The employees of Buckley and Nunn
have arranged a conversazione and dance for Aug.
Ist, at the Masonic Hall.
Why is it usual for the smartly-dressed, rather-
dandified, married man (the man who always has
an eye and a flattering word for likely-looking
girleens) to own a little, frumpy, frightened-
looking wife ? How was he attracted in the first
place ? And, in any case, why does he not use a little
judicious flattery and suggestion to tone her up
now that he’s got her ? Ornamental hubbies seem
to hide their lawful little frumps in the back-
ground, only trotting them round in a shamed
sort of perfunctory way at big official functions
and such shivoos, where it’s necessary to produce
your wife, if you’ve got one. Also, the sanie
questions and the same comment apply with equal
force to the dashing wife of the meek little citizen,
who is of no importance apart from his salary.
She rarely trots him round when his escort can be
avoided. It’s a law of compensation.
During the week the Athemeum Club will hang
a subscription picture of Justice Edward Holroyd,
and present him with a smaller replica. The
portrait—for which Leslie Wilkie, one of our
youngest artists, is responsible—depicts the little
judge, in a big judicial chair and his robes and
wig. The face is very faithfully done—a trifle
idealised, perhaps—and suggests that the judge
has an acquittal on the tip of his tongue ; and the
treatment of the strident scarlet draperies is
entirely successful.
Mrs. Dr. Hewlitt has always been rather
famous for the special atmosphere of her dainty
little dinners and card parties at the big grey
outer-city house, but recently there happened an
exciting and unexpected addition to the enter-
tainment. It was a Bridge party, and the players
were deep in calculations, when smoke came
tumbling up and flames gleamed on the premises.
A few buckets of water put out the conflagra-
tion, and Judkins’ sermon on how a Bridge party
was burned to a cinder in the midst of its sins
remains undelivered. And it would have been
such a thrilling sermon.
It was no sort of a 4th of July without the
genial Consul-General for the U.S.A. He had
flitted Northward, partly to get quit of some pro-
perty he recently acquired—to wit, a spacious
cold ; partly for other reasons. Vice-Consul
Merrill and his consort recepted in good ’Murri-
can style at the consulate. “Old Glory” was
much in evidence, tempered with the Union Jack
and Australian wattle. Consuls gathered in full
force, and discussed high politics and great inter-
national complications over fermented and spirit-
uous liquors. Womenfolk, many and various,
wrestled with tea, coffee and .an infinitude of
cates. There was no wearisome boldiug-forth
about “the day we celebrate.” If the Land o’
Liberty was toasted at all it must have been in the
inmost recesses of the consulate. The Prime
Minister bestowed a careworn smile on the recep-
tion. Henry Wrixon glanced at it nervously,
said the right thing to host and hostess, and fled.
Great times have come to cinematograph people,
dog showmen, and Punch and Judy folk. Young-
sters have pronounced in their favor, and children
are having it all their own way just now. The
noble army of commercial travellers gave a mam-
moth party, recently, to something like 700 plump
and pleasing young people. Never saw a prettier
lot—from toddlers to rising 15’s. They took pos-
session of the Masonic Hall. The C.T.A. men
made ideal hosts. Secretary Davies worked feel-
ingly, for he is the proud parent of something
very new in babies, and most of the other
men are married, and the lot of them were
tumbling over one another to amuse other
people’s children. Fathers score ’way above
mothers in this respect. You don’t see a mother
hustling much on behalf of Mrs. Brown’s Tommy
—not if she’s got a Tommy of her own. The
feeding of the guests began at four o’clock, and
went on, in relays, till six, and after that, at in-
tervals, till nine.- Odd intervals were filled up
with distributions of crackers, lollies, and squeaky
toys of all sorts, to say nothing of dances on
the platform, dances on the floor, marches, bag-
pipes, dog shows, bilious attacks, and so on.
All the schools have been breaking out into
breaking-up parties. Best of the lot was that of
the three motherly-looking little spinsters, the
Misses Turner, who seem to have given half the
leading men in this metropolis their educational
start in life. Their school in South Yarra
dominates a big slice of landscape, and is audible
anywhere within a half-mile radius. The little
ladies have week-end places of their own in the
hills and by the sea, where they trip off with
batches of boarders every now and then. These
trips naturally provoke comment. One little
Turner woman took train one day with ten small
boys. Enter that good old inquire-within-upon-
everything who haunts every railway route.
“My!” she ejaculated, after surveying the
youngsters. “Have you got nothing but boys?”
Guilelessly the dainty little spinster answered in
the affirmative. “Ah, well,” continued the
enquirer. “You can never tell. I had eight
boys myself before I had a girl.”
On July 1. Mr. John Dana and Miss Dorothy
Hunter took up the burden of holy matrimony.
The knot was hitched together at St. Paul’s
Cathedral, rather quietly. An overseas honey-
moon of long duration is projected by the con-
tented pair. The dainty bride is the eldest joy
of an Elmore squatter.
The much-advertised poster carnival, at the
South Yarra rink, turned out to be first cousin to
a second-rate football match. The arrangements
couldn’t have been worse. Skaters and onlookers
had to look out for themselves, and only free-
fighters could get admission after 10 minutes to 8.
The show in the rink was good, and a lot of the
dresses were clever, but many of the best had sus-
tained damage in the struggle before they took
the floor.
Another claim for damages caused by a broken
vow was brought against a grey-haired matron,
not so long ago, by a disappointed suitor,
30 years her junior. When her well-gilded
family considered the matter, they concluded
that they would prefer mamma to retain her
political prestige and the respect due to wrinkles
and snowy hair, rather than see her acquire an in-
fantile hubby of imperceptible social position, and
lose her dignity. The disappointed lover was
made to see things in that light, too. Since then,
the aged lady has suppressed the eternal feminine,
and devotes herself to politics in a dispassionate,
painstaking way.
From “ Rosna ” in Adelaide :
Adelaide hasn’t caught much of a glimpse of the new
aide yet. He is being tamed at the Residence (where he
can be heard barking on still nights), and taught the cor-
rect method of handling vice-regal wraps. His sportive
Ex. took him for a day’s quail shooting in the wilds,
either for use as a retriever or just by way of an airing ;
and I know a girl who declares her second cousin’s first
wife by a previous marriage saw' him picking a nosegay in
the vice-regal garden. But this is not actually authen-
ticated. We have now three bachelor aides at the
residence, and w'e intend to marry one of them, even if
we have to climb over our ow»n dead body to do it. -
Carreno of the curly-cue gave three recitals in Tor-
toise-tovvn last week, but although the musical popula-
tion crowded in long-haired herds to hear her, her houses
were not as packed as they deserved to be. Fact is she
was too modest jn her prices. If she' had charged a
guinea a la Paderewski, or even half, the whole peerage
would have put in an appearance (pun strictly copy-
right). Our peerage likes to feel it is paying a whole
golden coin for its seat, partly to show that it has the
coin and partly because it can then be quite certaip it is
not being jostled by the vulgar herd. (There’s a story
that a certain sbion of the snobocracy ordered the mana-
ger for one of last year’s most fashionable complimentary
concerts to cross off the seat next to his lot, in case any
common person should sit beside them.) All the
musical ton of the place, headed by Dr. Conservatorium
Ennis, flocked to the station to meet Carreno, and wel-
comed her formally at the South Australian after-
wards. .The usual compliments were exchanged,
and the star smiled amiably upon everyone.
Their Exes., with Miss Clowes and Captain-aide Ryder,
disported themselves at the Thursday evening concert;
also the Chief Justice Ways, Mr. and Mrs. Herbert
Rymill, the Bishop and Bishopess, the Melrose clan, and
hordes of other well-known folk. The star wore a
gorgeous robe of pale blue brocade, scattered over with
roses. She had some costly floral produce shot at her
between acts. Carreno finished with a matinee perform-
ance, and tore madly off to catch the Melbourne express.
In her haste she forgot to pack up the whirly thing she
wears over the “ n ” in her name, and it had to be, tele-
graphed after her.
The rink has closed with a slam, and melted into a
common C.vclorama room again. The smart girly popu-
lace immediately never smiled again, like Henry I.
The four clever Jurs maidens manipulated the pro-
gramme at the Lyric Club last week, and displayed a lot
of skill in the noisy art. They are a versatile family-r-
-two pianists, a fiddler, and an elocutionist. Also, lam
told, one of them paints.
The Row'ing Club ball drew shoals of carriage company
to the North Adelaide Institute last week. It was a
man-managed affair, and in getting up a ball the male of
our species proves that he isn’t so superfluous as he
looks. The Excellencies were present with the psual
procession of nice brass-buttoned aides, and got through
the lancers creditably.
Miss Jessie Ackermann, who is rushing through Aus-
tralia to tell the Westminster Gazette and other papers
whether the kangaroo still wears his pocket in his chest,
and how bad the rabbits are in Melbourne, spent a few
days in South Oss this week. She was greatly welcomed,
and all the societies gave her a reception ; in fact, she
was busy being feted most of the three days, and hadn’t
a chance to inquire why it was that the rabbits were so
bad in Melbourne. Since she was in Australia, 15 years
ago, Miss Ackermann has put five waist-bands round the
globe.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.
