SYDNEY.
POLITICAL
POINTS
“Mr. Reid is away in Sydney with Mr.
Spence advocating preference for unionists.”
That was the neatly malevolent way in which the
Age noticed G. Reid’s absence from the opening
of Parliament.
Sydney did not offer Prime Minister
Deakin a public welcome on behalf of its citizeris,
despite the good service he rendered to Australia
while in London. But a proposal to extend a
public welcome to the Maoriland football team
was hailed with enthusiasm.
Said that Iceberg Irvine is getting im-
patient as he freezes in an Opposition corner.
The arrangement by which G. Reid leads the
Opposition in name, and Joseph Cook in fact,
leaves no room for Irvine to squeeze in. There is
a move ahead to get the Opposition to ask Reid
to retire from the leadership. In that case Cook’s
chances of succeeding are said to be hopeless.
N.S. Wales’ general election prospects are
pretty cheerless all round. Even the Evening
News’ Sussex-street market reports are damp
and discouraging :
TO-DAY’S COMMERCIAL.
AN EGG-FAMINE.
SCARCEST FOR 20 YEARS.
F. C. Monger, M.L.A., of Westralia, states
that he is going to “ resume ” his advocacy of the
secession movement in Westralia, when he gets
properly rested. So far, Monger’s advocacy
hasn’t caused much ripple on the surface of
events, and his proposed resumption has been
received with the same profound calm which
greets the news that little Johnny, being now
restored to health, intends to take up the multi-
plication-table where he left off.
Even the hitherto faithful Argus now
comments on the infrequency of G. Reid at
the Australian Parliament, and asks him to
either gird up his loins for the coming fray,
or throw up the sponge and be dead alto-
gether. In the case of the Anti-Sosh champion
being too tired, or too moribund, to rise and
scatter Deakin, the paper seems to think that
the sword should be handed over to Bill Wilks or
Bruce Smith. The joy of B. Smith at being sized
up as fit for the same job as B. Wilks will be
something tremendous.
WHICH ONE WILL HE CHOOSEI
JAPAN: “ Full up ! Then one of you'll have to
come off the perch.”
Under the heading, “ Nuts for Socialists,”
S.M. Herald quotes from Saleeby’s recent
“Individualism and Collectivism ” :
The individualist must for ever be opposed to any form
of rule whatever which indicates the sacrifice of the
liberty of any individuals, however humble, to the in-
terests of other individuals, however distinguished.
Why don’t these people leave off their vague
platitudes, and come down to detail? For in-
stance :
The individualist must for ever be opposed to the law
which forbids men the liberty of selling cancerous meat
or diseased milk, or of passing bad money, or of gaining any
other advantage over their weaker or simpler neighbors.
Then, if they were frank like that, we should
know that Anti-Sosh is simply anarchy when it
isn’t mere savagery.
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy is famous
for its cures of coughs, colds, and croup.*
Dr. Arthur, who represents
Middle Harbor in the N.S. W. Assem-
bly, and who is a desperately earnest
man with a large Australian outlook,
lost in the lifeless gathering of small
characters who drift behind the Deform
Government, is to be opposed by a Syd-
ney business man named Scott Fell.
No one knows much about Scott
Fell save that he is doing fairly well
for himself in an office. The ideal
politician thinks, works and plans for
his country. The ideal business man
thinks, works, schemes, toils and grabs
for himself. And the business man,
in the vastness of his conceit, always
believes that a life spent in the sodden
selfishness of toiling exclusively for
self makes him just the unselfish,
altruistic sort of person who is calcu-
lated to do the best for the country.

for membership.
From the market reports of Sydney Tele-
graph :
The market for rice remained well controlled at the
recently higher levels, best Japan being firmly held at
£22 10s., and locally dressed from about £lB to £2l,
according to quality. Export orders in ration rice for
the Islands were repeated from £lO to £lO 10s.
Which serves as an illustration of why Fat yearns
for the colored person as an employee. The coolie
imported into Fiji from his Asiatic home is fed
on “ration rice,” at less than half the cost of
the ordinary article.
Premier Carruthers’ suggestion that Colonel
Ryrie, M.L.A. and amateur boxer, would make a
good Speaker of the N.S.W. Assembly, as he
could combine the offices of Speaker and Sergeant-
at-Arms, has the character of a loaded joke.
There is no sufficient reason why a Sergeant-at-
Arms, costing about £5OO a year, should be em-
ployed as an officer pf the House. The days of
physical resistance to a Speaker’s authority have
passed away, and, in any case, a Speaker’s order
to any of the policemen about the premises would,
if resistance did show itself, be ample for all
emergencies. Black Rod and Sergeant-at-Arms
are faded frills of a Parliamentary garment long
ago worn out.
In anticipation of increased Customs im-
posts, many merchants have lately been paying off
the duty on the goods which they have piled up
in bonded stores. In many cases the rush was
so great that there wasn’t room in the
importer’s warehouse for the goods, so he just left
them in the bonded store, duty paid. Now a
horrible impression is abroad that so long as the
goods remain in the bonded store they are liable
for any increase of duty, and another rush has set
in—to get the goods out this time. And even
hardened footballers, and people who are accus-
tomed to fight their way up the gallery steps when
Anderson is playing “The Eye on the Floor,”
say that this is something like a rush.
There are now only about 664 square
miles of Victoria that are not under some form of
local government—city, town, borough, or shire.
Of the 664 miles, about 600 are around Wonnan-
gatta, the most rugged part of Gippsland. But
it is intended to sweep this remnant in, and it
won’t be long before a shire councillor will hang a
promise on to the topmost peak. The other 64
unincorporated miles take in French Island.
The filling up of the “casual ” vacancy for
a Senator in S.A. is likely to cause trouble. The
Federal Attorney-General. says the vacancy is
casual. Three very high and expensive legal
opinions have been taken by the S.A. State
Government,, and they are all to the effect that
the vacancy is quite casual. And the conduct Of
the returning officers who muddled the election
was casual enough for anything. The bone of
contention is this: Crosbie was elected. He
died. While on his death-bed he was defeated
on a recount. After he was quite dead,
a High Court investigation (which was in-
complete because a very casual person had
allowed some ballot papers to be destroyed)
created the opinion that Crosbie had been rightly
elected, after ail, Under these circumstances, it
may be a casual vacancy caused by the death of
Crosbie, or it may be that the election was null
and void through the casual destruction of the
ballot papers. If the vacancy is quite casual, the
S.A. Parliament appoints a man on cheap prin-
ciples, and with Price as Premier, the appointee
will be a Democrat for celtain. If it isn’t
casual, there must be a huge, expensive election,
at which a Tory may get in, though the chances
are against him. Hence the row about the casual-
ness.
S.M. Herald reviews Moreing’s “Life
and Labor in Germany ” in the spirit one might
expect from the paper’s bigoted adherence to the
foreign importers. Mr. Moreingsent a number of
British workmen over to Germany to see for
themselves how German workmen live, and the
book tells what they saw. The Herald hints
that the favorable reports of the British workmen
were influenced by their knowledge of Mr.
Moreing’s opinion, and then goes on to say that,
anyhow, they weren’t reliable observers: —
It is conceivable that a deputation of English work-
men, without knowledge of German, or any particular
skill in the unravelling of social conditions, would not be,
in every respect, a trustworthy guide in the matter. The
reports, however, make interesting reading, and contain
much information as to the desirableness of being a
workerin Germany ratherthan in Greatßritain. As to what
the position will be when the “ boom” in German manu-
factures comes to an end—as indicated in recent cables—
there is nothing said in this pamphlet.
The sneer about the workmen not knowing Ger-
man is rich, since Mr. James Fairfax went to
Japan without knowing a word of Japanese, and
learnedly discoursed about the country when he
came back. And the Herald was quite sure,
without knowing any Taal at all, iaat the Boers
were a bad lot, and should be suppressed. It
couldn’t even hear their side of the case except
through an interpreter, but that made no differ-
ence.
THE ONE THING WANTING.
Mr. Deakin might accomplish a great work for the
Commonwealth were he able to heal all provincial divi-
sions and speak for a really united Australia.— Argus.
GRANNY : “’ Ere! How can there be any united
Australia with ’im out?”
Quite apart from the merits of the case,
Carruthers’ talk to a Sydney Municipal Council
deputation the other day gave a valuable hint as to
the way the Australian Government ought to talk
to Carruthers (the relative positions are about the
same). Carruthers gave the Council a severe lec-
ture about its mismanagement, and told it to
clean up its dirty spots before it talked about its
grievances against the State. There is sore need
of an Australian statesman who will say just that
and no less to Carruthers, and say it in public.
A new Australian-built lifeboat for use at
Watson’s Bay, Sydney, has just been launched.
When Carruthers agreed to get a lifeboat in
place of the imported ark hitherto in use, his
advisers pooh-poohed the idea of a locally-built
craft, and begged him to get one made in Eng-
land. The Cockatoo Dock people, however, in-
sisted that they could do the job, and pleaded so
hard for the chance that they got it. And they
have done their work well. The boat has
answered every test, except a practical one—and
that isn’t the boat’s fault, for up to now no gale
has transpired that the boat could be shoved into.
Thus the Sydney Worker’s London let-
ter
The distress in Leicester (Eng.) is terrible. The men
registered on the unemployed register of the Corpora-/
tion’s Distress Committee are mostly confined to the boot 1
industry. The depression is attributed to the unre-
stricted competition of American goods.
As Leicester is the constituency represented by
that queer anomaly, Labor-Freetrader Ramsay
Macdonald, a violent alteration of opinion should
be about due thereabouts. Either Macdonald
should alter his opinion about Freetrade, and the
glories of unrestricted foreign competition, or his
constituents should alter their opinion about
Macdonald. After all, lack of work means lack
of food, be the Freetraders’ boasted breakfast
table ever so free.
“ Falk ” from a small Tassy town
Tasmania, my country, how long shall the Federal
abomination oppress us? Shall we alone be silent? Look
at Sydney ! The right given her by the Constitution to
plaoe the Federal capital in Woolloomooloo is being trifled
with, an i even now she rallies to the banner of revolt.
Look at Westralia ! The transcontinental railway has not
been built within six weeks of Federation, as provided in
Clause 99J8, and her miners are marching overland on
Melbourne with picks and drills. Queensland knows how
clearly it was understood that the F’ederal Government
should never address her without first asking permission,
and the next insult will be avenged in blood. And we—
are we worms ? Shall we not also claim our rights ? Have
we not apple cases for barricades, and hop po'es for
offence? Was it not clearly understood that we should
have a Straits tunnel —or was it a free copy of the
Watchman all round, or—oh, tell me, someone, what was
it? What are our wrongs? It’s no good looking in the
Federal Constitution, because the Victorian printers
suppressed the clause I want. It’s true that my copy
says it was printed at Sydney, but I am not so easily
deceived. More Victorian cunning ! If Mr. Carruthers
would only come !

THE PEER: “ It's true I'm a darned nuisance,
but anyway I'm the best darned nuisance .”
There was a panic in Maoriland Cow
circles, t’other day, and a number of teat culti-
vators got up in a violent hurry and yelped
perturbedly when some of the factory hands took
it into their heads to form a union. If only half of
what the average agricultural paper prints about
the wonderful returns from dairying be true, there
is no industry better able to afford decent conditions
of labor than the industry of the bull’s aunt.
Nevertheless, the stereotyped cry went wailing
up and down the Cow country, that if the agita-
tion for better wages were persisted in it would
mean blue ruin and .red revolution, and a grey
desolation would fall on the business, and all its
prospects would be dun brown. The outlook, the
cow-farmers say, is black. Apparently the indus-
tries which call themselves the backbone of the
country are a great deal more limp than those
that don’t even call themselves gristle. '

and lung affections. No other remedy has such
a soothing, healing effect on the throat, lungs,
stomach and bowels* and no other remedy will so
invariably promote appetite, aid digestion, and
build up health and strength. In three sizes, of
all chemists.*

“The Gunyah” writes :
The Victorian Government has thought better of it,
and decided not to be another cantankerous insect
buzzing round the head of the Commonwealth ; so, at the
last Cabinet meeting it was decided, “ after due con-
sideration,” to allow the Commonwealth Government to
remain in possession of the lately disputed Patent Office
Library. The Bulletin par referring to the proposed trans-
fer of the library must have lifted the vei from the eyes,
not only of the Ministry, but also of the Argus, which had
previously rushed into ridiculous articles anent the
“brazen effrontery” of the Commonwealth in stealing
from Melbourne its Patent Office Library. They had all
evidently believed that the Commonwealth had removed
valuable and rare law', books, instead of patent specifica-
tions which are of no use to anyone save those interested
in patent matters.
The mail brings the full text of Tommy
Bent’s uncouth comments on England’s demon-
strations of goodwill towards Botha. “In my
country—in Australia—we would not have shouted
ourselves hoarse at him,” said Bent. “We
remember things. We have a lot of wooden legs,
armless men, and sorrowing widows to keep our
memory green.” T. Bent had the effrontery to
talk like this, not in the name of his own cabbage-
beds at Brighton, but in the name of all Aus-
tralia, as if Australia were exactly the same sort
of dull, stale, fat, humorless mass as Bent is.
Australia did make something of an ass of itself
over the S’African war ; but it has not remained
an ass, much less increased to such a stupendous,
ravening jackass as to want to throw mud at
Botha because he and his countrymen, in fighting
for their own fatherland, damaged a few Aus-
tralians who were not fighting for anything much
beyond pure devilment. Bent afterwards ex-
plained the situation by saying that he always
spoke straight out, arid said at once what was in
his thoughts. Solomon of Jerusalem foresaw
Bent when he said “ A fool uttereth all his mind.”

The N.S.W. Government is in a, state of
paralysis about six things (and about five of them
a whole lot of previous Governments have been
equally paralysed): (1) Greater Sydney ; (2) the
tramway congestion ; (3) the widening of George-
street; (4) the North Shore bridge ; (5) the exten-
sion of the railway into the city; (6) the Newcastle
harbor difficulty. It ku nvs exactly what the
Australian Government should do about every-
thing, and is simply boiling over with good advice
on Federal matters, but its own little job has
reduced it to chaos.
The twentieth State Parliament of N.S.W.
crowds a fourth session into its term. It pro-
mises to be just about the record Parliament for
hanging on to the last. N.S.W. had seven Par-'
liaments under the old law which provided that
an Assembly might last five years. The first
three endured a little over one year each—the
political sea was very stormy then. A couple
lasted a little over two years; one nearly four
years, and one nearly four years and ten months.
The present variety of Parliament that is sup-
posed to die in three years really lasts longer than
the old one that was supposed to live five years.
Under the triennial system, the Assembly that
hung on to life most tenaciously was the last one
(the 19th) ; it pegged out only eight days
short of its time. Premier Carruthers might put
up a record by letting this one perish natur-
ally on August 23. So far, two N.S.W. Assem-
blies have lasted six sessions; two five ses-
sions ; six have seen four sessions; five have
lived through three sessions; four only lasted
a couple of sessions; and a solitary one—
George Reid’s first Assembly—died in its first
session. Also, George Reid had the shortest-lived
Parliament; it lasted 10 months and 29 days.
The shortest session was the three-days meeting,
in 1904. The eleventh Parliament was a remark-
able animal in its way. There were six sessions.
The first lasted four months; the second, five
days; the fourth, eight days; the fifth, nine days;
and the sixth, 23 days. It was practically all
recess. The longest N.S.W. session lasted 11
months and eight days. The shortest-lived Minis-
try lasted 37 days. The most dishonest and
wooden-headed Ministry—but this par is already
too lengthy.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
No other Australian pugilist’s glory went out so
suddenly as that of Billiam Squires. After the oceans
of advertising ink that had been poured over him, after
the 6600 miles he had travelled, and the $5OOO he had
posted, to be laid out with three successive wallops was
too dreadful. And the worst of it is there’s no excuse to
be made. He was not beaten by any chance smack, but
bv superior fighting. He had met a man who declined
to be either rushed or bustled, and, like most other
fighters of the rushing fraternity, was just about helpless.
The only redeeming feature is his sterling pluck. Out-
geueralied, stunned, and, for the first time in his boxing
career, knocked down, the Australian went at his
opponent like a bulldog, and came up gamely from each
knockdown blow till he was stunned. Private cables tell
how Burns foiled Squires’ bull-dog rush by dancing
away like a bouncing ball on the end of a string, keeping
just out of effective range of the Australian’s blows.
Much of this dancing seems to have been done on one foot.
Squires rained rib-smashers, but Burns kept his ribs out of
range, and at the first sign of slackening on Squires’ part
Burns danced forward on the other foot and smote him
on the temple. With the light fighting-glove this ought
to have been a knock-out blow. It was the same blow
with which Burns, two years before, had battered a rival
pii"- so that he died within a few minutes. When Squires
ai-ose, Burns realised that he was up against something
extra’tough, and resumed his bouncing-ball retreat till
he got another opportunity, when he floored the Aus-
tralian a second time. When Squires got up again, he
was dazed, “ducking imaginary blows, trying to clinch
an imaginary opponent,’ and then Burns, ceasing his
ball-on-a-string dance, walked up, coolly measured his
distance, and smote the dazed Australian a finishing blow
on the chin. Beads brutal—but it is pugilism. The
trouble with Squires and his people was that they mistook
for skill a mere ability to rush in and hit hard, regardless
of punishment. In plain English, the Australian didn t
know how to fight, or he would not have walked into the
obvious tran laid for him.
* * * *
However, John Wren apparently believes firmly
that Squires is a champion, and he has made an effort to
•ret him another chance. The effort reflects much credit
on John Wren. The fact that he will probably make
money out of the event, if it comes off, does not detract
in the slightest from the merit of his action. On Monday
last he cabled to ’Frisco, offering Burns £5OO and_all the
o-ate —-win, lose, or draw —to fight Squires again - within a
month. Canadians are all rather keen after money, but
Burns has the reputation of being a regular Bluenose by
instinct, if not by birth, and unless he is tied up in some
way with other engagements (and there is no word of
anything of the sort just now), he will probably rush the
offer. A really dramatic finish to this little comedy
would be for Squires to refuse to meet Burns again.
There have been cases where men, hitherto regarded as
plucky, have, after a bad walloping, resolutely refused to
hold any further converse with the gentleman who sup-
plied it. * a •
“Micawber”: The recent demolition of William
Squires brings home, with painful suddenness, the wisdom
of Larry Foley’s remarks at a “ welcome ” tendered to
our black brudder, Jack Johnson, some several months
ago. During his discourse, Foley said : “ Squires is no
champion, but he is the pick of a bad lot, and may, be
depended upon to do his best.” Speaking to the veteran
last week, I asked his opinion as to the ability of the
Australian to beat Burns. “My boy,” said he, “if Squires
beats the Yankee, then all I can say is that the standard
of American boxing is down to bedrock.” Regretfully I
admit that the old-timer knew what he was talking about.
* ft * *
The big impetus boxing has received'in Vic-
toria is demonstrated by the success of recent amateur
and novice tournaments. The scraps and “ rastles” for
the Ballarat district championships drew big crowds
every night, and were a source of fat profits to the
VA.A.A. which ran them, besides bringing to light a
few boxers who are likely to figure in inter-State
struggles. The novice tournaments at the Athletic
Pavilion have been rushed by both competitors and cus-
tomers. The latest drew 50 triers and an audience on the
opening night that overflowed Wren’s establishment, a
rotunda capable of containing 4000 spectators. The
boxing of the novices was much superior to that in pre-
vious tournaments, the gameness and pertinacity of the
lads being particularly remarkable. Thirty pounds was
offered to the winner in each division, with cash prizes for
bout winners and runners-up. The management was
good, but the judgment was far from sound in many in-
stances, and defective refereeing knocks the spirit out of
a tournament. The best fight of the night, D. Walker v.
J. Mitchell, middleweights, was given to Walker, although
Mitchell was unquestionably his master, and had him any-
how at the finish.. Entries for the Slate amateur cham-
pionships, to be battled out this montn, are very numer-
ous, and a lust for slugging is abroad in the land.
“0.K.”: From the article on John Wren in the
first number of The Lone Hand :—“ Wren’s fights from first
to last have been worthy of their pro noter. The Noble
Art as the Tote Boss presented it to a gullible, hot-headed
public was the art of advertisement, whereof his show-
man Barney Reynolds is a rare old master. Having
engaged honest Bill Squires as a ‘ star ’ performer, for
gate-money purposes, the management had to fix up
other famous champions to fall before Bill’s rugged on-
slaught.” Now that poor Bill has been biffed clean off
his pedestal, it is interesting to remember that The Lone
Hand was the first hand to point a doubting finger at his
boxing capabilities.
* » * *
Someone recently thanked his Maker that the
spirit of professionalism is entirely absent from Australian
sport—especially cricket. And it is a matter for con-
gratulation. Apropos: The players are very shocked at
the proposal of the Board of Control to reduce the allow-
ance for playing against the English XI. from £25 to £l5,
exclusive of expenses and payment for loss of time. There
will be five matches, and, at £l5 per match, each amateur
will receive a lump sum of £75, and, in addition to that,
draw £5 ss. per week for expenses while out of his own
district. Almost immediately after, the same amateur
will have a chance to be shipped to England for a plea-
sant trip and for the purpose of making anything between
£7OO and £lOOO all for himself. Yes, it is fortunate our
players are all amateurs ! It is to maintain the “high”
standard of amateurism that the players want special
representation on the Australian Board of Control.
Turns out that N.S.W. Premier Carruthers’
threat to do something sudden and dreadful has not quite
broken the back of Sydney Cricket Ground Trustees, and
the question of what fees shall be paid for the use of
Sydney Cricket Ground remains unsettled. The stumbling-
block appears to be Richard Teece, who doesn’t seem to
care a hang whether the English XI. comes or not. He
reckons the Association is bluffing, and that it must
climb down to the Cricket Ground’s monopoly. Being on
the University Senate, he is able to block the Association
getting the use of the University Oval on the ground that
the applause of the people who watch the cricket disturbs
the studies of the professors. (The yells of the football
barrackers do not disturb the learned gentlemen.) The
only way to crumple up Mr. Teece is for the Association
to show him that it is in a position to kick back
with both feet. It is no good trusting to Joe Carruthers
to shift Teece ; the latter has too many friends in high
places whom Joe will not offend. Therefore, the Asso-
ciation can make up its mind that, unless it can retaliate
on the Cricket Trustees very severely, it will have to
pay the 20 per cent, after all. One of the best ways to
get back at the Trustees would be to play the inter-State
matches at Rushcutters Bay, and leave the big ground a
yawning space every Saturday afternoon. The chances
are that once the Trustees found the Association was
determined to do that, they would over-rule Teece as a
belligerent character who was likely to get them into
serious trouble.
* * * *
“ Blister ”: Is this a record ? A team of rustic
cricketers from Creswick(Vic.) tripped to Dean, and dis-
posed of a local eleven for one run, and that was a bye.
It seems as remarkable in its way as the case of the man
who held 13 trumps at whist and didn’t take a trick.
* * * *
The cause of muscular Christianity in Sydney
loses a powerful and healthy advocate by the departure,
for a holiday trip, of J. J. Virgo, the able and energetic
secretary of the Y.M.C. A. However, the chances are that
on his return he will find athletic virtue going on
serenely. It was J.J.V. who started the Y.M.C. A. Foot-
ball Club, and made it a body of young men who play a
rattling good game, and take the hard knocks as well as
any of them. Since he ran the Y.M.C.A. it has been a
body that any decent, self-respecting man could join, and
the long, gaunt face and woebegone expression have
entirely disappeared. The heathen in his blindness can
now stroll into the big building in Pitt-street, and get a
head put on him by scientific boxers, or have his ribs
soundly whacked by up-to-date exponents of the use of
single-sticks. The Association gave the departing secre-
tary a good send-off, and he deserved it.
* * * * *
The return N.S.W.-Queensland Rugby match
at Brisbane was a close thing. Messenger scored a try
and a goal for N.S.W., and Watson scored a try for
Queensland. After that both teams had several shots at
goal and missed only by inches, and the game resulted
in a victory for the Ma State by six points to three. The
team returned to Sydney on Tuesday morning, and the
“ All Blacks ” expedition left Wellington on Saturday
last, after sitting on the head of Wellington Province to
the tune of 19 to six. If the steamer kept time that
expedition will have arrived in Sydney before the public
holds this in its excited hands.
* •* * *
With the cracks away in Brisbane, the Sydney
barrackers expected to have indifferent football in Syd-
ney, but the crushing of St. George by North Sydney and
the downing of Balmain by University were great sights,
while the struggle between Glebe and Eastern Suburbs
was strenuous enough for anything. Eastern Suburbs
team, without its mighty Messenger, could not make
headway against the heavy bullockir.g pack of the Glebe,
and was overwhelmed by six points to nil. It was
by the weight of its pack that Sydney ran over
Manly to the tune of 23 to three. The Newtown
men ' fooled away the match against Western
Suburbs in an amazing manner, utterly neglecting to
take advantage of the high wind blowing when they had
it behind them. Western Suburbs utilised it for all it
was worth, and won by eight points to seven. But the
struggle of the day was between University and Balmain,
at University Oval. From start to finish play was un-
usually fast and sensational. Both sides made great
efforts, and the ball travelled about in a bewildering
manner. University had slightly more combination and
some little luck, and scored 17 to Balmain’s 12. The other
gay and alluring struggle was that between North Sydney
and St. George. In the first half the Saints scored eight
to nil, and then the other side got the wind and used it
with deadly effect. The game was three parts over when
North Sydney lifted the score one ahead of the Saints,
and the figures were nine to eight. Players and audience
went mad, and North Sydney men howled with ferocity
as they swept down the field to the attack, but they were
checked somewhat by one of them being ordered off the
field for violence. Before that wild Northern savagery St.
George crumpled up and was beaten by 12 points to eight.
* * * ye
Sydney Metropolitan Rugby competition has
now become an interesting gamble. South Sydney,
which had the bye cn Saturday last, is in front with 15
points, and Glebe holds second place with one point less.
Eastern Suburbs and University are together with 13
each, and Sydney and Western Suburbs have 12 and 11.
Below that are only single figures, and a single figure at
this stage is merely another name for oblivion.
* » » *
In the Association game the interest centred on
the struggle between Pyrmont and Balmain, the premier
and runner-up respectively last season. Both clubs had
received shocks to the nervous system the previous week,
when the premier club could play only a scoreless draw
against Rozelle, and Balmain was defeated by Petersham.
There was a widespread impression that Balmain’s
superior weight would wear out the premiers, but
nothing very dreadful happened. Pyrmont attacked vim-
f ully, and by half-time had scored two goals to the Bal-
mainers’ nil. Play became fierce, and illegalities were
the order.of the day till the referee threatened boiling oil
and eternal disqualification. Balmain attacked and
scored ; attacked again and was repulsed, followed up,
thrown down and trodden on, while the victorious Pyr-
mont scored again, and won with three goals to one.
With a head somewhat swelled over the playing
of a drawn game with the premier club, Rozelle
tackled the formidable Petersham crowd with a
jaunty expression. Petersham was one man short,
but in spite of that fact it put up so strenuous a
resistance to Rozelle’s opening attack that the head
trouble subsided rapidly, and the club settled down to
play all it knew. While Petersham was still a man short
Rozelle managed to get within striking distance and send
the ball into the net. Then the sides were equalised, and
the clubs so beautifully balanced that neither had any
hope of making any more points. The game ended in a
victory for Rozelle by one goal to nil. Glebe, of course,
ran over the Navy (the Navy is always run over), the
figures being five goals to nil. Ultimo had the bye.
Pyrmont is still leading in the competition with
15 points. Glebe and Ultimo are in the second line with
14 points each, and Balmain and Rozelle in the third with
10 each. The rest flourish single figures of various sizes.
m ♦ * *
In spite of a stiff wind and cold, showery
weather, Melbourne’s Saturday football provided some
pleasing episodes. Saints put a temporary stop to their
downward career by walking over Essendon —65 points to
37 —but as the ’Dons still divide with Eitzroy the honors
of bottoming the list of League clubs, the victory was
nothing to “ skite ” about. Geelong likewise ran over
’Roy—7l to 32 —and as the men from Corio Bay have suc-
cessfully beaten Essendon, Carlton and the Maroons,
they begin to think they know the game ; but they bump
against Saints next Saturday, and there may be a
different tale to tell. If they can win then, their hopes
of entering the final four will look rosy. Melbourne,
which, as last season, always threatens to do
something and never quite succeeds, put up a good
fight against Carlton, but spoilt what looked like a
winning- sprint in the last quarter by breaching the rales
in a regardless fashion. The umpire happened to be a
man who noticed that sort of thing, so Melbourne lost by
16 points : 9—16 to B—6. A similar misfortune happened
to South, in its argument with ’Wood. The threat of
rough play was visible all through, and only strict
umpiring prevented an unpleasant rough-and-tumble ,
but the umpiring was there all right. Entering
the last quarter South was 11 points in arrear,
but it had a fresh breeze behind it. ’Wood tried
kicking out of bounds, and was promptly penalised,
a goal resulting. Then ’Wood set out for the
strenuous finish that would break its opponents’ hearts.
By low skimming kicks that sent the ball crawling under
the wind its men worked the blown sphere into a good
position, whereupon South’s backs tried the brute-force,
rushing remedy, and free kicks against them were plenti-
ful, but only two behinds resulted. Open play—the3' are
bigger men than the Magpies—with high kicking might
have given them the two goals that meant victory, but
they persisted in forcing and rushing, and though they
snapped a behind, ’Wood replied with another just as the
bell rang, and departed winners by seven points—s 7 to 50.
* * * *
Of Association games that Obtween Ports and
Williamstown was the firmest discussion of the day, a fast
and strenuous argument, with each leading slightly
as the wind favored. It left Ports two points to the good
as the bell rang—34 to 32. Like St. Kilda, Essendon (A.)
has temporarily got into the mud, and sustained a notable
walloping from West, chiefly due to erratic kicking—the
board showing 11 goals six behinds to four goals 11
behinds. Richmond and Brunswick, both now under
the tutorship and having the assistance of ex-Collingwood
stars, bettered their positions at the expense of Prahran
and North respectively. The latter, having now sus-
tained nine successive defeats, will not be further men-
tioned here until it wins—or disbands. It is up to it to
do one thing or the other. Considering the high wind,
the occasional showers and the poor light, the shooting
throughout the afternoon was remarkably accurate, wit-
ness West’s 11 goals six behinds; Melbourne’s B—6;
Richniond’s 13—8. Preston scored six goals without
a behind —but as Footscray simultaneously notched 12
goals 14 behinds, Preston’s straight shooting was of no
practical advantage. During the week, sundry Associar
tion delegates got into wordy holts over the alleged
attempted abduction of two League players by anEssendon
(A.)delegate, said delegate being highly indignant because
he was said to have got the two to apply for permits while
they were unsober. He scorned the foul aspersion, etc.;
but it was significant that neither of the two ’bailers con-
cerned played for either League or Association on Satur-
day. Also, the Metropolitan Association considered the
questions of giving an umpire power to order a rough
player off the field, and of allowing substitutes for injured
players, but got no further than “ considering.”
* * * • *
Sydney footballers are somewhat exercised over
the news that Gillet, the Maoriland crack wing-forward,
is coming over with the team that is to play in Sydney.
Captain Judd has it on good authority that he has re-
ceived special instructions to worry the Australian backs.
Of course he has—what would he play wing-forward for
but to harass the backs? Why else is he placed on the
side of the scrum opposite to the referee? And Judd
inquires in a helpless tone of writing “how shall we
successfully cope with this harassing wing-forward?”
Maoriland wing-forward is usually a pointer of the deepest
dye, and it is an open question if he ought not to be
suppressed. How to cope with him is an easy matter.
There are several men in the Balmain and North Sydney
teams who can show the swiftest Maoriland wing-forward
points in illegality, and one of them might be picked
specially to look after Mr. Gillet.
■* * * *
The morning of June 10th found only five auto-
mobiles facing the starter of the Pekin to Paris Relia-
bility race, organised by the French paper Lk Matin.
The starter, The Bulletin understands, was the President
of the Pekin Automobile Association, who is a cove
authorised to wear a peacock’s feather in his hat and
yellow buttons on his tunic. The five starters comprised
two Dunlop-shod de Dions, an Italia, a Spyker, and, most
daring of all, aContal tri-car, ridden by a Frenchman
named Pons. The first day’s run was to Kalgan, 130
miles from the Chinese capital, and consisted of a
steady climb of some 5000 ft. The road was re-
ported as being indescribable in any European
language excepting that which gets the user
three months without the option. Caravans usually
take three days over it, but all the cars arrived there at
the end of the first day. The next town of any size was
the ancient Kuren, or, as it has been renamed, Urga,
800 miles away, across the Gobi Desert. The desert
should not have proved very formidable. It is level,
covered with a sort of spiky grass, and the mule teams of
some 5000 years have worn a well-defined track across it,
which is dotted over with small posts and villages.
Compared to what the Victoria Desert would have
been, it is a splendid road. Petrol stations had
been arranged every 60 miles, and, altogether, the
desert was far from being the most formidable por-
tion of the journey. The worst section should be
in the rugged mountainous country from Kiakhta, in
Trans-Baikal, to the lake from which the province takes
its name. Irkutsk and Yeniseisk provinces will both
provide much more severe obstacles than the sandy soil
of Gobi. At time of writing only the Italia car, driven
by Prince Scipione Borghese, had got through. That
descendant of a much disrespected line had arrived at
Irkutsk, and all the rest were still, apparently, in Gobi.
Pons’ tri-ear was known to have broken down, and was
being hauled out by natives and mules, while of the two
De Dions and the Spyker, there was no word. The record
from Pekin to Kiakhta is eight days, and was established
by the Chinese Imperial courier service with frequent
changes of men and horses, travelling night and day.
Borghese took 17 days to get over it.
The Automobile Grand Prix, France’s chief
motor event of the year, was responsible for one death
before the contest started. This year’s race was fixed for
the 48-mile triangle near Dieppe, and Albert Clement,
son of the French maker of that name, was thrown out
and crushed under his car while practising on the circuit.
His death caused the French Government to insist that
at the turnings there should be constructed dummy
roads, so that in the event of any competitor being un-
able to negotiate a turn he could run on to the dummy
track and pull up in safety. Although only 23 years of
age, Clement was a whizzer of renown and parts. He
just missed the Vanderbilt Cup in 1904 by 38 seconds in a
300-mile run, and finished third in last year’s Grand
Prix.
* ■* * »
The Petrol Consumption Test carried out by
the Automobile Club of Australia on the Long Bay-La
Perouse Road (Sydney) on Saturday supplied some very
interesting results. The 20 cars were carefully exam-
ined, and had their petrol tanks emptied ; and after
the club officials had poured half-a-gallon into
each tank, and sealed down the receptacle, the
cars were sent off to buzz up and down till
the petrol ran out. When the inevitable happened
the dried up car was to haul off the road and wait
patiently till the club officials came along and measured
the distance. This was not wildly cheerful, as a beastly
westerly wind was blowing, and went through everybody
in spite of wraps. The cars ranged from single cylinders
up to boomers with six cylinders, and the club had to
adopt a formula to make all these varying numbers of cylin-
ders and differences of horse-power work out dead level.
The longest distance was covered by Mr. Cusack’s single
cylinder 8-h.p. Rover, which travelled 23 miles 367yds.
on its half-gallon. All the same it didn’t win the gold
medal, which went to J. M. Arnott’s six cylinder 40-h.p.
Minerva, which travelled nine miles 1740yds. Arnott
and Cusack won the A and D class prizes respectively ;
B was won by L. W. Pye in a 12-14-h.p. Innes with a run
of 15 miles 7‘24yds. ; while class C went to I. Phizackerle.v
in a 10-12-h.p. Clemer.t-Talbot, the distance being 15 miles
1037yds. #
N.S.W. League of Wheelmen will fix theGoul-

* * * *
The Kangaroo’s tail is again exalted. A. J.
Clark, the Victorian cyclist who, in 1904, won the Aus-
tral wheel race, is making things hum in the U.S.A.,
where he is regarded as the fastest sprinter after Frank
Kramer and Ivor Lawson.
* * * *
The G.N. Hurdle Race at Flemington resulted
in another triumph for the bookmaker, who re-
turned to the betting paddock after the race, roaring
“I’ll pay the winner” in mockery of the disconsolate
army of backers. Though quoted in some of the news-
paper reports at 33 to 1, Bribery had been on offer at
40’s and 50's to 1. The Tasmanian gelding was one of the
three horses in the field that had started in last year’s
Hurdle Race. The fact that he then finished sixth to the
mighty Realm seemed to give him a good chance
this time but for the extra 161 b. on his back. Probably
the horses on Saturday were a bad lot. They had a hot-
headed pacemaker in Lion Heart, nevertheless the winner
took 5.53 for the journey—4sec. slower than last year.
Bribery (lOst. 21b.) won because none of the lightw eights
was of much account as it happened. Solano, the
runner-up, had 11.0, and he was followed by South Head
(10.1) and Superstition (11.2), so for the first time in
many years not one of the placed horses carried less than
10 stone.
The only lightweight that looked like winning
was Lion Heart (9.13). Here was a remarkable instance
of a horse’s persistence in spinning himself right out.
Ridden on his neck by a slim fellow, who had the, longest
of legs, and the shortest of stirrup leathers for a jockey
of his inches, Lion Heart bolted off with a lead from the
start. At one time he w'as, perhaps, 100 lengths ahead of
the crowd. When half the journey had been covered, he
was not less than 50 lengths to the good, but at 2 j miles
Lion Heart was the most beaten animal in the race. His
owner had expected him to stay it out; the trainer hoped
that he might just get home. The absurdity of Lion
Heart’s performance surprised even the touts, who had
predicted his collapse. As a fiery non-stayer, the ex-
Maorilander beat the record. Another striking disap-
pointment was Red Sea. The sturdy little son of Portsea
had to be spurred to keep him among the possibles, and
his final position, 7th, was as near the front as he had
ever been.
One of the best-handicapped and best-con-
ditioned chances for the G.N. Steeplechase on Saturday
is Decoration —last year’s winner. Decoration was only a
five-year-old when he beat Beilis and a lot more of them
by 20 lengths in record time. He will have 151 b. more
to carry this year, but the extra bit of age will discount
the disadvantage in weight, and if the course doesn’t
get sodden in the meantime both Decoration and Beilis
(13.1) are likely to be placed. Ofc the two top-weights
Decoration is preferred by the wise men who sometimes
prove worthy of respect. But even 12.3, which is
Decoration’s burden, comes very heavy on a jumper’s
back at the end of a three-mile struggle. The moderately
weighted ones have the chance of heavy-going on their
side. This scribe reckons that The Trick (9st.) is
the most dangerous opponent of the top weights. Some
of the others have no possible chance save the chance of
getting omitted from an extraordinary chapter of acci-
dents. •

The Y.R.C. has been in a forgiving mood of
late, and has raised the ban from many disqualified
trainers and jockeys. But the straw which is likely to
make the camel sag down in the middle happened along
last week, when Jack Wren made application for the
registration of certain racecourses of which he is alleged
to be the owner. So far, the V.R.O. has not been heard
from. Presumably, it is still speechless.

* * * *
Clarence Weber, who seems to have retired
from the wrestling circle, is reported as having taken to
weight-lifting competitions. It is probably almost as
lucrative and will certainly be a lot less dangerous. He
can always drop the 1501 b. bar with much more ease of
mind than he can drop Buttan Singh’s leg. The bar,
when it is let go, lies where it fell. When Buttan’s leg is
let go he turns on you.

The dog that won the Champion Cupatßenalla
(Vic.) Plurnpton coursing meeting last week, was named
Victor Daley. By the way, why drag the word “ Plump-
ton’’into the title of sundry Australian coursing clubs,
merely because of its ancient association with coursing
in England? It is just as absurdly out of place as the
name of Wren’s pony-course, Ascot.
BLACK FRIDAY IN MELBOURNE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The stricken city drooped its head,
And hid its solemn, thoughtful face,
As though in mourning for the dead,
Or sorrow for a dire disgrace.
I tapped a newsboy on the back
And asked him, “ Why this grief profound?
What makes the atmosphere so black?”
Quoth he—“ Bill’s beaten in one round.”
Great Squires is beaten ! He whose fame
Was as a light upon a hill ;
Whose blow, when struck with deadly aim,
Was guaranteed to maim or kill!
I muttered to the weeping youth,
“ You do surprise me ; you astound."
Again he sobbed the bitter truth—
“ Yus —beaten-in-one-bloomin’-round. ’■
A City Father, white with fear.
Stood at the corner of the street
And muttered, “ What is this I hear—
Has William suffered a defeat ?
I had a fitting speech to make
When home he came with laurels crowned.
I’m sorry for the country’s sake—
Poor fellow ! beaten in one round.”
A gal with grease-stains on her dress
Made brave pretence to blow her nose ;
The symptoms of her sore distress
She would not .willingly disclose.
But as she passed me, walking slow,
Methought I heard a moaning sound,
And then the words, “ Well, here’s a go!
Lord luv ’im ! Beaten in one round ! ”
On train and tram,-from noon tilj-ilight,
One subject only was discusst —
Folk talked of nothing save the'fight
That brought their Idol to the dust;
And ere they laid them down to sleep,
They knelt, as 'twere, beside a mound
Which marked the spot where, buried deep,
Lay William—“ Beaten in one round.”

is in the Sydney Town Hall. Such, however, is
not the case. The greatest organ is located in

burden. The climate of Australia, and the
tendency to over-eat and over-drink are the
principal causes of this great organ getting out of
harmony with the rest of the system. Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills reach the liver as no other
remedy does, opening the clogged ducts, causing
the bile to flow, and aiding in the digestion and
assimilation of food.*

This wonderful performance, it is cabled, was
$ accomplished on the Brooklands Track, England, by
S. F. Edge, affording further demonstration of the
Wonderful Wear=Resisting qualities of the world
famed DUNLOP MOTOR TYRE.
Mr. Edge’s almost incredible performance easily
ranks as the Finest Motor Record yet accomplished,
and equally the Finest Run yet established on
Pneumatic Tyres. CONSIDER what the Tyres had to
withstand in this all=day drive, and you can gather an

Melbourne billiardists were disappointed last
week in finding the popular local man Memmott utterly
ineaiiable of making a bold show against Inman, on what
had seemed fair handicap terms. From the very outset
of their game at Alcoek’s Hall, the visitor, conceding
‘’soo in 9000 to the local man, was able to do as he liked
S him Long before he had scored half his points
Inman was ahead of Memmott, and the rest of the game
had little interest for anybody save the leader, who was
nutting in good practice at Memmott’s expense. On this
showing, the disheartened Memmott would have no
chance of preserving his Commonwealth championship
title in his forthcoming match with young Lmdrum—but
billiards is often a game of surprises. A man recovers
“ form ”as inexplicably as he loses it; and Memmott,
who has much of the great player in his method, may get
back his lost “ touch ” and confidence under the trying
circs, of an attempt to prevent himself being snuffed
out by a mere boy. His best “form” is equal, or
superior, to Lindrum’s. It is never safe to assume that
an able-bodied artist has seen his best day.
As for Inman’s display, it raised the question
whether Stevenson can give him a start of 25 per cent, in
18 OOOup, though the length of the game will allow fall scope
for the scratch man’s ability to make some big breaks.
After dropping the idea of playing for a purse of £5OO
provided by Wren, the two Englishmen, who are keen
antagonists, agreed to meet on their own, and battle
under their respective managements—9ooo points on an
Alcock table in Melbourne, and 9000 on a Heiron and
Smith table in Sydney, the winner to get the bigger cut
at the gate-money. Stevenson gives a start of 4500 in
the 18,000, which is quite as much as his warmest bar-
racker would back him to do.
* * *
John Wren, having achieved another cheap ad.
through his rejected offer of a £5OO purse for a Stevenson
v. Inman billiard match in Melbourne, may be expected
to break out in a fresh place at any minute. Somebody
suggests that bowls provides a fine opening for Wren’s
gracious patronage. He might offer a £lOOO prize for a
bowls tournament without much risk of having to put up
the sugar.
* * * *
The Bulletin’s prediction, a week or two ago,
that the mingling of whitewashed ponies with horses would
sorely trouble the punting brotherhood, has since had
strong endorsement. Ten or more of the small nags
figured at Moorefield (Saturday), and two won races,
while others ran sufficiently forward to indicate that
they’ll have to be reckoned with when their riders get the
right instructions and act up to them—a provision that
makes all the difference in some cases.
* * - *
At the recent race between crack motor-
boats Invincible and Fairbanks, motor-boat men were
agreeably surprised to find that there is-quite a large
public interest taken in their sport. Although only
advertised on the morning of the race, the two boats
which “ followed ” the race each had a comfortable crowd
on board, and the Manly steamers that ran over the
course during the time of the contest were crowded by
people who had embarked specially to see it. Just now
the Invincible and Fairbanks have the floor to themselves,
but there are rumors that it will not be long so. Said
that at least two speed boats are under construction in
Sydney for the definite purpose of sweeping both the
present champion and its cedar rival into oblivion, and a
Melbourne enthusiast is, as usual, importing a Mercedes
engine of 100-h.p. for the same object. Another man is
threatening to bring out a 110-h.p. French engine to do
the job; but so far no one has said a word about building
a local engine to do it. If all these foreign notions do
arrive and lower the Invincible’s colors, it is to be sin-
cerely hoped that Mort’s Dock will take off its coat
and wade in to show what its splendid plant can really
turn out when put to it in deadl.vearnest.
Cabled that one of the English turf “toffs”—
Mr Leopold Rothschild—has been finding fault with the
starting-gate, and that the Jockey Club told him it
had come to stay. Presumably the average results of the
gate, as compared with the old system of starting a field
of horses, are accepted as an improvement by even a Im .v .
Rothschild. But it would be fairly open to him to com-
plain that the innovation leaves room for amendment. I
Under the “ barrier ” system a bad-tempered animal
seems more likely to get left at the post than if the field
were subject to the authoritative “ Come back !” of the
starter. A most absurd weakness in the improved
system is the rule which gives finality to the word
of the barrier. When it rises it says “Go ! ” and, though
the start, once in a way, may deprive one or more horses
of a reasonable chance to win, the man at the gate has no
authority to call them all back for another attempt to
get awa von fair terms. In short, a very bad start is now
a true start, whereas it was formerly cancelled as a false
one. And there's no ghost of a reason why this defect
in the system should not be remedied. As things are, it
is possible for the controller of the “gate” to show
favoritism whilst throwing all the responsibility upon the
dumb machine.
* * *
Goldsbrough, Mott & Co. report : A very quiet
tone has prevailed in the local wool market during the
past week, although we have been instrumental in placing
several large lines of skin wool at prices slightly below
those ruling last month. Buyers at present, however,
are not showing much inclination to do business,
evidently preferring to wait the opening of London sales
on the 9th inst., before committing themselves to any
great extent. Interest has been chiefly centred in the
stud sheep sales, and we have had the pleasure of wel-
coming our many friends who invariably visit Sydney at
this season of the year.*
Another ex-N.S.W. public officer, now
appointed to the Commonwealth service, has ar-
rived with some particulars showing how the Ma
State has suffered at the finger-nails of its raven-
ing sisters or daughters. In 1900, when the Curse
arrived, N.S. Wales had 4’38 persons per square
mile; at the end of last year it had 4’92 per
square mile, a gain of 54 per 100 miles. Victoria
in the same time has gained only 41 per 100
miles, Queensland but six, S. Australia but two,
and Westralia but nine; so that under Federa-
tion the poor, crushed and robbed Ma State’s
gain per square mile has been within a fraction
of as much as the gains of all the other mainland
States put together.
Nowadays the Stinking Fish seems too
often to be a New South Whale.
“ 0.K.,” from Melbourne :
Cole, of the Book Arcade—he of the good heart, but the
bad politics, who favors a tinted Australia, and should be
properly known as Brown Cole —has lately received a
severe shock to his confidence in Man’s honesty. A while
ago Cole published a sixpenny almanack of simple,mental
entertainment and instruction, whereof he wa,s so
proud that he promised to pay a shilling a copy for
any that were returned after a given interval. The
almanack was honestly worth its price to purchasers
who wanted it for reading purposes, therefore old King
Cole—confiding old soul !—reckoned that his only returns
at the end of the stated period would be expressions of
satisfaction from his customers. He was testing Human
Nature with every confidence in the success of his experi-
ment. Alas for Human Nature ! The almanack sold
freely at sixpence. The word went round in sundry
places that a sure profit of 100 per cent, could be made by
buying Cole’s latest publication and selling it back to him
in due course. The schemers didn’t read the bboklet.
They stocked it and waited for the rise. Within the past
few days the almanack returns have been rolling in and
the shillings have been rolling out. According to the
rules of the game, only one copy can be taken back from
a person who desires to exchange it for a “ bob,” so the
purchasers of large parcels are returning them as they
bought them, by instalments. For instance, a young
man named Brown, or Moses—more often Moses than
Brown —wi 1 drop into the Book Arcade with an almanack
at 11 a.m. An hour later he reappears, disguised in blue
spectacles, with another copy of the profitable work.
Anon he changes his hat and turns up again with yet
another shilling’s worth, and so on until his little stock
of almanacks is exhausted. Cole is the philanthropist
who gives new bobs for old tanners.
Reid’s political motto : Change here for
office.
N.S.W. Works Minister Lee lately had a
difference, or shindy, with a deputation about a
bridge. As a bribe, it was said that the bridge
would open up some State lands, and a basket of
fine strawberries, alleged to have been grown on
these lands, was trotted out. Then Lee (who was
also a trotter that day) trotted out a report by a
“ highly competent officer” of the \\orks Depart-
ment, stating that more barren land could hardly
be imagined than the State land in question, and
that, despite the strawberries, it was worthless for
tillage. That report may or may not be correct,
but the first thing that strikes one about it is that
when a Works official decides the quality of land it
looks very like a shoemaker getting beyond his last.
Also, there are some curious exam pies of apparently
barren but really fertile soils. 1 lie famous Cinna-
mon Gardens, near Colombo, in Ceylon, are
nearly pure quartz sand, as white as snow on the
surface. A few inches below the sand is grey.
The analysis given by an agricultural chemist
shows that the soil of those famous gardens
consists of 984 per cent, quartz sand, 1 per cent,
vegetable matter, and \ per cent, water. You
can’t well get anything in the way of soil that, on
paper, is poorer than that. Yet the Cinnamon
Gardens are famous. The inference is that, pro-
vided there is water, every place, even a gallows,
will grow something. It is only a question of
finding the right thing to grow. Another infer-
ence is that a Works official isn’t necessarily an
agricultural expert.

be able to supply the fullest particulars of AREA, SOIL,
CLIMATE, RAINFALL, FACILITIES TO MARKET,
NEARNESS TO PUBLIC SCHOOLS, TIMBER SUPPLY,
CREEK and RIVER FRONTAGES, and generally what
the lands are best suited for, etc., etc., and a PRINTED
REGISTER will be issued quarterly.
Specially in connection with the above, HARDIE
and GORMAN have secured the services of Mr. C. H.
GORMAN, who for years past was Manager of the
GOVERNMENT EXPERIMENTAL FARM at WOLLONG-
BAR, on the RICHMOND RIVER.
AGENCIES WILL BE FORMED IN ALL COUNTRY
DISTRICTS, and everything will be done in the most
comprehensive way to supply the wants of Purchasers,
and at. the same time give such detail information as may
justify inspection.

ments may be made to sell with other Auctioneers.

abject to Adjustment of Areas and Values.
Area —-5006 acres.
Situation —-In Gippsland, six miles
south of the Yarragon Railway
Station, 97 miles from Melbourne,
at the head of the Tarwin River.
District —Principally Grazing and Dairy-
ing, Fastening Cattle, Sheep and
Lambs.
Configuration —Undulating and hilly,
some cleared, some partly cleared.
Rainfall —Averages nearly 50 inches
yearly.
Water Supplies— Tarwin River,
numerous Creeks and Springs.
Soil —Grey loam, with darker and black
soil on tbe creek and river flats;
only small portions have been culti-
vated, and these are not in any way
exhausted.
Timber— Abundant supplies for fencing,
firewood, and building purposes on
every allotment.
Markets— Warragul, 14 miles ; Yarra-
gon, 6 miles ; and Melbourne, 97
miles.

property, the values of which are shown ■

After referring to the plan herewith, eligible
persons should take out and fill in the application
form included in this paper, sign it before a J.P.
or a Commissioner for taking Affidavits, and
forward it to “The Secretary, Lands Purchase
Board, Melbourne.”
NOTE.—When sending the application
enclose 3 per cent, of the actual value
of the land you apply for, also £1 for
preparation of Lease, and ss. Regis-
tration Fee. If not successful, these
moneys, less the Registration Fee, will
be returned. Send deposit on maximum
value.
If the Deposit is too small
or too large, it can be
afterwards rectified.
Presentation at any Victorian Railway Station
of the Board’s certificate that deposit has been
paid will entitle the applicant to a ticket, at holi-
day excursion fare, to visit the Estate and select
a block. The certificate will also entitle appli-
cant to a similar ticket to attend the Land Board.
The money sent to the Board by unsuccessful
applicants will be returned immediately. The
deposits, etc., of successful applicants will be
retained, and they will not be required to pay
anything further for six months.

ON LITTLE BOY
Mouth and Eyes Covered With
Crusts—Face Itched Most Fear-
fully—Hands Pinned Down to
Prevent Scratching.
MIRACULOUS CURE
BY CUTICURA REMEDIES
“ When my little boy was six months old, he
had eczema. The sores extended so quickly
oyer the whole body that we at once called in
Alfonso Hohrath.
the doctor. We then
went to another doctor,
but he could not help
him, and in our despair
we went to a third one.
Matters became so bad
that he had regular
holes in his cheeks,
large enough to put a
finger into. The food
had to be given with a
spoon, for his mouth
was covered with crusts
as thick as a finger, and
whenever he opened the
mouth they began to
bleed and suppurate, as
did also his eyes.
Hands, arms, chest, and
back, in short the whole
body, was covered over
and over. We had no
rest by day or night.
Whenever he was laid
in his bed, we had to
pin his hands down:
otherwise he would
scratch his face, and
make an open sore. I think his face must
have itched most fearfully.
“We finally thought nothing could help,
and I had made up my mind to send my wife
with the child to Europe, hoping that the sea
air might cure him, otherwise he was to be
put under good medical care there. But,
Lord be blessed, matters came differently,
and we soon saw a miracle. A friend of ours
spoke about Cuticura. We made a trial with
Cuticura Soap, Ointment, and Resolvent, and
within ten days or two weeks we noticed a
decided improvement. In ten weeks the child
was absolutely well, and his skin was smooth
and white as never before. F. Hohrath, Presi-
dent of the C. L. Hohrath Company,Manufact-
urers of Silk Ribbons, 4 to 20 Rink Alley,
South Bethlehem, Pa,, June 5, 1905.’’
The original of the above testimonial is on file in the
office of the Potter Drug & Chemical Corporation.
Reference: R. Towns & Co., Merchants, Sydney, N. S. W.
Cuticura Soap, Ointment, and Resolvent Pills are sold
throughout the world. Potter Drug & Chem. Corp., Sole
Props., Boston,!!. S.A.

Business, Robbery, etc.
The associated banks in Melbourne were undei
the impression, until the other week, that they had made
arrangements with the local police authorities whereby
five policemen would be rushed along in a cab to any bank
that telephoned an “ alarm ” between the hours of 6 p.m.
and 6 a.m. The mere mention of the place that sounded
an alarm was to signify “ Burglary on the premises,” and
bring a detachment of thief-catchers. The other night,
at 10.30 o’clock, the manager of the London Bank rang up
the police barracks in Russell-street. After about five
minutes ringing he got a reply, and when he stated
where he was ringing from a gruff authoritative voice
desired him to tell his business. As a test of police
alertness in the matter of bank robberies the “ ring-up ”
was a most instructive failure.
* * * *
Alleged now that most of the sago imported
into the Commonwealth is a compound of wheat and
maize, instead of being the pith of the sago palm ; so
acting-Customs Minister Chapman ordered analyses to be
made. “ If the allegation is proved the importers will
be required, under the Commerce Act, to apply to their
‘ sago ’ a true trade description.” That is all. Yet if a man
sold for 20s. a note of the defunct Oriental Bank in place
of one issued by a live, financial institution, an indignant
Judge would probably send him up for two years, instead
of Mr. Chapman simply requiring him to give a correct
description of his notes in future, Wonderful is the
difference between thieves.
• • » *
The English Church Times is moved to advo-
cate the abolition of the hot cross bun, owing to tha
sweating that seems inseparable from the production of
this flabby mass of indigestion. It has been discovered
! that Cold Country bakers are so rushed at the bun season
j that they often have to work for 30 hours continuously
for about 3d. an hour! One instance is given of bakers
toiling from Wednesday noon till Friday morning,, with-
out any rest at all, and then receiving the princely pay of
3s. 6d.—about Id. an hour. The “ chean loaf.” Heavens
yes—England ought to be proud of it!
* * *
“Agricola”: Of all sharks, the feminine shop-
ping shark is the most callous. The quibbler over full
measure, and the bargainer who beats down prices, are
varieties of this species. But the variety most dreaded
by the she-counter hand is the change' disputer. She
generally chooses a busy day, when there is no time to
argue the matter. “ That was half-a-crown I giv’ you,
Miss,” she says, when the worried saleswoman is too busy
| to remember that it was a florin, and it is no use showing
her the 2s. marked on the bottom of the purchase slip.
; “ I never seen what you put down. I on’y want me right
change ! ” and as her voice rises in a rapid crescendo, and
people begin to look round, the floorwalker ambles across
and arranges things to the tune of a deduction of six-
pence from the unfortunate she-hand’s screw on Friday
night. Some extra-bold sharks have been known to work
the swindle with half-a-sovereign, claiming that it was a
full-grown quid. In almost every case the firm comes
out quits, the shark wins, and the saleswoman loses.
However, hands in many shops have learned to press the
coin hard on the purchase slip, so that the impression is
printed on the duplicate by means of the carbon transfer
! and then the change disputer is bowled out first ball. ’
* * * *
Charity hath strange disguises. A Liverpool
(Eng.) woman, recently fined for unlicensed money-lend-
ing, pleaded that she wasn’t a professional, but lent
to working people “out of kindness, and to oblige'
neighbors.” A deeply-affected beak was just about to
dismiss the charitable one with Heaven’s blessing, when
it transpired that the “ kindness ” cost the unfortunate
borrowers just 866 per cent, per annum. This case, by
I the way, should int rest people like Parson Fitchett, who
are never tired of holding forth about the “freedom ” of
England because it has a comparatively short list of
Customs duties—only 47 of them. In Whitaker’s
Almanac there are six closely-printed pages of small type
setting forth the things you must not do in England
unless you pay a licence fee. They include being a
money-lender, a house-agent, a dealer in sweets, or the
owner of a carriage, and there are even ecclesiastical
licences—“ to hold the office of lecturer, etc., 10s.” And
a dog is 7s. 6d. So if Fitchett were in England he might
rank, for licensing purposes, as one dog and one-third of
a dog.
• * • «
“ Itan ” : You strike one of the other sort now
and again, but mostly the bagman has a loud-voiced
contempt for “these labor unions,” and their demands
for preference and things of that sort are “outrageous.”
Wflerefore it isn’t as generally known as it might be that
entrance to the Bagman’s Association is restricted to
males between the ages of 21 and 45. And to successfully
trade outside the ranks of this union is hopeless. In the
first place, the big warehouses will not employ outsiders •
and to become an insider isn’t as easy as telling lies'
But, once you get on the right side of the fence, you have
a 20 per cent, reduction in fares on coastal’ steamers
belonging to the inter-State shipping ring, 10 per cent,
on deep-sea liners, 20 per cent, at hotels, a substantial
concession from the Railway Department (which, for some
reason, is not announced), and a fund of assurance worth
a guinea an ounce. On the whole, there probably isn’t
one labor union in Australia so conservative ; not one so
exclusive; none so intolerant towards outsiders; and
scarcely one which has grabbed such advantages for its
members.

Last year the Melbourne Board of Works
made a profit of £08!)." out of the sheep alone which it
fattened on its sewage farm. The whole farm area is 8847
acres, of which less than half is in the Board’s occupa-
tion—the balance is either let, on lease or is in course' of
preparation.
STARCH SINGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(Suggested by recent inquiries.)
The world of Fashion fondles me ;
The dudes adore. Society
Could not exist without my aid.
I am that useful little maid
Who stiffens clothes, who shines the shirts.
Renews both cuffs and collars. Squirts
The magic paste each lady blesses
Over her blouses, skirts and dresses.
Without my help the Best Dressed Man
At once would look no better than
The baggy-trousered crew who write,
Or paint, or play—a shocking sight-
Without stiff skirts that hardly screen
Who would applaud the Ballerine?
Who would admire Fat’s broad chest arched
Unless its front were stiff and starched ?
I am that dainty cake of dirt
Each Fat Man blazons on his shirt;
While he who makes these magic blocks
Barely a shirt can sport, or socks.
I form the bridge that none can pass
'Twixt Toilers and the Upper Glass.
Without my gloss the world would be
Submerged in gross Democracy.
Protection is a wicked thing—
What legislation it would bring
To place an economic ban
On products not Australian !
Let Competition ever reign
Till workers are like serfs again
For all things unto Fat indebted ;
Let local toilers aye be sweated
So Duties high may not offend
Fat Europe at the other end ;
Let Fat and Foreign-trade arise ;
Let rampage Private Enterprise ;
And Anti-Sosh, potbellied, march
. Backwards, beneath its flag of starch.
VIC.
LOUIS ESSON.

“ 0 ” writes :
The retailer of groceries, or draperies, or fish, or flesh,
or bedsteads should not appear in working hours behind
his own counter between the date of his selection as a
candidate and polling-day. “ 1 was only lialf-an-hour
behind the counter this morning,” said one, “ when the
man who seconded the vote of confidence in me last night
came in, and carried away £3 worth on tick. He was
only out when a man who led the cheering cornered me
for 15s. worth.” “ I’d shut up till the election is over,”
said an ambitious politician; “only it would scatter my
trade, but I can’t stand this run. And, after all, I’m
not sure of their votes.” “If I get in,” wept another,
“ every penny of the £3OO will have been swamped before
polling day. “ Wait! ”he suddenly broke off, and dipped
under the counter. A red-faced, gruff-voiced man glared
round the shop, and, failing to find the object he sought,
he asked a “hand” where the boss was. “The boss?”
answered the mild young man. “He went home early,
and I don’t expect him back till to-morrow.” “Very
unfortunate, very unfortunate! ” cried the red-faced
man. “ I’ll come earlier to-morrow.” “ That’s the
way,” resumed the boss, bobbing up again from behind the
counter; “it goes on like that all the time. If that man
had caught me he would have carried away 30s. worth at
the very least, or he would have gone over to the other
side, and the fellow on the other side doesn’t keep a shop,
worse luck ! ”
Discovery of a Rational
Treatment for Deafness.
A glance through the medical literature of the
last 20 years reveals to the close observer a re-
markable diversity of opinion among aurists as
to what constitutes the proper method of treat-
ment for deafness and diseases of the ear. Some
authorities recommend in obstinate cases cutting
away the drumhead of the ear, other aural surgeons
are emphatic in saying that the results obtained
are too uncertain to justify such severe measures.
Another class of medical men base the treatment
of deafness almost entirely on the application of
medicated vapors forced through the nose into the
middle ear by way of the Eustachian tube, but it
is strange to find these sameauristsaclcnowledging,
in their writings on this subject, that it is doubt-
ful if medication applied in this way can reach
the seat of disease which causes defective hearing.
In the midst of all this doubt and uncertainty
there is fortunately the record of the discovery of
a rational method in the Electricon to successfully
apply Metallic Galvanic Electricity direct to the
Ears, permanently curing Deafness, and com-
pletely banishing all distressing Head Noises.
Like all innovations the Electricon received the
severest criticisms, but notwithstanding such
opposition, seven years’ test of the method has
given results so emphatically successful that the
usual medical opinions on the curability of deaf-
ness have been completely overthrown.
Those who are interested in the subject would
do well to read the complete description of this
method of treatment in the “Journal for the
Deaf,” published by the L. R. VERNON CO.,
60-64 Hunter Street, Sydney, and sent FREE to
to any one asking for it.

WHAT IS CATARRH ?
CATARRH is inflammation of the lining
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which iead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the sense of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks down
the affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
want relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.

ache, Neuralgia, Colds, Bronchitis, Asth-
ma. Write for booklet free, or send

The Eucrasy Co., Sydney.
Gentlemen, —I am sorry I have not written before to
state the result of the case I have been treating:, but I
have been too happy in the great blessing which has be-
fallen me. My was the patient to whom I gave the
powders, quite unknown to him, of course. At one time
it seemed hopeless, being in the bar all day, as he was, in
the very midst of temptation ; but I kept in the bar with
him myself as much as possible, and did not let him stay
in it any more than I could possibly help, and I am now
thankful to see him constantly turn away from the spirit
bottles with a shudder. I have about a dozen powders
left. I shall recommend Eucrasy to anyone I know
troubled like I was.—Believe me to be, yours sincerely
grateful, (Mrs.) M. J. S.
