“ Quidam ” : The English perch in Vic-
torian streams is rapidly destroying the Victorian
blackfish, and as the blackfish is generally reck-
oned a lot better fish than the imported perch,
fishermen are coming to the conclusion that
another imported article is a failure. A still
worse failure is the English carp, which voracious
but slushy fish is a nuisance to fishermen iii the
Turon and Macquarie Rivers, near Hill End
(N.S.W.), and is locally believed to be rapidly
displacing the Murray (or Macquarie) cod. By
the time Australia has wiped out all its native
fish in favor of imported ones, and completely
killed off the marsupials in favor of rabbits and
hares, and ruthlessly slaughtered all its valuable
native timbers, and seen its last lyre-bird eaten
by a slinking fox, it will probably begin to realise
that Nature gave it a dashed good outfit to start
with, and that, if it had only realised it about 20
years earlier, it might have turned the knowledge
to some good account. With the present rapidly-
disappearing fur-supply of the world, for instance,
a little animal which can produce such a valuable
crop on such cheap tucker as the Australian
’possum is worth paying attention to ; but it will
probably be several years after the last ’possum
has been slain, when furs are about 10 times their
present price, before Australia wakes up to the
I fact. Even now a man could start an elephant
farm nearly as easily as a ’possum farm. The
Tantanoola tiger appears to be the only animal
Australia can’t kill, and the presumption is that
it was imported.
“Hermes” writes:— *
The propensity of the opera singer to go on strike
is nothing compared to that of the professional cycling
crack. When Henri Mayer was here he lost his
head one night, and bursting his way through the rue,
sent one fellow spinning circles on the top of the track,
and the other waltzing across the grass. Disqualified for
that event, of course. Henri was in his dressing-room
when the news reached him. He rushed out with his
pump in one hand and his shirt in the other : “ Vare is
de McIntosh ? I vant him ke-vick.” Was he, Mayer,
not to start? Was he not the reputation, without him
would not the seats be empty ? Ten thousand devils—
big blue devils ! Yer well ! He not ride in the Sydney
Thousand, See where the McIntosh be then. Who the
McIntosh think the people come to see? The McIntosh?
Bah! Next day, accompanied by Ivor Lawson, who was
under a cloud at the time, he came along with a
doctor’s certificate. “Mr. de McIntosh, I am some-
thing sick!” he explained, ja'bbing himself cautiously
with his forefinger, in the neighbourhood of the
breastbone. “ Yes, I cannot race in Sydney Thousand ! ”
McIntosh looked at the certificate, which was from a
doctor he had never heard < f. “ All right, Mr. Mayer.
Sorry to hear it. But I can’t take this certificate. The
rule is that you must go before two doctors retained by.
the League, and if they certify you are ill, all right. If
they don’t, you either race or stand disqualified ; andas
this League is affiliated with the international body, that
disqualification will be enforced against you all over
Europe. It may not be anything serious, Mr. Mayer—
the liver, perhaps, or more likely the spleen. Oome in
this afternoon at 3 p.m., the doctors will be'here then.
Good morning ! ” Mayer’s eyes glittered. “ Ver well
Mr. McIntosh, I ride in Sydney Thousand—I come Aus-
tralia no more. I am stranger in strange land—you treat
me too considerately. I ’fraid if I come again I stay here
always—behind the wall ! ” And he nodded and strode
out.
“Nulla”: Talk about the casualness of
the Australian kid ! I saw one t’other day. He
was a two-year-old, and was accompanied by a
large, badly-frayed apple, and a ditto ditto dog.
He roamed about amongst the trams and ’buses,
until a desperate traffic policeman tucked him
under his arm and carted him to the lost kid
office. Did that kid howl for his mammy, or
promise never to do it again, or say that he pre-
ferred death to dishonor, or, in fact, say any of
the things a kid is supposed to say ? Not much.
He just made himself comfortable, and gnawed
his apple as though being tucked under the wet
macintosh of a huge “John ” wms the most com-
monplace proceeding imaginable. Also he
showed no concern about the whereabouts of his
dog; likewise the look of weird expectancy was
only noticeable when he proceeded to bury his
face in his apple.
“ F.C.” writes:—
Although the recent press condemnation of so-
called “ savage ” and “ vicious ” football has led Vic.
League and Association players into a less riotous frame
of mind, the very junior and country players still indulge
their habit of kicking the man instead of the ball. When
Seymour was playing Avenel the other Saturday, two
players came to blows, and the crowd rushed the ground.
The warriors were separated, but another argument
speedily arose, arid play was suspended while fights
raged in half-a-dozen parts of the field simul-
taneously. That diversion naturally left relations a
trifle strained, and when the game was continued
the players took to charging one another even as the
hulls of Bashan charged, and two Seymour men had to
be removed per ambulance. But for real “joy” one
looks to the “ church ” clubs around Melbourne. Same
Saturday Brighton Presbyterians proceeded to Ormond
to discuss matters with Ellendale, which, lacking a con-
stable, relied on .150 barraokers to k ep order. These
promptly set out to follow the umpire around the ground
in packs, speaking firmly to him the while, and
promising to “deal with” him every time he gave
a free kick to the Covenanters. Heaven knows the
result of the match, for before the finish the 150 beauties
wearied of talking and went for the umpire, who was
escorted into a furniture van, where the Scotehbyterians
dressed. Even then the hart-ackers were not to be
denied, and as they threatened t.o capsize van, horses and
players to make sure of getting the one lone umpire, the
driver was compelled to clear off with him to save his
life, leaving the players dressing in the road, to the
accompaniment of insulting- remarks, road metal, etc. It
is a vast pity these precious barrackers can’t be compelled
to play a game themselves, instead of smoking cigarettes,
spitting, and using choice language.
“W.P.B.”: I was talking to my fruit
John, the other day, and paid him a compliment
for having a good horse and keeping it in excellent
condition. Also asked what sort of a beast it was
morally. John shook liis head and looked sorrow-
ful for the vagaries of the animal that should have
been a companion rather than a delusion and a
snare. “No goo’,” he said, “ velly wi’. No
catchem someti’. Me catehem one day, velly
flash, muchee work catchem. He buckjump all
one si’, me losem alonga sclub, no fi’ long ti’. Give
small boy calloway Seed poun’ half help catchem.
Velly wi’, but goo’ work when qui’. I take him
longa Boulke one ti’. Maly Ann she won’t come,
me no like Maly Ann come—too much money
two. Maly Ann cly, so me solly. She come. I
take spling ca’-t,. get Boulke, go bed. Wake up,
no Maly Ann. She gone. Spling ca’t gone.
Hossee gone. All gone. Then I know why Maly
Ann cly. I catchee, beatee —know why she cly
tha ’ time !” And John chuckled happily. Then,
shaking his head, he looked solemn, and con-
tinued : “Maly Ann no goo’, steal flowty pong.
No fi’ long ti’ now. Plenty money flowty pong.
If me catchee Maly Ann cly some mo’.” He went
to the cart and fished out a length of clothes line.
Evidently he was ready to beat Maly Ann at any
moment if he met her on his rounds. I bade him
good day, and urged him to keep a bright look
out for Maly Ann, and he passed on with the
animal that was velly wi’ when it wasn’t qui’.
The latest suggestion from China is that the
“ extra-territorial ’’ rights of foreigners should be
abolished. The Asiatic peoples clearly intend to
rule in their own house. Since Japan secured
the abolition of extra-territorial rights no foreigner
gets a fair deal in a Japanese court, and that fact
is acting as a very good Aliens' Exclusion Act.

INVENTION
FOR THE

OF HAIR
CHE EVANS VACUUM CAP is a practical invention constructed on scientific and
hygienic principles, by the simple means of which a free and normal circulation

NATURE.
When a person suffers from
Rheumatism
Gout
Neuralgia
Lumbago
Sciatica
Blood Disorders
Ancemia
Indigestion
Biliousness
Jaundice
Gravel
Stone
Bladder Troubles
General Debility
Sick Headache
it is but Nature’s warning that the
KIDNEYS AND LIVER
are not efficiently doing their duty.
The Kidneys of the average person filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine
every day. In this quantity of urine should be dissolved about.an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains
in weight of uric acid, and other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounbe to nearly
an ounce. If the kidneys are working freely and healthily, all this solid matter ieaves the body dis-
solved in the urine, but, if through weakness or disease, the kidneys are unable to do their work
properly, a quantity of these urinary substances remains in the blood and flows through the veins con-
taminating the whole system. Then we suffer from some form of uric poisoning such as Rheu-
matism, Gout, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Persistent Headache, Neuralgia,
Gravel, Stone and Bladder Troubles. A simple test to make as to whether the kidneys are
healthy is to place some urine passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and let it stand
until next morning. If it is then cloudy, shows a sediment like brickdust, is of an unnatural color, Or
has particles floating about in it, the kidneys are weak or diseased, and steps must immediately be
taken to restore their vigor, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some of the many manifestations of uric
poisoning will result.
The Liver is an automatic chemical laboratory. In the liver various substances are actually
made from the blood. Two or three pounds of bile are thus made by the liver every day. The liver
takes sugar from the blood, converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again
supply it to the blood as the latter may require enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is
insoluble, into urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with the blood corpuscles
which have lived their life and are useful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we
suffer from some form of biliary poisoning such as Indigestion, Biliousness, Anaemia,
Jaundice, Sick Headache, General Debility, and Blood Disorders.
So intimate is the relation between the work done by the kidneys and that done by the liver, that
where there is any failure on the part of the kidneys, the liver becomes affected in sympathy and vice
versa. It was the realisation of the importance of this close union of the labor of those vital organs
which resulted in the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as
Warner’s Safe Cure.
Certain medical men, knowing what a boon it would be to humanity if some medicine could be found
which would act specifically on both the kidneys and liver, devoted themselves to an exhaustive search
for such a medium, and their devotion was eventually rewarded by their success in compounding'a
medicine which possesses the required quality in the fullest degree. Warner’s Safe Cure exhibits a
marvellous healing action in all cases of functional or chronic disease of the kidneys and liver, and
restoring them, as it is able to do, to health and activity, it, of necessity, cures all complaints due to
the retention in the system of urinary and biliary poisons. A vigorous action of the kidneys and liver
naturally eliminates the poisons, and troubles due to the presence of the poisons cease. Cures effected
by Warner’s Safe Cure are permanent simply because they are natural. [l2]
Science and Politics.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Sydney Morning Herald (27/6/’O7) stated that at
the meeting of the Linnean Society the President men-
tioned that a new educative body called the British
Science Guild had been launched in England; and said
that if the public could “ be trained, and the politician
induced to take an interest in natural science, it would
be well for the country.”
HERE’S a hopeful prospect dawning
For our local M.L.A.’s ;
They’re to tread the paths of knowledge,
And to walk in wisdom’s ways.
And our Legislators’ leisure
Will be profitably spent
In the patient cultivation
Of a scientific bent.
All the flora and. the fauna
Of our most prolific bush
Will be carefully examined
By the Legislative push.
They’ll inspect the birds, and make it
Quite apparent that the owl
For profundity of wisdom
Is an over-rated fowl.
Then the fish will be exploited,
And some M.L.A. will feel
Quite a brotherly sensation
When confronted by the eel.
In the numerous recesses
And adjournments of the House,
They’ll investigate creation
From the mammoth to the mouse.
They will chevy every lizard,
Every creeping thing they see,
From the pious praying mantis
To the wicked little flea.
And the eye of fancy shows us
Quite a corpulent M.P.,
Wiio is frantically chasing
A “ goanna ” up a tree.
By some fickle freak of fortune—
Some peculiar turn or twist—
Mr. Crick may earn distinction
As an entomologist.
On the breezy heights of Randwick
He will probably be met,
Chasing butterflies and beetles
With a patent folding net.
How he’ll love these healthful races !
Where he needn’t waste his tin,
Backing stiff uns for the places
They can never hope to win.
Still, in such Arcadian gambles,
If he really wants to bet,
He can back the blooming insect,
Or the sanguinary net.
Yes, the prospect’s very pleasing,
But, en passant, let’s suppose
Some enthusiastic Member
Carries serpents in his clothes !
In the absent-ibinded manner
Which some'scientists affect,
He may take ’em in his pocket
To the Halls of the Elect.
Just imagine what a scatter
There will be to reach the door,
If a snake gets loose and wriggles
On the Legislative floor !
And a horrid thought arises—
How the deuce will some M’s.P.
Tell the substance from the shadow
If a snake they chance to see ?
Still the scheme is worth a trial,
And we candidly confess
That the movement has our wishes
. For its ultimate success.
And we think a blend of science
With our politics might throw
Quite a glamor o’er the future
Of Australia, doncherknow.
N.S.W.
IRONBARK.
The Trials of Tom.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I FIRST met the next-door neighbor’s
tomcat when he, drove our own feline back
into our lines with great slaughter. He
appeared then as a rapidly-whirling ring of blade
encircling a less mobile ball of grey. The grey
was our cat, and his language was a deep purple.
The whole thing made quite an artistic color-
scheme, and I hated to destroy it. But it had to
be. I hurled the brick, and it hit the grey bull’s-
eye, an excellent shot from a marksman’s point
of view, but unsatisfactory from the standpoint
of our cat. The black “outer” disengaged itself
in great haste and clove the atmosphere in a bee-
line for the top of the fence. Our discredited
general limped into the kitchen with his left ear
in festoons, and a “ regret to report ” expression.
And hereafter our yard was added to the
dominions of the next-door Alexander, our cat
being busily engaged in searching for mice under
the kitchen dresser whenever the victor chose to
triumph in his newly-acquired territory.
I grew quite friendly with the black war-
rior— I was always a hero-worshipper. I
named him Tom, and discussed the ques-
tions of the day with him. He was an intel-
ligent beast, much more intelligent than the
average suburban alderman. When I mentioned
George Reid he would wink slowly—first with one
eye and then with the other. If I said “Cook,”
his tail would bristle up ; while the name of Car-
ruthers was oddly connected in his mind with
flies. I can’t see the connection myself, but hope
to work it out some day. Never believe what
people say about cats being mere bellies on legs.
Because they do not fawn upon their owners like
dogs, they are charged with lack of intellect. To
me, the dog seems the fool.
But that is another story, as Shakspeare says.
This cat lived with some rather faddy people.
They had a baby, and, will it be believed, they
would not let the poor cat sleep on said baby’s
face ! They did not mind the infant pulling the
cat’s tail, but they had no sense of justice, and
when Tom, entering into the gambols of the child,
made pretty, red scratches on his hands, he was
cruelly beaten, and Carlo, a base, time-serving
retriever and a fatted slave, chased him into our
yard. Man’s inhumanity to man is all very well,
but what about woman’s inhumanity to eat ? One
would think a community of nature and intefe,is
would lead to harmony.
When first I knew Tom he was every inch a
cat. lie could handle any other tomcat in the
parish, and it had to be a big dog, and a woolly
one—for his claws caught in long wool—who could
strike terror to his soul. But when the aforesaid
baby had reached the age of two years, and Tom,
in consequence, made our yard his real head-
quarters, he was attacked one day by my terrier,
and fled for safety. Formerly our dog had been
afraid of him, but now—plainly Tom was failing.
Day by day his warlike spirit and high intelli-
gence waned, until at last our old cowardly cat
beat him in fair combat. I was vastly puzzled, for
he was notan old cat, his black fur showed no
grey, and his teeth were good. I matched him,
and encouraged him again against our own cat, but
he was more decisively beaten than before.
Then one day my aunt, reading an article in a
faith-healing paper, looked up over her spectacles
and informed me that this faith-healing and health-
food and vegetarian business was gaining ground,
and that there must consequently be something
in it. “And, James,” she continued, “the
Featherstonhaughs ye gods !—“ next door
have turned vegetarian. Their cook left last
month because of it, and they haven’t been able to
get another.”
I saw it all. The fiendish cruelty of their con-
duct ! Did they consider, in their selfish care of
their own dyspeptic Little Marys, the poor cat
which they had brought into the w I mean —
er—nurtured tenderly from youth ? Imagine the
agony of Tom when he was offered, day in, day
out, pure gluten and granose, and lentils, and
butter made out of nuts. My sympathy was
aroused. I bought a beef-steak, and called Tom
over the fence. He fainted when he saw the steak.
I waited until he recovered. When he had done
so, he approached the meat cautiously, and, while
he was considering whether it was meat or that
villainous imitation, which the phytophagi call
protose, my terrier, who knew meat when he saw
it, snapped up the steak and bolted with it.
There is little more to aid —except that my aunt,
complaining of the quality of our drinking water,
sent me up to the roof to inspect our tanks a
month or so after this incident. With a long, long
pole I removed the waterlogged carcase of Tom
from our little reservoir, and dropped it down the
next door neighbor’s kitchen chimney.
HOWARD ASHTON.
A Long Way to Cork.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
were spelling in the shade of a
bush fence, or pile of cut scrub, or some-
thing, and Pat O’Brien had a place that
ought’ve bin covered by a patch ; and it was in
the sun with Pat’s outlying regions. And Dave
Regan had a burning glass. Whispered Dave to
his mate lazily :
“I’ll pop the glass onter Pat, Joe, an’ when he
jumps, you jump too, an’ yell ‘ Snake ! ’ ”
“ Uh-um,” murmured Joe, and he reached care-
lessly for a new axe-handle, which he fingered
abstractedly.
Joe rolled over very lazily, on to his elbow, and
applied the glass.
In a little while Pat got up like a nervous horse
that had thought it was miles away from man
and alone, till suddenly yelled at. And his
language was bad about bull-dog ants. But, at
the same time almost, Joe jumped up, yelled
“ Snake !” and started to slash the bushes with
the axe-handle.
“ Beggob, boys,” said Pat, “ I’m bit! ”
They were all up now.
“ I’m bit, boys—an’ where ye can’t tie it ? ”
Joe and Dave took him, one on each side, and
started to run him along the track ; but they
hadn’t gone far when, at the hurried suggestion
of one of the others, and clamorous approval of
the rest (there were four others), they threw Pat
on his flat and sat on him while Dave cut the
place with his pocket knife and squeezed out as
much blood as he could.
Then they ran him on again, only stopping
once to take more of his blood, till they got to the
huts.
“Doan’t cut me roomp off, boys,” he said
pathetically, the second time. “For I’d as lief
die as be widdout it.”
They ran him on.
The storekeeper was absent after his horse, so
they walked Pat up and down while the super
opened the store with the wood axe, and handed
out two bottles of brandy.
They gave Pat a long pull, and gave a nip to
two fresh men who relieved the pacers, and
walked Pat up and down with a spurt, while the
rest had a nip to brace their nerves.
“ It’s a long way to Cork, boys,” said Pat, “ it’s
a long way to Cork.”
They gave him another pull, and walked him
up and down.
“I can feel it goin’ through me like fire, boys,”
he said ; “I can feel it goin’ through me like fire.
Can’t ye tie me up somehow ? Take a twist on a
bit of fincin’ wire or something.”
One of them picked up a piece of fencing wire,
but dropped it hopelessly.
They gave him another pull and walked him
up and down. And every time they walked him
up and down the others had nips to keep up their
spirits.
“I’m drowsin’ down, boys,” he said wearily ;
“I’m drowsin’ down. Ah 1 boys, it’s a pity to
lose such a man.”
They roused him up and walked him up and
down before giving him another pull, but they
had nips themselves to keep up to it.
“Ah! boys!” he said. It’s a long way to
Cork.”
“ So it seems,” said the super, but he got out a
couple more bottles to be ready. He had some
himself. They gave Pat another pull, and walked
him up and down. The relief had pulls before
they went in, and the relieved had pulls when they
fell out.
And they walked him up and down.
They started a man off on horseback to Stiff-
ner’s, on the main road, to see if there was a
doctor or snake-bite expert there, and to bring
back more spirits in case they ran short. The
super gave him two quid, but he never came
back.
And they walked Pat up and down, and did
exactly as before till they couldn’t wake him, nor
the super—nor themselves, till next day.
Pat woke first, and thought, and remembered.
Then he roused Dave, and, staggering, walked
him, up and down.
“Dave,” he said (in conclusion), “Dave, my
friend. Ye saved my life wid y’ere pocket-knife.
Here’s a couple o’ quid for yer sweetheart, me
boy. An’ there’s wan of the same again whinever
and any time ye ask for it.”
“Don’t mention it, Pat,” said Dave. “It was
nothing. I’d do the same to yer any day.”
“ I know ye would, me boy,” said Pat, and, the
super being still unconscious, they lay down again,
well within the home gums shade, and slept like
brothers.
HENRY LAWSON.

The Sinners.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BILLY’S clothes show creases yet,
Billy’s face is pale ;
Half an hour ago they let
Billy out of gaol.
Got the chance he’s waited long,
Going to see it through ;
Strolling, hopeful, happy, strong,
Back to Waterloo.
Making for his sister’s place,
All his thoughts of her ;
Thinking of her kiddy face,
When he went to “stir”;
Maggie, who was good as he
Knew that he wascrook;
How she cried the night that she
Heard that lie was took.
Maggie’s changed since Billy’s been
Doing bits of time;
Maggie's frocks are like the sheen
Of a pantomime.
Through the streets does Maggie roam
Nightly, up and down;
Maggie’s left her work and home—
Maggie’s on the town.
Maggie’s battling every night,
Up in Castlereagh ;
Maggie’s always three-parts tight,
In the light of day.
Quick for action if they stare,
Maggie quickly turns;
And a “ boy ” has Maggie there,
Catching what she earns.
Billy’s eyes are shot with blood,
Billy’s jaw is set;
When they sight him through the mud,
Billy’s cobbers “ get ”;
Scaring all the push he knew
With a loaded squirt;
Looking for the fellow who
Done his sister dirt.
Maggie laughs, for Maggie’s game;
! Maggie will not tell;
When he threatens for the name,
Bids him go to hell.
Billy sees her hardened cheeks
Flaming like a rose,
Sniffs the perfume cheap that reeks;
Billy cries—and goes.
Billy’s courage all has dropped ;
Billy’s down and out;
Billy’s soul it was that stopped
His almighty clout.
Billy’s at his game anew.
In the shadows dim—
Now that she’s a sinner too,
Just the same as him.
N.s.w.
MILTON MACGREGOR.
The Irishman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ f INHERE is no one who can make love
like an Irishman.”
The girl wore a black pongee even-
ing dress, with a hint of cream like the petal of a
magnolia blossom, and a touch of pink like the
heart of a blush rose about her shoulders. Her
slim waist was encircled by a wide belt of the kind
called Empire, and when she leaned forward,
clasping her soft hands around her knee, one
caught the shimmer of the silk against the long
graceful lines of her limbs.
“No?” I answered, inquiringly. I doubted
her —because I had once known a Scotchman.
She looked dreamily out into the dusk of the
garden, where a feather palm waved in the
drowsy breeze, for it was a hot night in a tropical
city.
“Tell me about the Irishman,” I said, dragging
the cushion of my chair into that weak spot in
my back, and thinking that middle age had its
compensations after all, since I was always given
the most comfortable chair.
“You would not understand, ” she said. She for-
got that I had once been young, and had worn, per-
haps, black pongee with a wide belt and a thing
that sparkled in my hair. Young people are so
very decided.
“ Try me,” I said, and I moved my chair nearer
to her. I could see the lids narrowing her blue
eyes, in the stream of orange light that came
through an open door looking on to the verandah.
She was staring hard at me, summing me up as a
possible confidant. She was no fool, this girl—in
spite of the Irishman.
“ I remember the day I spent with him —all
alone,” she said. “ I had no business to go, of
course, but then I like doing things I ought not to
do. He got the tickets, and he was waiting on
the platform when I got there. It was an early
train. I remember that I didn’t wait for break-
fast. There was a truck-load of pigs—pigs of all
things ! The reek of them was all about the plat-
forms, and I could hear them squealing as I came
down the hill, and it made me think for a
minute that the circus had come back. We
had to wait till they moved on, before our
train came in. He hurried me into a small first-
class carriage. He always did things in a hurry.
1 hadn’t known him so very long after all, when
lie first—but perhaps you will understand, if you
have ever known an Irishman like my Irishman,
that I didn’t mind as much as I should have then.
I was glad—yes, glad—when he kissed me again.
He had such merry eyes, and his hair curled all
over his head —no matter how short he kept it
cropped you could always trace the little bronze
ripples —and he was so straight and tall and
young. Why, even you—but never mind ! I am
not asking your opinion. I am reckless to-night,
as I was that day ; the black coffee I had after
dinner has gone to my head, I suppose. I must
talk to somebody !
“Have you ever travelled alone m a railway
carriage with the man you loved ? If so, you will
al ways like the smell of the shiny leather cushions,
uid the varnish, and the coal dust, and every-
thing. There is such a delightful sense of freedom
—such a running away from trouble—such a leav-
ing of the old leering, babbling world behind you.
I lave you ever let him point out to you—if he has
the seeing eye—the little things worth noting along
ili,; line—the wattle, like living, growing sunshine
in the swamp paddocks; a bank of rough, red soil
crowned by wisps of coarse faded grass; a black,
freshly-ploughed field adjoining a perfect square
of bright green barley ; the polished curves of a
branch line running between the dull olive of
hillside scrubs ; a sandy road walled in by ti-trees,
and across it the dark shadow of a towering gum,
and, showing between the slender bleached
trunks of the ti-ti’ee, the red flank of a rumina-
tive cow ?
“ He holds your hands and he laughs into your
eyes. He says since the world began there never
was a woman like you. He doesn’t go in for the
down-on-your-knees attitude at all. He wants
you close to him, not on a chilly pedestal to be
worshipped afar off. He is as light-hearted as
the breeze, and as full of vitality as the sunshine.
He calls you all sorts of unexpected pet names,
and they don’t come trippingly off his tongue,
either. He makes a joke of love, even while he
offers it, but his eyes are so fond you forget that
to-morrow he may see that this is a world full of
women. He says you have no faults—your weak
points are the ones he picks out to praise ”- —•
“That’s not like a Scotchman,” I murmured,
wondering at the girl. I had never heard her
talk like this before. I supposed, as she said,
that it was the black coffee. I was glad in a way,
too, because I liked the girl, and 1 knew that it
meant she trusted me.
She did not notice my irreverent interruption.
She was looking away, with her deep blue eyes,
at the feather palm and the darkness. She went
back to her story.
“ When we reached the seaside place we had
fixed upon for our day’s outing we saw the little
town standing, as though surrounded by water,
against the blue of the high tide. The stiff breeze
had beaten the lift of the waves into white froth,
and a red-funnelled dredge was rocking and lung-
ing heavily away out in the bay. He found a
hidden beach where a great fig tree climbed the
rocks and clung to the crumbling cliffs with its
tough-twisted roots, and where a gravel of shells,
golden and white, stretched away under our feet
to where the sand was wet and hard.
“We lunched on—l forget what—something
out of a basket, and we laughed all the time.
That was the best of him. He was never too
serious, though he could be, ah !so tender. I had
a soft white silk blouse on, with a collar of pale
blue turned back sailor-wise at the throat, and
just before we left to catch our train home he put
his big brown hand under my chin, and, lifting my
face, kissed my throat—just there —and said it
was a seal—sealing me to him—for ever—and
then—the stars came out and we —we came
home.”
She was silent. I could not see her eyes as she
leaned back in her chair, but I saw the white
hands shiver in her lap.
A strident gramophone began to play next door.
“You never heard what became of him?” I
said. Somehow I guessed that they had not
stolen another day.
“Yes,” she answered, and she was her keen,
cynical self again. “I heard of him last week.
He has been married eighteen months. A man
who stays here has seen him out west. He and
his wife and his—his thumping baby—yes ! that
was the expression—thumping baby ! ”
I looked at her in silence, tracing the long lines
of her graceful, girlish figuie in the darkness, and
seeing the glint of her high-heeled, patent
leather shoes with the silver buckles, where the
lamplight caught them.
“ Thumping baby ! ” I repeated, stupidly.
“That takes the romance out of the picture,
doesn’t it”? she said, hardly. “They fed it on
a patent food, the man said. The Irishman mixed
it himself. Fancy a baby that thumps.”
She settled herself comfortably in her chair.
“ Your turn now,” she said. “Did you have
coffee to-night ?”
The breeze had freshened, and I moved back
my chair and got up to fetch a wrap. I am old
enough to be careful about chills.
She stretched out a white, detaining hand.
“Don’t go,” she said, imperiously. “I want
to hear all about your Scotchman ” !
But I laughed, shaking off her ringless hand.
“ There is nothing to tell about a Scotchman—
there never is,” I answered.
M. FORREST.
Nest Eggs.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THINGS had gone wrong with the
Joneses. Mrs. Jones had just returned
from town, and stood before her dressing-
table, on which she emptied the contents of a
small velvet bag. Two or three half-crowns and
several shillings she picked out carefully, and
placed in a neat little pile. The rest —two
diamond rings, a diamond necklet, and pendant
—she left in a glittering mass where she had
emptied them.
Jones entered with his finger on an advertise-
ment.
“Look here, Emma, here’s something that’d
suit us—Poultry farm, going concern. We’d
have to raise a little more to doit with, though.”
“We could sell something,” suggested Mrs.
Jones.
“That’s what we’ll have to do,” acquiesced
Jones. At that moment his eye fell on the
jewels. “ Emma,” he said feelingly, “come what
will, I’ll never ask you to part with your
diamonds, my girl. Not for the common fund,
anyhow. I gave you those before we were
married, and you must never part with them—
unless—perhaps, when lam dead and gone. But
I hope there’ll be no necessity for it even then.”
All the time Mrs. Jones stood gazing at her
husband as if she had never seen him before—in
fact, as if she had never before come across the
species even. “ There’s the piano, and that silver
teapot, and things of your mother’s,” he added.
“We ought to raise something on them. And
you won’t have much use for the phseton
either, Emma.”
* * * *
The Joneses were installed at the poultry farm.
Jones had taken off his coat, and was endeavoring
to look familiar with things, and Mrs. Jones had
nearly finished unpacking, and sat on the bed
sorting out a hamper of miscellanies.
“I think, Emma,” said Jones, coming in,
“ that everything is very complete. We might
want a few china nest-eggs.”
“We don’t want any more glass nest-eggs,”
interrupted Mrs. Jones, acidly.
“Not glass, my dear, china—to look like the
real thing, you know.”
“We don’t want them,” snapped Mrs. Jones.
Adversity had not chastened her temper.
“ I had no idea you knew anything about those
things,” said Jones, apologetically,
“I know everything'there is to know about
glass nest-eggs,” said Mrs. Jones ; and she emptied
a bundle of miscellany on the floor, with a bang,
and there was a diamond pendant among the
things, too.
